test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_059.jpg	Institute of Integral Studies. Founded in 1968, the institute specializes in “transpersonal psychology,” a school of therapy with a strong
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_060.jpg	spiritual orientation rooted in the work of Carl Jung and Abraham Maslow as well as the “wisdom traditions” of the East and the West,
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_061.jpg	including Native American healing and South American shamanism. Stanislav Grof, a pioneer of both transpersonal and psychedelic
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_062.jpg	therapy, has been on the faculty for many years.
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_063.jpg	In 2016, the institute began offering the nation’s first certificate program in psychedelic therapy. As part of his degree program, Andrei had
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_064.jpg	to undergo psychotherapy and found his way to a Native American “doing medicine work” in the Four Corners as well as the Bay Area.
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_065.jpg	“Whoopee!” he recalled thinking. “Because of my LSD experience, I knew it was viable.” Medicine work became his vocation. “I help
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_066.jpg	people find out who they are so they can live their lives fully. I used to work with whoever came to me, but some were too fucked up.
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_067.jpg	If you’re on the edge of psychosis, this work can push you over. You need a strong ego in order to let go of it and then be able to spring
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_068.jpg	back to your boundaries.” He mentioned he’d once been sued by a troubled client who blamed him for a subsequent breakdown. “So I
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_069.jpg	decided, I don’t work with crazies anymore. And as soon as I made this statement to the universe, they stopped coming.” These days he
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_070.jpg	works with a lot of young people in the tech world. “I’m the dangerous virus of Silicon Valley.
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_071.jpg	They come to me wondering, ‘What am I doing here, chasing the golden carrot in the golden cage?’ Many of them go on to do something
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_072.jpg	more meaningful with their lives. [The experience] opens them up to the spiritual reality.”It’s hard to say exactly what put me off working
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_073.jpg	with Andrei, but oddly enough it was less the New Agey spiritualism than his nonchalance about a process I still found exotic and scary. “I
test/images/synth_doc_00125_line_074.jpg	don’t play the psychotherapy game,” he told me, as blasé as a guy behind a deli counter wrapping and slicing a sandwich. “None of that
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_000.jpg	Researchers can plug the output directly into common OCR training loops. Although the rendering is intentionally clean, the annotation
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_001.jpg	format is designed to extend naturally to noisier pipelines.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_002.jpg	Adding backgrounds, blur, or geometric distortions only requires post-processing the rendered image. The sentence-level bounding boxes
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_003.jpg	remain valid under any pixel-space transform that preserves axes, and they can be warped alongside the image for more aggressive
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_004.jpg	augmentations. PROLOGUE A New Door MIDWAY THROUGH the twentieth century, two unusual new molecules, organic compounds
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_005.jpg	with a striking family resemblance, exploded upon the West. In time, they would change the course of social, political, and cultural history,
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_006.jpg	as well as the personal histories of the millions of people who would eventually introduce them to their brains. As it happened, the arrival
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_007.jpg	of these disruptive chemistries coincided with another world historical explosion—that of the atomic bomb.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_008.jpg	There were people who compared the two events and made much of the cosmic synchronicity. Extraordinary new energies had been loosed
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_009.jpg	upon the world; things would never be quite the same. The first of these molecules was an accidental invention of science. Lysergic acid
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_010.jpg	diethylamide, commonly known as LSD, was first synthesized by Albert Hofmann in 1938, shortly before physicists split an atom of
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_011.jpg	uranium for the first time. Hofmann, who worked for the Swiss pharmaceutical firm Sandoz, had been looking for a drug to stimulate
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_012.jpg	circulation, not a psychoactive compound.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_013.jpg	It wasn’t until five years later when he accidentally ingested a minuscule quantity of the new chemical that he realized he had created
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_014.jpg	something powerful, at once terrifying and wondrous. The second molecule had been around for thousands of years, though no one in the
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_015.jpg	developed world was aware of it.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_016.jpg	Produced not by a chemist but by an inconspicuous little brown mushroom, this molecule, which would come to be known as psilocybin,
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_017.jpg	had been used by the indigenous peoples of Mexico and Central America for hundreds of years as a sacrament. Called teonanácatl by the
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_018.jpg	Aztecs, or “flesh of the gods,” the mushroom was brutally suppressed by the Roman Catholic Church after the Spanish conquest and
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_019.jpg	driven underground. In 1955, twelve years afterAlbert Hofmann’s discovery of LSD, a Manhattan banker and amateur mycologist named
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_021.jpg	Gordon Wasson sampled the magic mushroom in the town of Huautla de Jiménez in the southern Mexican state of Oaxaca. Two years
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_022.jpg	later, he published a fifteen-page account of the “mushrooms that cause strange visions” in Life magazine, marking the moment when news
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_023.jpg	of a new form of consciousness first reached the general public.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_024.jpg	(In 1957, knowledge of LSD was mostly confined to the community of researchers and mental health professionals. People would not
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_025.jpg	realize the magnitude of what had happened for several more years, but history in the West had shifted. The impact of these two molecules
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_026.jpg	is hard to overestimate.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_027.jpg	The advent of LSD can be linked to the revolution in brain science that begins in the 1950s, when scientists discovered the role of
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_028.jpg	neurotransmitters in the brain. That quantities of LSD measured in micrograms could produce symptoms resembling psychosis inspired
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_029.jpg	brain scientists to search for the neurochemical basis of mental disorders previously believed to be psychological in origin. At the same
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_030.jpg	time, psychedelics found their way into psychotherapy, where they were used to treat a variety of disorders, including alcoholism, anxiety,
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_031.jpg	and depression.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_032.jpg	For most of the 1950s and early 1960s, many in the psychiatric establishment regarded LSD and psilocybin as miracle drugs. The arrival of
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_033.jpg	these two compounds is also linked to the rise of the counterculture during the 1960s and, perhaps especially, to its particular tone and
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_034.jpg	style. For the first time in history, the young had a rite of passage all their own: the “acid trip.” Instead of folding the young into the adult
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_035.jpg	world, as rites of passage have always done, this one landed them in a country of the mind few adults had any idea even existed. The effect
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_036.jpg	on society was, to put it mildly, disruptive. Yet by the end of the 1960s, the social and political shock waves unleashed by these molecules
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_037.jpg	seemed to dissipate.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_038.jpg	The dark side of psychedelics began to receive tremendous amounts of publicity—bad trips, psychotic breaks, flashbacks, suicides—and
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_039.jpg	beginning in 1965 the exuberance surrounding these new drugs gave way to moral panic. As quickly as the culture and the scientific
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_040.jpg	establishment had embraced psychedelics, they now turned sharply against them. By the end of the decade, psychedelic drugs—which had
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_041.jpg	been legal in most places—wereoutlawed and forced underground.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_042.jpg	At least one of the twentieth century’s two bombs appeared to have been defused. Then something unexpected and telling happened.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_043.jpg	Beginning in the 1990s, well out of view of most of us, a small group of scientists, psychotherapists, and so-called psychonauts, believing
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_044.jpg	that something precious had been lost from both science and culture, resolved to recover it. Today, after several decades of suppression
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_045.jpg	and neglect, psychedelics are having a renaissance.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_046.jpg	A new generation of scientists, many of them inspired by their own personal experience of the compounds, are testing their potential to
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_047.jpg	heal mental illnesses such as depression, anxiety, trauma, and addiction. Other scientists are using psychedelics in conjunction with new
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_048.jpg	brain-imaging tools to explore the links between brain and mind, hoping to unravel some of the mysteries of consciousness. One good way
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_049.jpg	to understand a complex system is to disturb it and then see what happens. By smashing atoms, a particle accelerator forces them to yield
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_050.jpg	their secrets. By administering psychedelics in carefully calibrated doses, neuroscientists can profoundly disturb the normal waking
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_051.jpg	consciousness of volunteers, dissolving the structures of the self and occasioning what can be described as a mystical experience.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_052.jpg	While this is happening, imaging tools can observe the changes in the brain’s activity and patterns of connection. Already this work is
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_053.jpg	yielding surprising insights into the “neural correlates” of the sense of self and spiritual experience. The hoary 1960s platitude that
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_054.jpg	psychedelics offered a key to understanding—and “expanding”—consciousness no longer looks quite so preposterous. How to Change
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_055.jpg	Your Mind is the story of this renaissance. Although it didn’t start out that way, it is a very personal as well as public history.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_056.jpg	Perhaps this was inevitable. Everything I was learning about the third- person history of psychedelic research made me want to explore this
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_057.jpg	novel landscape of the mind in the first person too—to see how the changes in consciousness these molecules wrought actually feel and
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_058.jpg	what, if anything, they had to teach me about my mind and might contribute to my life. • • •THIS WAS, FOR ME, a completely
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_059.jpg	unexpected turn of events. The history of psychedelics I’ve summarized here is not a history I lived. I was born in 1955, halfway through
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_060.jpg	the decade that psychedelics first burst onto the American scene, but it wasn’t until the prospect of turning sixty had drifted into view that I
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_061.jpg	seriously considered trying LSD for the first time. Coming from a baby boomer, that might sound improbable, a dereliction of generational
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_062.jpg	duty.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_063.jpg	But I was only twelve years old in 1967, too young to have been more than dimly aware of the Summer of Love or the San Francisco Acid
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_064.jpg	Tests. At fourteen, the only way I was going to get to Woodstock was if my parents drove me.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_065.jpg	Much of the 1960s I experienced through the pages of Time magazine. By the time the idea of trying or not trying LSD swam into my
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_066.jpg	conscious awareness, it had already completed its speedy media arc from psychiatric wonder drug to counterculture sacrament to
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_067.jpg	destroyer of young minds.
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_068.jpg	I must have been in junior high school when a scientist reported (mistakenly, as it turned out) that LSD scrambled your chromosomes; the
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_069.jpg	entire media, as well as my health-ed teacher, made sure we heard all about it. A couple of years later, the television personality Art
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_070.jpg	Linkletter began campaigning against LSD, which he blamed for the fact his daughter had jumped out of an apartment window, killing
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_071.jpg	herself. LSD supposedly had something to do with the Manson murders too. By the early 1970s, when I went to college, everything you
test/images/synth_doc_00122_line_072.jpg	heard about LSD seemed calculated to terrify.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_000.jpg	If the Earth were enveloped in a large solar flare, itself an insignificant event astrophysically, our biosphere would be instantly sterilized,
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_001.jpg	and that catastrophe would have as little effect on the sun as a raindrop has on an erupting volcano. Our biosphere is, in terms of its mass,
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_002.jpg	energy or any similar astrophysical measure of significance, a negligible fraction even of the Earth, yet it is a truism of astronomy that the
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_003.jpg	solar system consists essentially of the Sun and Jupiter. Everything else (including the Earth) is ‘just impurities’. Moreover, the solar system
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_004.jpg	is a negligible component of our Galaxy, the Milky Way, which is itself unremarkable among the many in the known universe. So it seems
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_005.jpg	that, as Stephen Hawking put it, ‘The human race is just a chemical scum on a moderate-sized planet, orbiting round a very average star in
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_006.jpg	the outer suburb of one among a hundred billion galaxies.’ Thus the prevailing view today is that life, far from being central, either
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_007.jpg	geometrically, theoretically or practically, is of almost inconceivable insignificance.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_008.jpg	Biology, in this picture, is a subject with the same status as geography. Knowing the layout of the city of Oxford is important to those of us
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_009.jpg	who live there, but unimportant to those who never visit Oxford.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_010.jpg	Similarly,it seems that life is a property of some parochial area, or perhaps areas, of the universe, fundamental to us because we are alive,
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_011.jpg	but not at all fundamental either theoretically or practically in the larger scheme of things. But remarkably, this appearance is misleading, It
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_012.jpg	is simply not true that life is insignificant in its physical effects, nor is it theoretically derivative. As a first step to explaining this, let me
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_013.jpg	explain my earlier remark that life is a form of virtual-reality generation. I have used the word ‘computers’ for the mechanisms that execute
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_014.jpg	gene programs inside living cells, but that is slightly loose terminology. Compared with the general-purpose computers that we
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_015.jpg	manufacture artificially, they do more in some respects and less in others.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_016.jpg	One could not easily program them to do word processing or to factorize large numbers. On the other hand, they exert exquisitely accurate,
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_017.jpg	interactive control over the responses of a complex environment (the organism) to everything that may happen to it. And this control is
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_018.jpg	directed towards causing the environment to act back upon the genes in a specific way (namely, to replicate them) such that the net effect
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_019.jpg	on the genes is as independent as possible of what may be happening outside. This is more than just computing.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_020.jpg	It is virtual-reality rendering. The analogy with the human technology of virtual reality is no perfect.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_021.jpg	First, although genes are enveloped, just as a user of virtual reality is, in an environment whose detailed constitution and behaviour are
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_022.jpg	specified by a program (which the genes themselves embody), the genes do not experience that environment because they have neither
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_023.jpg	senses nor experiences. So if an organism is an virtual-reality rendering specified by its genes, it is a rendering without an audience.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_024.jpg	Second, the organism is not only being rendered, it is being manufactured. It is not a matter of ‘fooling’ the gene into believing that there is
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_025.jpg	an organism out there.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_026.jpg	The organism really is out there. However, these differences are unimportant. As I have said, all virtual-reality rendering physically
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_027.jpg	manufactures the rendered environment. The inside of any virtual-reality generator in the act of rendering is precisely a real, physical
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_028.jpg	environment, manufactured to have the properties specified in the program. It is just that we users sometimes choose to interpret it as a
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_029.jpg	different environment, which happens to feel the same.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_030.jpg	As for the absence of a user, let us consider explicitly what the role of the user of virtual reality is. First, it is to kick the rendered
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_031.jpg	environment and to be kicked back in return — in other words, to interact with the environment in an autonomous way. In the biological
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_032.jpg	case, that role is performed by the external habitat. Second, it is to provide the intention behind the rendering. That is to say, it makes little
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_033.jpg	sense to speak of a particular situation as being a virtual-reality rendering if there is no concept of the rendering being accurate or
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_034.jpg	inaccurate.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_035.jpg	I have said that the accuracy of a rendering is the closeness, as perceived by the user, of the rendered environment to the intended one. But
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_036.jpg	what does accuracy mean for a rendering which no one intended and no one perceives? It means the degree of adaptation of the genes to
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_037.jpg	their niche.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_038.jpg	We can infer the ‘intention’ of genes to render environment that will replicate them, from Darwin’s theory of evolution. Genes become
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_039.jpg	extinct if they do not enact that ‘intention’ as efficiently or resolutely as other competing genes.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_040.jpg	So living processes and virtual-reality renderings are, superficial differences aside, the same sort of process. Both involve the physical
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_041.jpg	embodying ofgeneral theories about an environment. In both cases these theories are used to realize that environment and to control,
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_042.jpg	interactively, not just its instantaneous appearance but also its detailed response to general stimuli. Genes embody knowledge about their
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_043.jpg	niches.
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_044.jpg	Everything of fundamental significance about the phenomenon of life depends on this property, and not on replication per se. So we can
test/images/synth_doc_00130_line_045.jpg	now take the discussion beyond replicators.
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_000.jpg	I tried to reassure her as we abruptly turned around and picked up our pace. It was hot and the air was thick with humidity. She said, “I
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_001.jpg	really don’t want to run into anybody.” I assured her we wouldn’t.
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_002.jpg	I still felt more or less myself, but it may be that Judith’s distress was keeping me from feeling the mushrooms; somebody had to be ready
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_003.jpg	to act normally if a neighbor happened to drive by and roll down his window for a chat, a prospect that was quickly taking on the
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_004.jpg	proportions of nightmare. In fact shortly before we got back to home base—so it now felt to both of us—we spotted a neighbor’s pickup
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_005.jpg	truck bearing down on us and, like guilty children, we ducked into the woods until it passed. Judith made a beeline for the couch in the
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_006.jpg	living room, where she lay down with the shades drawn, while I went into the kitchen to polish off my cup of mushroom tea, because I
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_007.jpg	wasn’t yet feeling very much.
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_008.jpg	I was a little worried about her, but once she reached her base on the living room couch, her mood lightened and she said she was fine. I
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_009.jpg	couldn’t understand her desire to be indoors. I went out and sat on the screened porch for a while, listening to the sounds in the garden,
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_010.jpg	which suddenly grew very loud, as if the volume had been turned way up. The air was stock-still, but the desultory sounds of flying insects
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_011.jpg	and thedigital buzz of hummingbirds rose to form a cacophony I had never heard before.
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_012.jpg	It began to grate on my nerves, until I decided I would be better off regarding the sound as beautiful, and then all at once it was. I lifted an
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_013.jpg	arm, then a foot, and noted with relief that I wasn’t paralyzed, though I also didn’t feel like moving a muscle. Whenever I closed my eyes,
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_014.jpg	random images erupted as if the insides of my lids were a screen. My notes record: Fractal patterns, tunnels plunging through foliage, ropy
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_015.jpg	vines forming grids.
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_016.jpg	But when I started to feel panic rise at the lack of control I had over my visual field, I discovered that all I needed to do to restore a sense of
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_017.jpg	semi-normalcy was to open my eyes. To open or close my eyes was like changing the channel. I thought, “I am learning how to manage this
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_018.jpg	experience.” Much happened, or seemed to happen, during the course of that August afternoon, but I want to focus here on just one
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_019.jpg	element of the experience, because it bears on the questions of nature and our place in it that psilocybin seems to provoke, at least for me. I
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_020.jpg	decided I wanted to walk out to my writing house, a little structure I had built myself twenty- five years ago, in what is now another life,
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_021.jpg	and which holds a great many memories.
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_022.jpg	I had written two and a half books in the little room (including one about building it), sitting before a broad window that looked back over
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_023.jpg	a pond and the garden to our house. However, I was still vaguely worried about Judith, so before wandering too far from the house, I went
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_024.jpg	inside to check on her. She was stretched out on the couch, with a cool damp cloth over her eyes. She was fine. “I’m having these very
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_025.jpg	interesting visuals,” she said, something having to do with the stains on the coffee table coming to life, swirling and transforming and
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_026.jpg	rising from the surface in ways she found compelling. She made it clear she wanted to be left alone to sink more deeply into the
test/images/synth_doc_00132_line_027.jpg	images—she is a painter.
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_000.jpg	The day after my journey I was glad for the opportunity to return to Mary’s room for a couple of hours of “integration.” I hoped to make
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_001.jpg	senseof what happened by telling the story of my trip and hearing her thoughts about it. What you’ve just read is the result, and the
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_002.jpg	beneficiary, of that work, for immediately after the journey I was much more confused by it than I am now.
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_003.jpg	What now reads like a reasonably coherent narrative highlighting certain themes began as a jumble of disjointed images and shards of
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_004.jpg	sense. To put words to an experience that was in fact ineffable at the time, and then to shape them into sentences and then a story, is
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_005.jpg	inevitably to do it a kind of violence. But the alternative is, literally, unthinkable. Mary had taken apart the altar, but we sat in the same
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_006.jpg	chairs, facing each other across a small table. Twenty-four hours later, what had I learned?
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_007.jpg	That I had had no reason to be afraid: no sleeping monsters had awakened in my unconscious and turned on me. This was a deep fear that
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_008.jpg	went back several decades, to a terrifying moment in a hotel room in Seattle when, alone and having smoked too much cannabis, I had had
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_009.jpg	to marshal every last ounce of will to keep myself from doing something deeply crazy and irrevocable. But here in this room I had let down
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_010.jpg	my guard completely, and nothing terrible had happened. The serpent of madness that I worried might be waiting had not surfaced or
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_011.jpg	pulled me under. Did this mean it didn’t exist, that I was psychologically sturdier than I believed?
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_012.jpg	Maybe that’s what the episode with Bob was all about: maybe I was more like him than I knew, and not nearly as deep or complicated as I
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_013.jpg	liked to think. (Can a recognition of one’s shallowness qualify as a profound insight? Mary wasn’t so sure: “You bring a different self to the
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_014.jpg	journey every time.” The demons might rouse themselves the next time.
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_015.jpg	That I could survive the dissolution of my ego without struggle or turning into a puddle was something to be grateful for, but even better
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_016.jpg	was the discovery that there might be another vantage—one less neurotic and more generous—from which to take in reality. “That alone
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_017.jpg	seems worth the price of admission,” Mary offered, and I had to agree. Yet, twenty-four hours later, my old ego was back in uniform and
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_018.jpg	on patrol, so what long-term good was that beguiling glimpse of a loftier perspective? Mary suggested that having had a taste of a different,
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_019.jpg	less defended way to be, I might learn, through practice, to relax the ego’s trigger-happy command of my reactions to people and events.
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_020.jpg	“Now you have had anexperience of another way to react—or not react.
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_021.jpg	That can be cultivated.” Meditation, she suggested, was one way to do that. It is, I think, precisely this perspective that had allowed so
test/images/synth_doc_00134_line_022.jpg	many of the volunteers I interviewed to overcome their fears and anxieties, and in the case of the smokers, their addictions.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_000.jpg	For even in purely practical applications, the explanatory power of a theory is paramount and its predictive power only supplementary. If
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_001.jpg	this seems surprising, imagine that an extraterrestrial scientist has visited the Earth and given us an ultra-high- technology ‘oracle’ which
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_002.jpg	can predict the outcome of any possible experiment, but provides no explanations. According to instrumentalists, once we had that oracle
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_003.jpg	we should have no further use for scientific theories, except as a means of entertaining ourselves.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_004.jpg	But is that true? How would the oracle be used in practice?
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_005.jpg	In some sense it would contain the knowledge necessary to build, say, an interstellar spaceship. But how exactly would that help us to
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_006.jpg	build one, or to build another oracle of the same kind — or even a better mousetrap? The oracle only predicts the outcomes of experiments.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_007.jpg	Therefore, in order to use it at all we must first know what experiments to ask it about.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_008.jpg	If we gave it the design of a spaceship, and the details of a proposed test flight, it could tell us how the spaceship would perform on such a
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_009.jpg	flight. But it could not design the spaceship for us in the first place. And even if it predicted that the spaceship we had designed would
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_010.jpg	explode on take-off, it could not tell us how to prevent such an explosion.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_011.jpg	That would still be for us to work out. And before we could work it out, before we could even begin to improve the design in any way, we
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_012.jpg	should have to understand, among other things, how the spaceship was supposed to work. Only then would we have any chance of
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_013.jpg	discovering what might cause an explosion on take-off.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_014.jpg	Prediction — even perfect, universal prediction — is simply no substitute for explanation. Similarly, in scientific research the oracle would
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_015.jpg	not provide us with any new theory.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_016.jpg	Not until we already had a theory, and had thought of an experiment that would test it, could we possibly ask the oracle what would
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_017.jpg	happen if the theory were subjected to that test. Thus, the oracle would not be replacing theories at all: it would be replacing experiments. It
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_018.jpg	would spare us the expense of running laboratories and particle accelerators. Instead of building prototype spaceships, and risking the
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_019.jpg	lives of test pilots, we could do all the testing on the ground with pilots sitting in flight simulators whose behaviour was controlled by the
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_020.jpg	predictions of the oracle.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_021.jpg	The oracle would be very useful in many situations, but its usefulness would always depend on people’s ability to solve scientific problems
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_022.jpg	in just the way they have to now, namely by devising explanatory theories. It would not even replace all experimentation, because its
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_023.jpg	ability to predict the outcome of a particular experiment would in practice depend on how easy it was to describe the experiment
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_024.jpg	accurately enough for the oracle to give a useful answer, compared with doing the experiment in reality. After all, the oracle would have to
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_025.jpg	have some sort of ‘user interface’. Perhaps a description of the experiment would have to be entered into it, in some standard language. In
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_026.jpg	that language, some experiments would be harder to specify than others.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_027.jpg	In practice, for many experiments the specification would be too complex to be entered. Thus the oracle would have the same general
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_028.jpg	advantages and disadvantages as any other source of experimental data, and it would be useful only in cases where consulting it happened
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_029.jpg	to be more convenient than using other sources.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_030.jpg	To put that another way: there already is one such oracle out there, namely the physical world. It tells us the result of any possible
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_031.jpg	experiment if we ask it in the right language (i.e. if we do theexperiment), though in some cases it is impractical for us to ‘enter a
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_032.jpg	description of the experiment’ in the required form (i.e. to build and operate the apparatus). But it provides no explanations.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_033.jpg	In a few applications, for instance weather forecasting, we may be almost as satisfied with a purely predictive oracle as with an explanatory
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_034.jpg	theory. But even then, that would be strictly so only if the oracle’s weather forecast were complete and perfect. In practice, weather
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_035.jpg	forecasts are incomplete and imperfect, and to make up for that they include explanations of how the forecasters arrived at their
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_036.jpg	predictions. The explanations allow us to judge the reliability of a forecast and to deduce further predictions relevant to our own location
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_037.jpg	and needs.
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_038.jpg	For instance, it makes a difference to me whether today’s forecast that it will be windy tomorrow is based on an expectation of a nearby
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_039.jpg	high-pressure area, or of a more distant hurricane. I would take more precautions in the latter case. Meteorologists themselves also need
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_040.jpg	explanatory theories about weather so that they can guess what approximations it is safe to incorporate in their computer simulations of
test/images/synth_doc_00131_line_041.jpg	the weather, what additional observations would allow the forecast to be more accurate and more timely, and so on.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_000.jpg	This parallel-universe multiplicity is the real reason for the unpredictability of the weather. Our inability to measure the initial conditions
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_001.jpg	accurately is completely irrelevant. Even if we knew the initial conditions perfectly, the multiplicity, and therefore the unpredictability of
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_002.jpg	the motion, would remain.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_003.jpg	And on the other hand, in contrast to the classical case, an imaginary multiverse with only slightly different initial conditions would not
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_004.jpg	behave very differently from the real multiverse: it might suffer hurricanes in 30.000001 per cent of its universes and not in the remaining
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_005.jpg	69.999 999 per cent. The flapping of butterflies’ wings does not, in reality, cause hurricanes because the classical phenomenon of chaos
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_006.jpg	depends on perfect determinism, which does not hold in any single universe.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_007.jpg	Consider a group of identical universes at an instant at which, in all of them, a particular butterfly’s wings have flapped up. Consider a
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_008.jpg	second group of universes which at the same instant are identical to the first group, except that in them the butterfly’s wings are down.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_009.jpg	Wait for a few hours. Quantum mechanics predicts that, unless there are exceptional circumstances (such as someone watching the
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_010.jpg	butterfly and pressing a button to detonate a nuclear bomb if it flaps its wings), the two groups of universes, nearly identical at first, are
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_011.jpg	still nearly identical.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_012.jpg	But each group, within itself, has become greatly differentiated. It includes universes with hurricanes, universes without hurricanes, and
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_013.jpg	even a very tiny number of universes in which the butterfly has spontaneously changed its species through an accidental rearrangement of
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_014.jpg	all its atoms, or the Sun has exploded because all its atoms bounced by chance towards the nuclear reaction at its core. Even so, the two
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_015.jpg	groups still resemble each other very closely.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_016.jpg	In the universes in which the butterfly raised its wings and hurricanes occurred, those hurricanes were indeed unpredictable; but the
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_017.jpg	butterfly was not causally responsible, for there were near-identical hurricanes in universes where everything else was the same but the
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_018.jpg	wings were lowered. It is perhaps worth stressing the distinction between unpredictability and intractability. Unpredictability has nothing
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_019.jpg	to do with the available computational resources. Classical systems are unpredictable (or would be,if they existed) because of their
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_020.jpg	sensitivity to initial conditions.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_021.jpg	Quantum systems do not have that sensitivity, but are unpredictable because they behave differently in different universes, and so appear
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_022.jpg	random in most universes. In neither case will any amount of computation lessen the unpredictability. Intractability, by contrast, is a
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_023.jpg	computational-resource issue. It refers to a situation where we could readily make the prediction if only we could perform the required
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_024.jpg	computation, but we cannot do so because the resources required are impractically large.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_025.jpg	In order to disentangle the problems of unpredictability from those of intractability in quantum mechanics, we have to consider quantum
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_026.jpg	systems that are, in principle, predictable. Quantum theory is often presented as making only probabilistic predictions. For example, in the
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_027.jpg	perforated-barrier-and-screen type of interference experiment described in Chapter 2, the photon can be observed to arrive anywhere in the
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_028.jpg	‘bright’ part of the shadow pattern.
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_029.jpg	But it is important to understand that for many other experiments quantum theory predicts a single, definite outcome. In other words, it
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_030.jpg	predicts that all universes will end up with the same outcome, even if the universes differed at intermediate stages of the experiment, and it
test/images/synth_doc_00133_line_031.jpg	predicts what that outcome will be.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_000.jpg	That is, if the rules designated a given proof as valid, he wanted to be sure that they could never designate any proof with the opposite
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_001.jpg	conclusion as valid. How could he be sure of such a thing? This time, consistency would have to be proved, using a method of proof which
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_002.jpg	itself adhered to the same rules of inference. Then Hilbert hoped that Aristotelian completeness and certainty would be restored, and that
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_003.jpg	every true mathematical statement would in principle be provable under the rules, and that no false statement would be. In 1900, to mark
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_004.jpg	the turn of the century, Hilbert published a list of problems that he hoped mathematicians might be able to solve during the course of the
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_005.jpg	twentieth century.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_006.jpg	The tenth problem was to find a set of rules of inference with the above properties, and, by their own standards, to prove them consistent.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_007.jpg	Hilbert was to be definitively disappointed. Thirty-one years later, Kurt Gödel revolutionized proof theory with a root-and-branch
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_008.jpg	refutation from which the mathematical and philosophical worlds are still reeling: he proved that Hilbert’s tenth problem is insoluble.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_009.jpg	Gödel proved first that any set of rules of inference that is capable of correctly validating even the proofs of ordinary arithmetic could
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_010.jpg	never validate a proof of its own consistency. Therefore there is no hope of finding the provably consistent set of rules that Hilbert
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_011.jpg	envisaged. Second, Gödel proved that if a set of rules of inference in some (sufficiently rich) branch of mathematics is consistent (whether
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_012.jpg	provably so or not), then within that branch of mathematics there must exist valid methods of proof that those rules fail to designate as
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_013.jpg	valid. This is called Gödel’s incompleteness theorem.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_014.jpg	To prove his theorems, Gödel used a remarkable extension of the Cantor ‘diagonal argument’ that I mentioned inChapter 6. He began by
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_015.jpg	considering any consistent set of rules of inference. Then he showed how to construct a proposition which could neither be proved nor
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_016.jpg	disproved under those rules.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_017.jpg	Then he proved that that proposition would be true. If Hilbert’s programme had worked, it would have been bad news for the conception
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_018.jpg	of reality that I am promoting in this book, for it would have removed the necessity for understanding in judging mathematical ideas.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_019.jpg	Anyone — or any mindless machine — that could learn Hilbert’s hoped-for rules of inference by heart would be as good a judge of
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_020.jpg	mathematical propositions as the ablest mathematician, yet without needing the mathematician’s insight or understanding, or even having
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_021.jpg	the remotest clue as to what the propositions were about. In principle, it would be possible to make new mathematical discoveries without
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_022.jpg	knowing any mathematics at all, beyond Hilbert’s rules. One would simply check through all possible strings of letters and mathematical
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_023.jpg	symbols in alphabetical order, until one of them passed the test for being a proof or disproof of some famous unsolved conjecture.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_024.jpg	In principle, one could settle any mathematical controversy without ever understanding it — without even knowing the meanings of the
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_025.jpg	symbols, let alone understanding how the proof worked, or what it proved, or what the method of proof was, or why it was reliable. It may
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_026.jpg	seem that the achievement of a unified standard of proof in mathematics could at least have helped us in the overall drive towards
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_027.jpg	unification — that is, the ‘deepening’ of our knowledge that I referred to in Chapter 1. But the opposite is the case. Like the predictive
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_028.jpg	‘theory of everything’ in physics, Hilbert’s rules would have told us almost nothing about the fabric of reality. They would, as far as
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_029.jpg	mathematics goes, have realized the ultimate reductionist vision, predicting everything (in principle) but explaining nothing.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_030.jpg	Moreover, if mathematics had been reductionist then all the undesirable features which I argued in Chapter 1 are absent from the structure
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_031.jpg	of human knowledge would have been present in mathematics: mathematical ideas would have formed a hierarchy, with Hilbert’s rules at
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_032.jpg	its root. Mathematical truths whose verification from the rules was very complex would have been objectively less fundamental than those
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_033.jpg	that could be verified immediately from the rules. Since there could have been only a finite supply of such fundamental truths, as time went
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_034.jpg	on mathematics would have had to concern itself with ever less fundamental problems. Mathematics might well have come to an end,
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_035.jpg	under this dismal hypothesis.
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_036.jpg	If it did not, it would inevitably have fragmented into ever more arcane specialities, as the complexity of the ‘emergent’ issues that
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_037.jpg	mathematicians would have been forced to study increased, and as the connections between those issues and the foundations of the subject
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_038.jpg	became ever more remote. Thanks to Goedel, we know that there will never be a fixed method of determining whether a mathematical
test/images/synth_doc_00137_line_039.jpg	proposition is true, any more than there is a fixed way of determining whether a scientific theory is true.
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_000.jpg	In terms of its gross effect on the outcomes of physical processes, knowledge is at least as significant as any other physical quantity.But is
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_001.jpg	there, as the ancients assumed there must be in the case of life, a basic physical difference between knowledge-bearing and non-knowledge-
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_002.jpg	bearing objects, a difference that depends neither on the objects’ environments nor on their effects on the remote future, but only on the
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_003.jpg	objects’ immediate physical attributes? Remarkably, there is. To see what it is, we must take the multiverse view.
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_004.jpg	Consider the DNA of a living organism, such as a bear, and suppose that somewhere in one of its genes we find the sequence
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_005.jpg	TCGTCGTTTC. That particular string of ten molecules, in the special niche consisting of the rest of the gene and its niche, is a replicator. It
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_006.jpg	embodies a small but significant amount of knowledge.
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_007.jpg	Now suppose, for the sake of argument, that we can find a junk-DNA (non-gene) segment in the bear’s DNA which also has the sequence
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_008.jpg	TCGTCGTTTC. Nevertheless this sequence is not; worth calling a replicator, because it contributes almost nothing to its replication, and it
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_009.jpg	embodies no knowledge.
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_010.jpg	It is a random sequence. So here we have two physical objects, both segments of the same DNA chain, one of which embodies knowledge
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_011.jpg	and the other is a random sequence. But they are physically identical. How can knowledge be a fundamental physical quantity, if one object
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_012.jpg	has it while a physically identical object does not?
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_013.jpg	It can, because these two segments are not really identical. They only look identical when viewed from some universes, such as ours. Let us
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_014.jpg	look at them again, as they appear in other universes. We cannot directly observe other universes, so we must use theory. We know that
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_015.jpg	DNA in living organisms is naturally subject to random variations — mutations — in the sequence of A, C, G and T molecules.
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_016.jpg	According to the theory of evolution, the adaptations in genes, and therefore the genes’ very existence, depend on such mutations having
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_017.jpg	occurred. Because of mutations, populations of any gene contain a degree of variation, and individuals carrying genes with higher degrees
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_018.jpg	of adaptation tend to have more offspring than other individuals.
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_019.jpg	Most variations in a gene make it unable to cause its replication, because the altered sequence no longer instructs the cell to manufacture
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_020.jpg	anything useful. Others merely make replication less likely (that is, they narrow the gene’s niche). But some may happen to embody new
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_021.jpg	instructions that make replication more likely. Thus natural selection occurs. With each generation of variation and replication the degree
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_022.jpg	of adaptation of the surviving genes tends to increase.
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_023.jpg	Now, a random mutation, caused for instance by a cosmic-ray strike, causes variation not only within the population of the organism in
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_024.jpg	one universe, but between universes as well. A cosmic ‘ray’ is a high-energy sub-atomic particle and, like a photon emitted from a torch, it
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_025.jpg	travels in different directions in different universes.
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_026.jpg	So when a cosmic-ray particle strikes a DNA strand and causes a mutation, some of its counterparts in other universes are missing their
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_027.jpg	copies of the DNA strand altogether, while others are striking it at different positions, and hence causing different mutations. Thus a single
test/images/synth_doc_00139_line_028.jpg	cosmic-ray strike on a single DNA molecule will in general cause a large range of different mutations to appear in different universes.
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_000.jpg	It’s still early days in our understanding of consciousness, and no single one of our vocabularies for approaching the subject—the
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_001.jpg	biological, the psychological, the philosophical, or the spiritual—has yet earned the right to claim it has the final word. It may be that by
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_002.jpg	layering these different perspectives one upon the other, we can gain the richest picture of what might be going on.In a follow-up study to
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_003.jpg	the NYU trial, “Patient Experiences of Psilocybin-Assisted Psychotherapy,” published in the Journal of Humanistic Psychology in 2017,
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_004.jpg	Alexander Belser, a member of the NYU team, interviewed volunteers to better understand the psychological mechanisms underlying the
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_005.jpg	transformations they experienced.
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_006.jpg	I read the study as a subtle attempt to move beyond the mystical experience paradigm to a more humanistic one and at the same time to
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_007.jpg	underscore the importance of the psychotherapist in the psychedelic experience. (Note the use of the term “psilocybin-assisted
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_008.jpg	psychotherapy” in the title; neither of the papers in Psychopharmacology mentioned psychotherapy in its title, only the drug. A few key
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_009.jpg	themes emerged. All of the patients interviewed described powerful feelings of connection to loved ones (“relational embeddedness” is the
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_010.jpg	term the authors used) and, more generally, a shift “from feelings of separateness to interconnectedness.” In most cases, this shift was
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_011.jpg	accompanied by a repertoire of powerful emotions, including “exalted feelings of joy, bliss, and love.” Difficult passages during the journey
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_012.jpg	were typically followed by positive feelings of surrender and acceptance (even of their cancers) as people’s fears fell away. Jeffrey Guss, a
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_013.jpg	coauthor on the paper and a psychiatrist, interprets what happens during the session in terms of the psilocybin’s “egolytic” effects—the
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_014.jpg	drug’s ability to either silence or at least muffle the voice of the ego.
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_015.jpg	In his view, which is informed by his psychoanalytic training, the ego is a mental construct that performs certain functions on behalf of the
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_016.jpg	self. Chief among these are maintaining the boundary between the conscious and the unconscious realms of the mind and the boundary
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_017.jpg	between self and other, or subject and object. It is only when these boundaries fade or disappear, as they seem to do under the influence of
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_018.jpg	psychedelics, that we can “let go of rigid patterns of thought, allowing us to perceive new meanings with less fear.” The whole question of
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_019.jpg	meaning is central to the approach of the NYU therapists,* and is perhaps especially helpful in understanding the experience of the cancer
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_020.jpg	patients on psilocybin. For many of these patients, a diagnosis of terminal cancer constitutes, among other things, a crisis of meaning. Why
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_022.jpg	Why have I been singled out for this fate? Is there any sense to life and the universe? Under the weight of this existential crisis, one’s
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_023.jpg	horizon shrinks, one’s emotional repertoirecontracts, and one’s focus narrows as the mind turns in on itself, shutting out the world. Loops
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_024.jpg	of rumination and worry come to occupy more of one’s mental time and space, reinforcing habits of thought it becomes ever more difficult
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_025.jpg	to escape. Existential distress at the end of life bears many of the hallmarks of a hyperactive default network, including obsessive
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_026.jpg	self-reflection and an inability to jump the deepening grooves of negative thinking.
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_027.jpg	The ego, faced with the prospect of its own extinction, turns inward and becomes hypervigilant, withdrawing its investment in the world
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_028.jpg	and other people. The cancer patients I interviewed spoke of feeling closed off from loved ones, from the world, and from the full range of
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_029.jpg	emotions; they felt, as one put it, “existentially alone.” By temporarily disabling the ego, psilocybin seems to open a new field of
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_030.jpg	psychological possibility, symbolized by the death and rebirth reported by many of the patients I interviewed. At first, the falling away of
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_031.jpg	the self feels threatening, but if one can let go and surrender, powerful and usually positive emotions flow in—along with formerly
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_032.jpg	inaccessible memories and sense impressions and meanings. No longer defended by the ego, the gate between self and other—Huxley’s
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_033.jpg	reducing valve—is thrown wide open. And what comes through that opening for many people, in a great flood, is love.
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_034.jpg	Love for specific individuals, yes, but also, as Patrick Mettes came to feel (to know!), love for everyone and everything—love as the
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_035.jpg	meaning and purpose of life, the key to the universe, and the ultimate truth. So it may be that the loss of self leads to a gain in meaning.
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_036.jpg	Can this be explained biologically? Probably not yet, but recent neuroscience offers a few intriguing clues. Recall that the Imperial College
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_037.jpg	team found that when the default mode network disintegrates (taking with it the sense of self), the brain’s overall connectivity increases,
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_038.jpg	allowing brain regions that don’t ordinarily communicate to form new lines of connection. Is it possible that some of these new connections
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_039.jpg	in the brain manifest in the mind as new meanings or perspectives? The connecting of formerly far- flung dots?
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_040.jpg	It may also be that psychedelics can directly imbue otherwise irrelevant sensory information with meaning. A recent paper in Current
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_041.jpg	Biology* described an experiment in which pieces of music that held no personal relevance for volunteers were played for them while on
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_042.jpg	LSD.Under the influence of the psychedelic, however, volunteers attributed marked and lasting personal meaning to the same songs.These
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_043.jpg	medicines may help us construct meaning, if not discover it. No doubt the suggestibility of the mind on psychedelics and the guiding
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_044.jpg	presence of psychotherapists also play a role in attributing meaning to the experience. In preparing volunteers for their journeys, Jeffrey
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_045.jpg	Guss speaks explicitly about the acquisition of meaning, telling his patients “that the medicine will show you hidden or unknown shadow
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_046.jpg	parts of yourself; that you will gain insight into yourself, and come to learn about the meaning of life and existence.” (He also tells them
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_047.jpg	they may have a mystical or transcendent experience but carefully refrains from defining it.) “As a result of this molecule being in your
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_048.jpg	body, you’ll understand more about yourself and life and the universe.” And more often than not this happens. Replace the science-y word
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_049.jpg	“molecule” with “sacred mushroom” or “plant teacher,” and you have the incantations of a shaman at the start of a ceremonial healing.
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_050.jpg	But however it works, and whatever vocabulary we use to explain it, this seems to me the great gift of the psychedelic journey, especially to
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_051.jpg	the dying: its power to imbue everything in our field of experience with a heightened sense of purpose and consequence. Depending on
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_052.jpg	one’s orientation, this can be understood either in humanistic or in spiritual terms—for what is the Sacred but a capitalized version of
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_053.jpg	significance? Even for atheists like Dinah Bazer—like me!—psychedelics can charge a world from which the gods long ago departed with
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_054.jpg	the pulse of meaning, the immanence with which they once infused it. The sense of a cold and arbitrary universe governed purely by
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_055.jpg	chance is banished. Especially in the absence of faith, these medicines, in the right hands, may offer powerful antidotes for the existential
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_056.jpg	terrors that afflict not only the dying.
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_057.jpg	To believe that life has any meaning at all is of course a large presumption, requiring in some a leap of faith, but surely it is a helpful one,
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_058.jpg	and never more so than at the approach of death. To situate the self in a larger context of meaning, whatever it is—a sense of oneness with
test/images/synth_doc_00135_line_059.jpg	nature or universal love—can make extinction of the self somewhat easier to contemplate.
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_000.jpg	Which usually made things worse. Andrew Weil, who as a young doctor volunteered in the Haight- Ashbury Free Clinic in 1968, saw a lot
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_001.jpg	of bad trips and eventually developed an effective way to “treat” them. “I would examine the patient, determine it was a panic reaction,
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_002.jpg	and then tell him or her, ‘Will you excuse me for a moment? There’s someone in the next room who has a serious problem.’ They would
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_003.jpg	immediately begin to feel much better.” The risks of LSD and other psychedelic drugs were fiercely debated during the 1960s, both among
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_004.jpg	scientists and in the press. Voices on both sides of this debate typically cherry-picked evidence and anecdotes to make their case, but Sidney
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_005.jpg	Cohen was an exception, approaching the question with an open mind and actually conducting research to answer it.
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_006.jpg	Beginning in 1960, he published a series of articles that track his growing concerns. For his first study, Cohen surveyed forty-four
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_007.jpg	researchers working with psychedelics, collecting data on some five thousand subjects taking LSD or mescaline on a total of twenty-five
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_008.jpg	thousand occasions. He found only two credible reports of suicide in this population (a low rate for a group of psychiatric patients), several
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_009.jpg	transient panic reactions, but “no evidence of serious prolonged physical side effects.” He concluded that when psychedelics are
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_010.jpg	administered by qualified therapists and researchers, complications were “surprisingly infrequent” and that LSD and mescaline were
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_011.jpg	“safe.”Leary and others often cited Cohen’s 1960 paper as an exoneration of psychedelics. Yet in a follow-up article published in the Journal
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_012.jpg	of the American Medical Association in 1962, Cohen reported new and “alarming” developments. The casual use of LSD outside the
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_013.jpg	clinical setting, and in the hands of irresponsible therapists, was leading to “serious complications” and occasional “catastrophic
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_014.jpg	reactions.” Alarmed that physicians were losing control of the drug, Cohen warned that “the dangers of suicide, prolonged psychotic
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_015.jpg	reactions and antisocial acting out behavior exist.” In another paper published in the Archives of General Psychiatry the following year, he
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_016.jpg	reported several cases of psychotic breaks and an attempted suicide and presented an account of a boy who, after ingesting a sugar cube
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_017.jpg	laced with LSD that his father, a detective, had confiscated from a “pusher,” endured more than a month of visual distortions and anxiety
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_018.jpg	before recovering.
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_019.jpg	It was this article that inspired Roy Grinker, the journal’s editor, to condemn psychedelic research in an accompanying commentary, even
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_020.jpg	though Cohen himself continued to believe that psychedelics in the hands of responsible therapists had great potential. A fourth article that
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_021.jpg	Cohen published in 1966 reported still more LSD casualties, including two accidental deaths associated with LSD, one from drowning and
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_022.jpg	the other from walking into traffic shouting, “Halt.” But balanced assessments of the risks and benefits of psychedelics were the exception
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_023.jpg	to what by 1966 had become a full-on moral panic about LSD. A handful of headlines from the period suggests the mood: “LSD-Use
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_024.jpg	Charged with Killing Teacher”; “Sampled LSD, Youth Plunges from Viaduct”; “LSD Use Near Epidemic in California”; “Six Students
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_025.jpg	Blinded on LSD Trip in Sun”; “Girl, 5, Eats LSD and Goes Wild”; “Thrill Drug Warps Mind, Kills”; and “A Monster in Our Midst—a Drug
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_026.jpg	Called LSD.” Even Life magazine, which had helped ignite public interest in psychedelics just nine years before with R. Gordon Wasson’s
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_027.jpg	enthusiastic article on psilocybin, joined the chorus of condemnation, publishing a feverish cover story titled “LSD: The Exploding Threat
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_028.jpg	of the Mind Drug That Got out of Control.” Never mind that the magazine’s publisher and his wife had recently had several positive LSD
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_029.jpg	experiences themselves (under the guidance of Sidney Cohen); now the kids were doing it, and it had gotten “out of control.” With pictures
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_030.jpg	of crazed people cowering in corners, the story warned that “an LSD trip is not always a round trip”but rather could be “a one-way trip to
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_031.jpg	an asylum, a prison or a grave.”* As Clare Boothe Luce wrote to Sidney Cohen in 1965, “LSD has been your Frankenstein monster.” • • •
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_032.jpg	OTHER POWERFUL DRUGS subject to abuse, such as the opiates, have managed to maintain a separate identity as a legitimate tool of
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_033.jpg	medicine. Why not psychedelics?
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_034.jpg	The story of Timothy Leary, the most famous psychedelic researcher, made it difficult to argue that a bright line between the scientific and
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_035.jpg	the recreational use of psychedelics could be drawn and patrolled. The man had deliberately—indeed gleefully—erased all such lines.
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_036.jpg	But the “personality” of the drug may have as much to do with the collapse of such distinctions as the personalities of people like Timothy
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_037.jpg	Leary or the flaws in their research. What doomed the first wave of psychedelic research was an irrational exuberance about its potential
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_038.jpg	that was nourished by the drugs themselves—that, and the fact that these chemicals are what today we would call disruptive technologies.
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_039.jpg	For people working with these powerful molecules, it was impossible not to conclude that—like that divinity student running down
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_040.jpg	Commonwealth Avenue—you were suddenly in possession of news with the power to change not just individuals but the world.
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_041.jpg	To confine these drugs to the laboratory, or to use them only for the benefit of the sick, became hard to justify, when they could do so much
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_042.jpg	for everyone, including the researchers themselves! Leary might have made his more straitlaced colleagues cringe at his lack of caution, yet
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_043.jpg	most of them shared his exuberance and had come to more or less the same conclusions about the potential of psychedelics; they were just
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_044.jpg	more judicious when speaking about them in public. Who among the first generation of psychedelic researchers would dispute a word of
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_045.jpg	this classic gust of Leary exuberance, circa 1963: “Make no mistake: the effect of consciousness-expanding drugs will be to transform our
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_046.jpg	concepts of human nature, of human potentialities, of existence. The game is about to be changed, ladies and gentlemen. Man is about to
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_047.jpg	make use of that fabulous electrical network he carries around inhis skull.
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_048.jpg	Present social establishments had better be prepared for the change. Our favorite concepts are standing in the way of a floodtide, two
test/images/synth_doc_00138_line_049.jpg	billion years building up.
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_000.jpg	He conjectured that this repertoire consisted precisely of ‘every function that would naturally be regarded as computable’. He meant
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_001.jpg	computable by mathematicians. But mathematicians are rather untypical physical objects. Why should we assume that rendering them in
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_002.jpg	the act of performing calculations is the ultimate in computational tasks? It turns out that it is not.
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_003.jpg	As I shall explain in Chapter 9, quantum computers can perform computations of which no (human) mathematician will ever, even in
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_004.jpg	principle, be capable. It is implicit in Turing’s work that he expected what ‘would naturally be regarded as computable’ to be also what
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_005.jpg	could, at least in principle, be computed in nature. This expectation is tantamount to a stronger, physical version of the Church-Turing
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_006.jpg	conjecture. The mathematician Roger Penrose has suggested that it should be called the Turing principle:The Turing principle (for abstract
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_007.jpg	computers simulating physical objects) There exists an abstract universal computer whose repertoire includes any computation that any
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_008.jpg	physically possible object can perform. Turing believed that the ‘universal computer’ in question was the universal Turing machine.
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_009.jpg	To take account of the wider repertoire of quantum computers, I have stated the principle in a form that does not specify which particular
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_010.jpg	‘abstract computer’ does the job. The proof I have given of the existence of Cantgotu environments is essentially due to Turing.
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_011.jpg	As I said, he was not thinking explicitly in terms of virtual reality, but an ‘environment that can be rendered’ does correspond to a class of
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_012.jpg	mathematical questions whose answers can be calculated. Those questions are computable. The remainder, the questions for which there is
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_013.jpg	no way of calculating the answer, are called non-computable. If a question is non-computable that does not mean that it has no answer, or
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_014.jpg	that its answer is in any sense ill-defined or ambiguous. On the contrary, it means that it definitely has an answer.
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_015.jpg	It is just that physically there is no way, even in principle, of obtaining that answer (or more precisely, since one could always make a
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_016.jpg	lucky, unverifiable guess, of proving that it is the answer). For example, a prime pair is a pair of prime numbers whose difference is 2, such
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_017.jpg	as 3 and 5, or 11 and 13. Mathematicians have tried in vain to answer the question whether there are infinitely many such pairs, or only a
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_018.jpg	finite number of them.
test/images/synth_doc_00143_line_019.jpg	It is not even known whether this question is computable. Let us suppose that it is not.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_000.jpg	New rays of relation appear between us and all the world’s Others. Even in the case of the minerals, modern physics (forget psychedelics!)
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_001.jpg	gives us reason to wonder if perhaps some form of consciousness might not figure in the construction of reality. Quantum mechanics holds
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_002.jpg	that matter may not be as innocent of mind as the materialist would have us believe. For example, a subatomic particle can exist
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_003.jpg	simultaneously in multiple locations, is pure possibility, until it is measured—that is, perceived by a mind. Only then and not a moment
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_004.jpg	sooner does it drop into reality as we know it: acquire fixed coordinates in time and space.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_005.jpg	The implication here is that matter might not exist as such in the absence of a perceiving subject. Needless to say, this raises some tricky
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_006.jpg	questions for a materialist understanding of consciousness. The ground underfoot may be much less solid than we think.This is the view of
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_007.jpg	quantum physics, not some psychonaut—though it is a very psychedelic theory.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_008.jpg	I mention it only because it lends some of the authority of science to speculations that would otherwise sound utterly lunatic. I still tend to
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_009.jpg	think that consciousness must be confined to brains, but I am less certain of this belief now than I was before I embarked on this journey.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_010.jpg	Maybe it too has slipped out from between the bars of that cage. Mysteries abide. But this I can say with certainty: the mind is vaster, and
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_011.jpg	the world ever so much more alive, than I knew when I began.Glossary active placebo: A type of placebo used in drug trials to fool the
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_012.jpg	volunteer into thinking he has received the psychoactive drug being tested. In the psilocybin trials, researchers have used niacin, which
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_013.jpg	produces a tingling sensation, and methylphenidate (Ritalin), which is a stimulant. ayahuasca: A psychedelic tea made from a combination
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_014.jpg	of plants native to the Amazon basin, typically Banisteriopsis caapi and Psychotria viridis (or chacruna), and used sacramentally by
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_015.jpg	indigenous peoples of South America.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_016.jpg	The chacruna plant contains the psychedelic compound DMT (N,N-dimethyltryptamine), but it is deactivated by digestive enzymes unless
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_017.jpg	it is ingested with a monoamine oxidase inhibitor such as Banisteriopsis. In 2006, the U.S. Supreme Court affirmed the right of the
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_018.jpg	Brazil-based UDV Church to use ayahuasca as a sacrament.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_019.jpg	Beckley Foundation: The organization established by Amanda Feilding in England in 1998 to support research into psychedelics and
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_020.jpg	advocate internationally for the reform of drug laws. The organization is named for Feilding’s ancestral estate in Oxfordshire
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_021.jpg	(BeckleyFoundation.org). Council on Spiritual Practices (CSP): A nonprofit organization established by Bob Jesse in 1993 and “dedicated to
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_022.jpg	making direct experience of the sacred more available to more people.” CSP helped organize and fund the first experiments in psychedelic
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_023.jpg	research at Johns Hopkins; CSP also supported the suit that resulted in the 2006 Supreme Court decision recognizing ayahuasca as a
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_024.jpg	sacrament in the UDV Church.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_025.jpg	In 1995, CSP developed and published the “Code of Ethics for Spiritual Guides” that many underground psychedelic guides have adopted
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_026.jpg	(csp.org). default mode network (DMN): A set of interacting brain structures first described in 2001 by the Washington University
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_027.jpg	neuroscientist Marcus Raichle. The default mode network, called that because it is most active when the brain is in a resting state, links
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_028.jpg	parts of the cerebral cortex with deeper and evolutionarily older structures of the brain involved in emotion and memory.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_029.jpg	(Its key structures include, and link, the posterior cingulate cortex, the medial prefrontal cortex, and the hippocampus. Neuroimaging
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_030.jpg	studies suggest that the DMN is involved in such higher-order “metacognitive” activities as self-reflection, mental projection, time travel,
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_031.jpg	and theory of mind— the ability to attribute mental states to others.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_032.jpg	Activity in the DMN falls during the psychedelic experiences, and when it falls most precipitously volunteers often report a dissolution of
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_033.jpg	their sense of self. DMT (or N,N-dimethyltryptamine): A rapid-onset, intense, and short-acting psychedelic compound sometimes referred
