test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_034.jpg	to as “the businessman’s trip.” This tryptamine molecule is found in many plants and animals for reasons not well understood.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_035.jpg	empathogen: A psychoactive drug that produces a heightened sense of connectedness, emotional openness, and compassion.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_036.jpg	MDMA, or Ecstasy, is such a drug. Also sometimes called anentactogen. entheogen: From the Greek, “generating the divine within.” A
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_037.jpg	psychoactive substance that produces or facilitates a spiritual experience. Entheogens have been used by many cultures for thousands of
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_038.jpg	years, whether by shamans or as part of religious or spiritual practices.
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_039.jpg	However, the term was not coined until the 1970s, by a group of scholars that included R. Gordon Wasson, Richard Evans Schultes,
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_040.jpg	Jonathan Ott, and Carl Ruck. The word was intended to help rehabilitate psychedelics by distinguishing their ancient spiritual role from the
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_041.jpg	recreational uses to which they were often put beginning in the 1960s. Esalen, or the Esalen Institute: A retreat center in Big Sur, California,
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_042.jpg	founded in 1962 to explore the various methods for expanding consciousness that often go under the umbrella of the human potential
test/images/synth_doc_00140_line_043.jpg	movement.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_000.jpg	And then in a handful of sentences he slid into a prophetic voice, one in which the whole future trajectory of Timothy Leary could be
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_001.jpg	foretold: Listen! Wake up! You are God! You have the Divine plan engraved in cellular script within you. Listen!
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_002.jpg	Take this sacrament! You’ll see!
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_003.jpg	You’ll get the revelation! It will change your life! But at least for the first year or two at Harvard, Leary went through the motions of doing
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_004.jpg	science. Back in Cambridge that fall, he recruited Richard Alpert, a promising assistant professor who was heir to a railroad fortune, and,
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_005.jpg	having secured the tacit approval of their department chair, David McClelland, the two launched the Harvard Psilocybin Project, operating
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_006.jpg	out of a tiny broom closet of an office in the Department of Social Relations in a house at 5 Divinity Avenue.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_007.jpg	(I went looking for the house, but it has long since been razed and replaced by a sprawling, block-long brick science building. Leary, ever
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_008.jpg	the salesman, had convinced Harvard that the research he proposed to undertake was squarely in the tradition of William James, who in
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_009.jpg	the early years of the century had also studied altered states of consciousness and mystical experience at Harvard. The university placed
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_010.jpg	one condition on the research: Leary and Alpert could give the new drugs to graduate students, but not to undergraduates. Before long, an
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_011.jpg	intriguingly titled new seminar showed up in the Harvard course listings: Experimental Expansion of Consciousness The literature
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_012.jpg	describing internally and externally induced changes in awareness will be reviewed.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_013.jpg	The basic elements of mystical experiences will be studied cross-culturally. Themembers of the seminar will participate in experiences with
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_014.jpg	consciousness expanding methods and a systematic analysis of attention will be paid to the problems of methodology in this area.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_015.jpg	This seminar will be limited to advanced graduate students. Admission by consent of the instructor. “Experimental Expansion of
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_016.jpg	Consciousness” proved to be extremely popular. • • • IN ITS THREE YEARS of existence, the Harvard Psilocybin Project accomplished
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_017.jpg	surprisingly little, at least in terms of science. In their first experiments, Leary and Alpert administered psilocybin to hundreds of people of
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_018.jpg	all sorts, including housewives, musicians, artists, academics, writers, fellow psychologists, and graduate students, who then completed
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_019.jpg	questionnaires about their experiences. According to “Americans and Mushrooms in a Naturalistic Environment: A Preliminary Report,”
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_020.jpg	most subjects had generally very positive and occasionally life-changing experiences. “Naturalistic” was apt: these sessions took place not
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_021.jpg	in university buildings but in comfortable living rooms, accompanied by music and candlelight, and to a casual observer they would have
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_022.jpg	looked more like parties than experiments, especially because the researchers themselves usually joined in.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_023.jpg	(Leary and Alpert took a heroic amount of psilocybin and, later, LSD. At least in the beginning, Leary, Alpert, and their graduate students
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_024.jpg	endeavored to write up accounts of their own and their subjects’ psilocybin journeys, as if they were pioneers exploring an unmapped
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_025.jpg	frontier of consciousness and the previous decade of work surveying the psychedelic landscape had never happened. “We were on our
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_026.jpg	own,” Leary wrote, somewhat disingenuously. “Western literature had almost no guides, no maps, no texts that even recognized the
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_027.jpg	existence of altered states.” Drawing on their extensive fieldwork, however, Leary did do some original work theorizing the idea of “set”
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_028.jpg	and “setting,” deploying the words in this context for the first time in the literature. These useful terms, if not the concepts they denote—for
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_029.jpg	which Al Hubbard deservesmost of the credit—may well represent Leary’s most enduring contribution to psychedelic science. Leary and
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_030.jpg	Alpert published a handful of papers in the early years at Harvard that are still worth reading, both as well-written and closely observed
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_031.jpg	ethnographies of the experience and as texts in which the early stirrings of a new sensibility can be glimpsed. Building on the idea that the
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_032.jpg	life-changing experiences of volunteers in the Psilocybin Project might have some broader social application, in 1961 Leary and a graduate
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_033.jpg	student, Ralph Metzner, dreamed up a more ambitious research project.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_034.jpg	The Concord Prison Experiment sought to discover if the potential of psilocybin to change personality could be used to reduce recidivism
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_035.jpg	in a population of hardened criminals. That this audacious experiment ever got off the ground is a testimony to Leary’s salesmanship and
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_036.jpg	charm, for not only the prison psychiatrist but the warden had to sign off on it. The idea was to compare the recidivism rates of two groups
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_037.jpg	of prisoners in a maximum security prison in Concord, Massachusetts. A group of thirty-two inmates received psilocybin in sessions that
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_038.jpg	took place in the prison, with one member of Leary’s team taking the drug with them—so as not to condescend to the prisoners, Leary
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_039.jpg	explained, or treat them like guinea pigs.* The other remained straight in order to observe and take notes. A second group of inmates
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_040.jpg	received no drugs or special treatment of any kind.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_041.jpg	The two groups were then followed for a period of months after their release. Leary reported eye-popping results: ten months after their
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_042.jpg	release, only 25 percent of the psilocybin recipients had ended up back in jail, while the control group returned at a more typical rate of 80
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_043.jpg	percent. But when Rick Doblin at MAPS meticulously reconstructed the Concord experiment decades later, reviewing the outcomes subject
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_044.jpg	by subject, he concluded that Leary had exaggerated the data; in fact, there was no statistically significant difference in the rates of
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_045.jpg	recidivism between the two groups. (Even at the time, the methodological shortcomings of the study had prompted David McClelland, the
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_046.jpg	department chair, to write a scathing memo to Metzner.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_047.jpg	Of Leary’s scientific work, Sidney Cohen, himself a psychedelic researcher, concluded that “it was the sort of research that made scientists
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_048.jpg	wince.” Leary played a more tangential role in one other, much more credible study done in the spring of 1962: the Good Friday
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_049.jpg	Experiment, describedin chapter one. Unlike the Concord Prison Experiment, the “Miracle at Marsh Chapel,” as it became known, made a
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_050.jpg	good faith effort to honor the conventions of the controlled, double-blind psychology experiment. Neither the investigators nor the
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_051.jpg	subjects—twenty divinity students— were told who had gotten the drug and who had gotten the placebo, which was active.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_052.jpg	The Good Friday study was far from perfect; Pahnke suppressed the fact that one subject freaked out and had to be sedated. Yet Pahnke’s
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_053.jpg	main conclusion—that psilocybin can reliably occasion a mystical experience that is “indistinguishable from, if not identical with,” the
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_054.jpg	experiences described in the literature—still stands and helped to inspire the current wave of research, particularly at Johns Hopkins,
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_055.jpg	where it was replicated (roughly speaking) in 2006. But most of the credit for the Good Friday Experiment rightfully belongs to Walter
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_056.jpg	Pahnke, not Timothy Leary, who was critical of its design from the start; he had told Pahnke it was a waste of time to use a control group or
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_057.jpg	a placebo. “If we learned one thing from that experience,” Leary later wrote, “it was how foolish it was to use a double- blind experiment
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_058.jpg	with psychedelics. After five minutes, no one’s fooling anyone.” • • • BY NOW, Leary had pretty much lost interest in doing science; he
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_059.jpg	was getting ready to trade the “psychology game” for what he would call the “guru game.” (Perhaps Leary’s most endearing character trait
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_060.jpg	was never to take himself too seriously—even as a guru.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_061.jpg	It had become clear to him that the spiritual and cultural import of psilocybin and LSD far outweighed any therapeutic benefit to
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_062.jpg	individuals. As with Hubbard and Huxley and Osmond before him, psychedelics had convinced Leary that they had the power not just to
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_063.jpg	heal people but to change society and save humankind, and it was his mission to serve as their prophet.
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_064.jpg	It was as though the chemicals themselves had hit upon a brilliant scheme for their own proliferation, by colonizing the brains of a certain
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_065.jpg	type of charismatic and messianic human. “We were thinking far-out history thoughts at Harvard,” Leary later wrote about this period,
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_066.jpg	“believing that it was a time (after the shallow,nostalgic fifties) for far-out visions, knowing that America had run out of philosophy, that a
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_067.jpg	new, empirical, tangible meta-physics was desperately needed.” The bomb and the cold war formed the crucial background to these ideas,
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_068.jpg	investing the project with urgency. Leary was also encouraged in his shift from scientist to evangelist by some of the artists he turned on. In
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_069.jpg	one notable session at his Newton home in December 1960, Leary gave psilocybin to the Beat poet Allen Ginsberg, a man who needed no
test/images/synth_doc_00136_line_070.jpg	chemical inducement to play the role of visionary prophet.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_000.jpg	That mathematicians throughout the ages should have made various mistakes about matters of proof and certainty is only natural. The
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_001.jpg	present discussion should lead us to expect that the current view will not last for ever, either. But the confidence with which
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_002.jpg	mathematicians have blundered into these mistakes and their inability to acknowledge even the possibility of error in these matters are, I
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_003.jpg	think, connected with an ancient and widespread confusion between the methods of mathematics and its subject-matter. Let me explain.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_004.jpg	Unlike the relationships between physical entities, relationships between abstract entities are independent of any contingent facts and of
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_005.jpg	any laws of physics.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_006.jpg	They are determined absolutely and objectively by the autonomous properties of the abstract entities themselves. Mathematics, the study of
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_007.jpg	these relationships and properties, is therefore the study of absolutely necessary truths. In other words, the truths that mathematics studies
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_008.jpg	are absolutely certain.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_009.jpg	But that does not mean that our knowledge of those necessary truths is itself certain, nor does it mean that the methods of mathematics
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_010.jpg	confer necessary truth on their conclusions. After all, mathematics also studies falsehoods and paradoxes. And that does not mean that the
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_011.jpg	conclusions of such a study are necessarily false or paradoxical.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_012.jpg	Necessary truth is merely the subject-matter of mathematics, not the reward we get for doing mathematics. The objective of mathematics is
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_013.jpg	not, and cannot be, mathematical certainty. It is not even mathematical truth, certain or otherwise. It is, and must be, mathematical
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_014.jpg	explanation.Why, then, does mathematics work as well as it does? Why does it lead to conclusions which, though not certain, can be
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_015.jpg	accepted and applied unproblematically for millennia at least?
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_016.jpg	Ultimately the reason is that some of our knowledge of the physical world is also that reliable and uncontroversial. And when we
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_017.jpg	understand the physical world sufficiently well, we also understand which physical objects have properties in common with which abstract
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_018.jpg	ones. But in principle the reliability of our knowledge of mathematics remains subsidiary to our knowledge of physical reality. Every
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_019.jpg	mathematical proof depends absolutely for its validity on our being right about the rules that govern the behaviour of some physical
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_020.jpg	objects, be they virtual-reality generators, ink and paper, or our own brains.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_021.jpg	So mathematical intuition is a species of physical intuition. Physical intuition is a set of rules of thumb, some perhaps inborn, many built
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_022.jpg	up in childhood, about how the physical world behaves. For example, we have intuitions that there are such things as physical objects, and
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_023.jpg	that they have definite attributes such as shape, colour, weight and position in space, some of which exist even when the objects are
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_024.jpg	unobserved. Another is that there is a physical variable — time — with respect to which attributes change, but that nevertheless objects can
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_025.jpg	retain their identity over time.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_026.jpg	Another is that objects interact, and that this can change some of their attributes. Mathematical intuition concerns the way in which the
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_027.jpg	physical world can display the properties of abstract entities.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_028.jpg	One such intuition is that of an abstract law, or at least an explanation, that underlies the behaviour of objects. The intuition that space
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_029.jpg	admits closed surfaces that separate an ‘inside’ from an ‘outside’ may be refined into the mathematical intuition of a set, which partitions
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_030.jpg	everything into members and non-members of the set. But further refinement by mathematicians (starting with Russell’s refutation of
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_031.jpg	Frege’s set theory) has shown that this intuition ceases to be accurate when the sets in question contain ‘too many’ members (too large a
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_032.jpg	degree of infinity of members). Even if any physical or mathematical intuition were inborn, that would not confer any special authority
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_033.jpg	upon it.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_034.jpg	Inborn intuition cannot be taken as a surrogate for Plato’s ‘memories’ of the world of Forms. For it is a commonplace observation that many
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_035.jpg	of the intuitions built into human beings by accidents of evolution are simply false. For example, the human eye and its controlling
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_036.jpg	software implicitly embody the false theory that yellow light consists of a mixture of red and green light (in the sense that yellow light
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_037.jpg	gives us exactly the same sensation as a mixture of red light and green light does). In reality, all three types of light have different
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_038.jpg	frequencies and cannot be created by mixing light of other frequencies.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_039.jpg	The fact that a mixture of red and green light appears to us to be yellow light has nothing whatever to do with the properties of light, but is
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_040.jpg	a property of our eyes. It is the result of a design compromise that occurred at some time during our distant ancestors’ evolution. It is just
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_041.jpg	possible (though I do not believe it) that Euclidean geometry or Aristotelian logic are somehow built into the structure of our brains, as the
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_042.jpg	philosopher Immanuel Kant believed. But that would not logically imply that they were true. Even in the still more implausible event that
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_043.jpg	we have inborn intuitions that we are constitutionally unable to shake off, such intuitions would still not be necessary truths.The fabric of
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_044.jpg	reality, then, does have a more unified structure than would have been possible if mathematical knowledge had been verifiable with
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_045.jpg	certainty, and hence hierarchical, as has traditionally been assumed.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_046.jpg	Mathematical entities are part of the fabric of reality because they are complex and autonomous. The sort of reality they form is in some
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_047.jpg	ways like the realm of abstractions envisaged by Plato or Penrose: although they are by definition intangible, they exist objectively and
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_048.jpg	have properties that are independent of the laws of physics.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_049.jpg	However, it is physics that allows us to gain knowledge of this realm. And it imposes stringent constraints. Whereas everything in physical
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_050.jpg	reality is comprehensible, the comprehensible mathematical truths are precisely the infinitesimal minority which happen to correspond
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_051.jpg	exactly to some physical truth — like the fact that if certain symbols made of ink on paper are manipulated in certain ways, certain other
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_052.jpg	symbols appear.
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_053.jpg	That is, they are the truths that can be rendered in virtual reality. We have no choice but to assume that the incomprehensible mathematical
test/images/synth_doc_00141_line_054.jpg	entities are real too, because they appear inextricably in our explanations of the comprehensible ones.
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_000.jpg	Yet though it is true that a chemical launched me on this journey, it is also true that everything I experienced I experienced: these are events
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_001.jpg	that took place in my mind, psychological facts that were neither weightless nor evanescent. Unlike most dreams, the traces these
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_002.jpg	experiences inscribed remain indelible and accessible. The day after my journey I was glad for the opportunity to return to Mary’s room for
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_003.jpg	a couple of hours of “integration.” I hoped to make senseof what happened by telling the story of my trip and hearing her thoughts about
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_004.jpg	it. What you’ve just read is the result, and the beneficiary, of that work, for immediately after the journey I was much more confused by it
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_005.jpg	than I am now.
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_006.jpg	What now reads like a reasonably coherent narrative highlighting certain themes began as a jumble of disjointed images and shards of
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_007.jpg	sense. To put words to an experience that was in fact ineffable at the time, and then to shape them into sentences and then a story, is
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_008.jpg	inevitably to do it a kind of violence.
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_009.jpg	But the alternative is, literally, unthinkable. Mary had taken apart the altar, but we sat in the same chairs, facing each other across a small
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_010.jpg	table. Twenty-four hours later, what had I learned? That I had had no reason to be afraid: no sleeping monsters had awakened in my
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_011.jpg	unconscious and turned on me. This was a deep fear that went back several decades, to a terrifying moment in a hotel room in Seattle
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_012.jpg	when, alone and having smoked too much cannabis, I had had to marshal every last ounce of will to keep myself from doing something
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_013.jpg	deeply crazy and irrevocable.
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_014.jpg	But here in this room I had let down my guard completely, and nothing terrible had happened. The serpent of madness that I worried
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_015.jpg	might be waiting had not surfaced or pulled me under. Did this mean it didn’t exist, that I was psychologically sturdier than I believed?
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_016.jpg	Maybe that’s what the episode with Bob was all about: maybe I was more like him than I knew, and not nearly as deep or complicated as I
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_017.jpg	liked to think. (Can a recognition of one’s shallowness qualify as a profound insight?
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_018.jpg	Mary wasn’t so sure: “You bring a different self to the journey every time.” The demons might rouse themselves the next time. That I could
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_019.jpg	survive the dissolution of my ego without struggle or turning into a puddle was something to be grateful for, but even better was the
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_020.jpg	discovery that there might be another vantage—one less neurotic and more generous—from which to take in reality. “That alone seems
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_021.jpg	worth the price of admission,” Mary offered, and I had to agree. Yet, twenty-four hours later, my old ego was back in uniform and on
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_022.jpg	patrol, so what long-term good was that beguiling glimpse of a loftier perspective? Mary suggested that having had a taste of a different,
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_023.jpg	less defended way to be, I might learn, through practice, to relax the ego’s trigger-happy command of my reactions to people and events.
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_024.jpg	“Now you have had anexperience of another way to react—or not react.
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_025.jpg	That can be cultivated.” Meditation, she suggested, was one way to do that. It is, I think, precisely this perspective that had allowed so
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_026.jpg	many of the volunteers I interviewed to overcome their fears and anxieties, and in the case of the smokers, their addictions. Temporarily
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_027.jpg	freed from the tyranny of the ego, with its maddeningly reflexive reactions and its pinched conception of one’s self-interest, we get to
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_028.jpg	experience an extreme version of Keats’s “negative capability”—the ability to exist amid doubts and mysteries without reflexively reaching
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_029.jpg	for certainty.
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_030.jpg	To cultivate this mode of consciousness, with its exceptional degree of selflessness (literally!), requires us to transcend our subjectivity
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_031.jpg	or—it comes to the same thing—widen its circle so far that it takes in, besides ourselves, other people and, beyond that, all of nature. Now I
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_032.jpg	understood how a psychedelic could help us to make precisely that move, from the first- person singular to the plural and beyond. Under
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_033.jpg	its influence, a sense of our interconnectedness—that platitude—is felt, becomes flesh. Though this perspective is not something a chemical
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_034.jpg	can sustain for more than a few hours, those hours can give us an opportunity to see how it might go. And perhaps to practice being there.
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_035.jpg	I left Mary’s loft in high spirits, but also with the feeling I was holding on to something precious by the thinnest, most tenuous of threads. It
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_036.jpg	seemed doubtful I could maintain my grip on this outlook for the rest of the day, much less the rest of my life, but it also seemed worth
test/images/synth_doc_00144_line_037.jpg	trying.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_000.jpg	In the nineteenth century, few things would have been regarded more confidently as real than the force of gravity. Not only did it figure in
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_001.jpg	Newton’s then-unrivalled system of laws, but everyone could feel it, all the time, even with their eyes shut — or so they thought. Today we
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_002.jpg	understand gravity through Einstein’s theory rather than Newton’s, and we know that no such force exists. We do not feel it! What we feel
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_003.jpg	is the resistance that prevents us from penetrating the solid ground beneath our feet.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_004.jpg	Nothing is pulling us downwards. The only reason why we fall downwards when unsupported is that the fabric of space and time in which
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_005.jpg	we exist is curved. Not only do explanations change, but our criteria and ideas about what should count as an explanation are gradually
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_006.jpg	changing (improving) too. So the list of acceptable modes of explanation will always be open-ended, and consequently the list of acceptable
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_007.jpg	criteria for reality must be open-ended too. But what is it about an explanation — given that, for whatever reasons, we find it satisfactory —
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_008.jpg	that should make us classify some things as real and others as illusory or imaginary?
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_009.jpg	James Boswell relates in his Life of Johnson how he and Dr Johnson were discussing Bishop Berkeley’s solipsistic theory of the
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_010.jpg	non-existence of the material world. Boswell remarked that although no one believed the theory, no one could refute it either.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_011.jpg	Dr Johnson kicked a large rock and said, as his foot rebounded, ‘I refute it thus.’ Dr Johnson’s point was that Berkeley’s denial of the rock’s
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_012.jpg	existence is incompatible with finding an explanation of the rebound that he himself felt. Solipsism cannot accommodate any explanation
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_013.jpg	of why that experiment — or any experiment — should have one outcome rather than another. To explain the effect that the rock had on
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_014.jpg	him, Dr Johnson was forced to take a position on the nature of rocks. Were they part of an autonomous external reality, or were they
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_015.jpg	figments of his imagination? In the latter case he would have to conclude that ‘his imagination’ was itself a vast, complex, autonomous
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_016.jpg	universe.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_017.jpg	The same dilemma confronted the solipsistic professor who, if pressed for explanations, would be forced to take a position on the nature of
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_018.jpg	the audience. And the Inquisition would have had to take a position on the source of the underlying regularity in the motion of planets, a
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_019.jpg	regularity that is explicable only by reference to the heliocentric theory.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_020.jpg	For all these people, taking their own position seriously as an explanation of the world would lead them directly to realism and Galilean
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_021.jpg	rationality. But Dr Johnson’s idea is more than a refutation of solipsism.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_022.jpg	It also illustrates the criterion for reality that is used in science, namely, if something can kick back, it exists. ‘Kicking back’ here does not
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_023.jpg	necessarily mean that the alleged object is responding to being kicked — to being physically affected as Dr Johnson’s rock was. It is enough
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_024.jpg	that when we ‘kick’ something, the object affects us in ways that require independent explanation.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_025.jpg	For example, Galileo had no means of affecting planets, but he could affect the light that came from them. His equivalent of kicking the
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_026.jpg	rock was refracting that light through the lenses of his telescopes and eyes.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_027.jpg	That light responded by ‘kicking’ his retina back. The way it kicked back allowed him to conclude not only that the light was real, but that
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_028.jpg	the heliocentric planetary motions required to explain the patterns in which the light arrivedwere also real.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_029.jpg	By the way, Dr Johnson did not directly kick the rock either. A person is a mind, not a body. The Dr Johnson who performed the
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_030.jpg	experiment was a mind, and that mind directly ‘kicked’ only some nerves, which transmitted signals to muscles, which propelled his foot
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_031.jpg	towards the rock. Shortly afterwards, Dr Johnson perceived being ‘kicked back’ by the rock, but again only indirectly, after the impact had
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_032.jpg	set up a pressure pattern in his shoe, and then in his skin, and had then led to electrical impulses in his nerves, and so forth.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_033.jpg	Dr Johnson’s mind, like Galileo’s and everyone else’s, ‘kicked’ nerves and ‘was kicked back’ by nerves, and inferred the existence and
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_034.jpg	properties of reality from those interactions alone. What Dr Johnson was entitled to infer about reality depends on how he could best
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_035.jpg	explain what had happened.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_036.jpg	For example, if the sensation had seemed to depend only on the extension of his leg, and not on external factors, then he would probably
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_037.jpg	have concluded that it was a property of his leg, or of his mind alone. He might have been suffering from a disease which gave him a
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_038.jpg	rebounding sensation whenever he extended his leg in a certain way.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_039.jpg	But in fact the rebounding depended on what the rock did, such as being in a certain place, which was in turn related to other effects that
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_040.jpg	the rock had, such as being seen, or affecting other people who kicked it. Dr Johnson perceived these effects to be autonomous
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_041.jpg	(independent of himself) and quite complicated. Therefore the realist explanation of why the rock produces the rebounding sensation
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_042.jpg	involves a complicated story about something autonomous.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_043.jpg	But so does the solipsist explanation. In fact, any explanation that accounts for the foot-rebounding phenomenon is necessarily a
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_044.jpg	‘complicated story about something autonomous’.
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_045.jpg	It must in effect be the story of the rock. The solipsist would call it a dream-rock, but apart from that claim the solipsist’s story and the
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_046.jpg	realist’s could share the same script. My discussion of shadows and parallel universes in Chapter 2 revolved around questions of what does
test/images/synth_doc_00145_line_047.jpg	or does not exist, and implicitly around what should or should not count as evidence of existence.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_000.jpg	Dan, for his part, was pleased that someone had taken the article seriously. Richie’s interests in consciousness had been first aroused by the
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_001.jpg	works of authors such as Aldous Huxley, British psychiatrist R.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_002.jpg	D. Laing, Martin Buber, and, later, Ram Dass, whose Be Here Now was published just at the start of his graduate studies.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_003.jpg	But these interests had been driven underground during his college years in the psychology department at New York University’s uptown
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_004.jpg	campus in the Bronx, where staunch behaviorists, followers of B. F. Skinner, dominated the psychology department.8 Their firm
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_005.jpg	assumption was that only observable behavior was the proper study of psychology—looking inside the mind was a questionable endeavor,
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_006.jpg	a taboo waste of time. Our mental life, they held, was completely irrelevant to understanding behavior.9 When Richie signed up for a
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_007.jpg	course in abnormal psychology, the textbook was ardently behaviorist, claiming that all psychopathology was the result of operant
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_008.jpg	conditioning, where a desired behavior earns a reward, like a tasty pellet for a pigeon when it pecks the right button.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_009.jpg	That view, Richie felt, was bankrupt: it not only ignored the mind, it also ignored the brain. Richie, who could not stomach this dogma,
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_010.jpg	dropped the course after the first week.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_011.jpg	Richie’s steely conviction was that psychology should study the mind—not reinforcement schedules for pigeons—and so he became a
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_012.jpg	rebel. Richie’s interests in what went on in the mind were, from the strict behaviorist perspective, transgressive.10 While by day he fought
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_013.jpg	the behaviorist tide, his nights were his own to explore other interests. He volunteered to help with sleep research at Maimonides Medical
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_014.jpg	Center, where he learned how to monitor brain activity with EEGs, an expertise that would serve him well throughout the rest of his career
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_015.jpg	in the field. His senior honors thesis adviser was Judith Rodin, with whom Richie conducted research on daydreaming and obesity.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_016.jpg	His hypothesis was that because daydreams take us out of the present, we become less sensitive to the body’s cues of satiety, and so
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_017.jpg	continue eating instead of stopping. The obesity part was because of Rodin’s interest in the topic; daydreaming was Richie’s way of
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_018.jpg	beginning to study consciousness.11 For Richie the study was an excuse to learn techniques to probe what was actually going on inside the
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_019.jpg	mind, using physiological and behavioral measures.Richie monitored people’s heart rate and sweating while they let their mind wander or
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_020.jpg	did mental tasks. This was his first use of physiological measures to infer mental processes, a radical method at the time.12 This
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_021.jpg	methodological sleight of hand, tacking an element of consciousness studies on to an otherwise respectable, mainstream research study,
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_022.jpg	was to be a hallmark of Richie’s research for the next decade or so, when his interest in meditation found little to no support in the ethos of
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_023.jpg	the time. Designing a dissertation that didn’t depend on the meditation piece in itself but could be a stand-alone study on just the
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_024.jpg	nonmeditators turned out to be a smart move for Richie.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_025.jpg	He secured his first academic position at the Purchase campus of the State University of New York, where he kept his interest in meditation
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_026.jpg	to himself while doing seminal work in the emerging field of affective neuroscience—how emotions operate in the brain. Dan, however,
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_027.jpg	could find no teaching post at any university that reflected his own interests in consciousness, and gladly accepted a job in journalism—a
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_028.jpg	career path that eventually led to his becoming a science writer at the New York Times. While there he harvested Richie’s research on
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_029.jpg	emotions and the brain (among other scientists’ work) in writing Emotional Intelligence.13 Of the more than eight hundred articles Dan
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_030.jpg	wrote at the Times, just a meager handful had anything to do with meditation—even as we both continued to attend meditation retreats on
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_031.jpg	our own time. We shelved the notion publicly for a decade or two, while privately pursuing the evidence that intense and prolonged
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_032.jpg	meditation can alter the core of a person’s very being. We were both flying under the radar.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_033.jpg	ALTERED STATES William James Hall looms over Cambridge as an architectural mistake, a fifteen- story modernist white slab glaringly
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_034.jpg	out of place amid the surrounding Victorian homes and the lowlying brick-and-stone buildings of the Harvard campus. At the beginning of
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_035.jpg	the twentieth century, William James became Harvard’s first professor of psychology, a field he had a major hand in inventing as he
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_036.jpg	transitioned from the theoretical universe of philosophy to a more empirical and pragmatic view of the mind. James’s former home still
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_037.jpg	stands in the adjacent neighborhood.neighborhood. Despite this history, as graduate students in the department housed in William James
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_038.jpg	Hall, we were never assigned a single page of James to read—he had long before fallen out of fashion.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_039.jpg	Still, James became an inspiration to us, largely because he engaged the very topic that our professors ignored and that fascinated us:
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_040.jpg	consciousness. Back in James’s day, toward the end of the nineteenth and start of the twentieth centuries, there was a fad among Boston’s
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_041.jpg	cognoscenti to imbibe nitrous oxide (or “laughing gas,” as the compound came to be called when dentists routinely deployed it). James’s
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_042.jpg	transcendent moments with the help of nitrous oxide led him to what he called an “unshakable conviction” that “our normal waking
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_043.jpg	consciousness . . . is but one special type of consciousness, whilst all about it, parted from it by the filmiest of screens, there lie potential
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_044.jpg	forms of consciousness entirely different.”14 After pointing out the existence of altered states of consciousness (though not by that name),
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_045.jpg	James adds, “We may go through life without suspecting their existence; but apply the requisite stimulus, and at a touch they are there in
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_046.jpg	all their completeness.” Dan’s article had begun with this very passage from William James’s The Varieties of Religious Experience, a call to
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_047.jpg	study altered states of consciousness. These states, as James saw, are discontinuous with ordinary consciousness. And, he observed, “No
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_048.jpg	account of the universe in its totality can be final which leaves these other forms of consciousness quite disregarded.” The very existence of
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_049.jpg	these states “means they forbid a premature closing of our accounts with reality.” Psychology’s topography of the mind foreclosed such
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_050.jpg	accounts.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_051.jpg	Transcendental experiences were not to be found anywhere in that terrain; if mentioned at all, they were relegated to the less desirable
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_052.jpg	realms. From the early days of psychology, beginning with Freud himself, altered states were dismissed as symptoms of one or another
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_053.jpg	form of psychopathology.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_054.jpg	For instance, when French poet and Nobel laureate Romain Rolland became a disciple of the Indian saint Sri Ramakrishna around the
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_055.jpg	beginning of the twentieth century, he wrote to Freud describing the mystical state he experienced—and Freud diagnosed it as regression to
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_056.jpg	infancy.15 By the 1960s, psychologists routinely dismissed drug-triggered altered states as artificially induced psychosis (the original term
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_057.jpg	for psychedelics was “psychotomimetic” drugs—psychosis mimics). As we found, similar attitudes applied to meditation—this suspicious
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_058.jpg	new route to altering the mind—at leastapplied to meditation—this suspicious new route to altering the mind—at least among our faculty
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_059.jpg	advisers.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_060.jpg	Still, in 1972 the Cambridge zeitgeist included a fervent interest in consciousness as Richie entered Harvard and Dan returned from his
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_061.jpg	sojourn in Asia (the first of two) to begin his doctoral dissertation. Charles Tart’s bestseller of the day, Altered States of Consciousness,
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_062.jpg	collected articles on biofeedback, drugs, self-hypnosis, yoga, meditation, and other such avenues to James’s “other states,” capturing the
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_063.jpg	ethos of the day.16 In brain science, excitement revolved around the recent discovery of neurotransmitters, the chemicals that send
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_064.jpg	messages between neurons, like the mood regulator serotonin—magic molecules that could pitch us into ecstasy or despair.17 The lab work
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_065.jpg	on neurotransmitters filtered into the general culture as a scientific pretext for attaining altered states through drugs like LSD. These were
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_066.jpg	the days of the psychedelic revolution, which had had its roots in the very department at Harvard we were in, which perhaps helps explain
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_067.jpg	why the remaining stalwarts took a dim view of any interest in the mind that smacked of altered states.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_068.jpg	AN INNER JOURNEY Dalhousie nestles in the lower reaches of the Dhauladhar range, a branch of the Himalayas that stretches into India’s
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_069.jpg	Punjab and Himachal Pradesh states. Established in the mid-nineteenth century as a “hill station” where the bureaucrats of the British Raj
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_070.jpg	could escape the summer heat of the Indo- Gangetic Plain, Dalhousie was chosen for its gorgeous setting. With its picturesque bungalows
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_071.jpg	left over from colonial days, this hill station has long been a tourist attraction. But it wasn’t the setting that brought Richie and Susan to
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_072.jpg	Dalhousie that summer of 1973.
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_073.jpg	They had come for a ten-day retreat—their first deep dive— with S. N. Goenka, the same teacher Dan had done successive retreats with in
test/images/synth_doc_00142_line_074.jpg	Bodh Gaya a few years before while on his first sojourn in India for his predoctoral traveling fellowship.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_000.jpg	But who was this “I” that was able to take in the scene of its own dissolution? Good question. It wasn’t me, exactly. Here, the limits of our
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_001.jpg	language become a problem: in order to completely make sense of the divide that had opened up in my perspective, I would need a whole
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_002.jpg	newfirst-person pronoun.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_003.jpg	For what was observing the scene was a vantage and mode of awareness entirely distinct from my accustomed self; in fact I hesitate to use
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_004.jpg	the “I” to denote the presiding awareness, it was so different from my usual first person. Where that self had always been a subject
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_005.jpg	encapsulated in this body, this one seemed unbounded by any body, even though I now had access to its perspective. That perspective was
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_006.jpg	supremely indifferent, neutral on all questions of interpretation, and unperturbed even in the face of what should by all rights have been an
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_007.jpg	unmitigated personal disaster.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_008.jpg	Yet the “personal” had been obliterated. Everything I once was and called me, this self six decades in the making, had been liquefied and
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_009.jpg	dispersed over the scene. What had always been a thinking, feeling, perceiving subject based in here was now an object out there. I was
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_010.jpg	paint!
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_011.jpg	The sovereign ego, with all its armaments and fears, its backward- looking resentments and forward-looking worries, was simply no more,
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_012.jpg	and there was no one left to mourn its passing. Yet something had succeeded it: this bare disembodied awareness, which gazed upon the
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_013.jpg	scene of the self’s dissolution with benign indifference.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_014.jpg	I was present to reality but as something other than my self. And although there was no self left to feel, exactly, there was a feeling tone,
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_015.jpg	which was calm, unburdened, content. There was life after the death of the ego.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_016.jpg	This was big news. When I think back on this part of the experience, I’ve occasionally wondered if this enduring awareness might have
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_017.jpg	been the “Mind at Large” that Aldous Huxley described during his mescaline trip in 1953. Huxley never quite defined what he meant by
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_018.jpg	the term—except to speak of “the totality of the awareness belonging to Mind at Large”—but he seems to be describing a universal,
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_019.jpg	shareable form of consciousness unbounded by any single brain.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_020.jpg	Others have called it cosmic consciousness, the Oversoul, or Universal Mind. This is supposed to exist outside our brains—as a property of
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_021.jpg	the universe, like light or gravity, and just as pervasive. Constitutive too. Certain individuals at certain times gain access to this awareness,
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_022.jpg	allowing them to perceive reality in its perfected light, at least for a time. Nothing in my experience led me to believe this novel form of
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_023.jpg	consciousness originated outside me; it seems just as plausible, and surely more parsimonious, to assume it was a product of my brain,
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_024.jpg	justlike the ego it supplanted.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_025.jpg	Yet this by itself strikes me as a remarkable gift: that we can let go of so much—the desires, fears, and defenses of a lifetime!—without
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_026.jpg	suffering complete annihilation. This might not come as a surprise to Buddhists, transcendentalists, or experienced meditators, but it was
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_027.jpg	sure news to me, who has never felt anything but identical to my ego. Could it be there is another ground on which to plant our feet? For
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_028.jpg	the first time since embarking on this project, I began to understand what the volunteers in the cancer-anxiety trials had been trying to tell
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_029.jpg	me: how it was that a psychedelic journey had granted them a perspective from which the very worst life can throw at us, up to and
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_030.jpg	including death, could be regarded objectively and accepted with equanimity. • • • ACTUALLY, this understanding arrived a little later,
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_031.jpg	during the last part of my psilocybin trip, when the journey took a darker turn.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_032.jpg	After spending an unknown number of hours in computer world—for time was completely lost on me—I registered the desire to check
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_033.jpg	back in on reality, and to pee again. Same deal: Mary guided me to the bathroom by the elbow, geriatrically, and left me there to produce
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_034.jpg	another spectacular crop of diamonds. But this time I dared to look in the mirror. What looked back at me was a human skull, but for the
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_035.jpg	thinnest, palest layer of skin stretched over it, tight as a drum. The bathroom was decorated in a Mexican folk art theme, and the head/skull
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_036.jpg	immediately put me in mind of the Day of the Dead.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_037.jpg	With its deep sockets and lightning bolt of vein zigzagging down its temple on one side, I recognized this ashen head/skull as my own but
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_038.jpg	at the same time as my dead grandfather’s. This was surprising, if only because Bob, my father’s father, is not someone with whom I ever
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_039.jpg	felt much in common. In fact I loved him for all the ways he seemed unlike me—or anyone else I knew.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_040.jpg	Bob was a preternaturally sunny and seemingly uncomplicated man incapable of thinking ill of anyone or seeing evil in the world. (His
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_041.jpg	wife, Harriet, amply compensated for his generosity of spirit. Bob had a long career as a liquor salesman, making the weekly rounds of the
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_042.jpg	nightclubs in Times Square for a company that everyone but he knew was owned by the mob. Upon reaching the age I am now, he retired
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_043.jpg	to become a painter of lovelynaive landscapes and abstractions in spectacular colors; I’d brought one of them with me to Mary’s room,
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_044.jpg	along with a watercolor of Judith’s. Bob was a genuinely happy, angst-free man who lived to be ninety-six, his paintings becoming ever
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_045.jpg	more colorful, abstract, and free toward the end.
test/images/synth_doc_00148_line_046.jpg	To see him so vividly in my reflection was chilling.
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_000.jpg	However, the response of the meditators who had practiced the longest showed the fastest recovery.This may be a neural echo of
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_001.jpg	“nonstickiness”—showing an appropriate initial response to a disturbing image, but then not having that response linger. CHAPTER SIX.
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_002.jpg	PRIMED FOR LOVE 1. The Desert Fathers were early Christian hermits who lived in communities located in remote areas of Egypt’s desert
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_003.jpg	in the early centuries AD. There they could better focus on their religious practices, mainly the recitation of Kyrie Eleison (a Greek phrase
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_004.jpg	meaning “Lord, have mercy”), a Christian “mantra.” These hermit communities were the historic predecessors of Christian orders for
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_005.jpg	monks and nuns; repetition of Kyrie Eleison remains a primary practice among Eastern Orthodox monks, e.g., those on Mount Athos.
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_006.jpg	Historical records suggest that Christian monks from Egypt settled on Mount Athos in the seventh century, fleeing Islamic conquest. Helen
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_007.jpg	Waddell, The Desert Fathers (Ann Arbor: University of Michigan Press, 1957). 2. The Good Samaritan setup was an experiment, one of an
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_008.jpg	extensive, systematic series of studies of the conditions that encourage or inhibit altruistic acts.
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_009.jpg	Daniel Batson, Altruism in Humans (New York: Oxford University Press, 2011). 3. Sharon Salzberg, Lovingkindness: The Revolutionary
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_010.jpg	Art of Happiness (Boston: Shambhala, 2002). 4.

test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_012.jpg	self-criticism is not limited to depression; it shows up in a range of emotional problems. Like these researchers, we’d like to see a study that
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_013.jpg	shows a meditation-induced rise in self- compassion coupled with a similar shift in related brain circuitry. See Ben Shahar, “A Wait-List

test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_035.jpg	18. There are numerous factors at work in altruism, but the ability to feel the suffering of someone else seems a key ingredient. To be sure,
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_036.jpg	the changes in the meditators were not as strong nor as long-lasting as the structural brain patterns unique to the kidney donors. See
test/images/synth_doc_00149_line_037.jpg	Desbordes, “Effects of Mindful- Attention and Compassion Meditation Training on Amygdala Response to Emotional Stimuli in an
