test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_000.jpg	Nor is there much scope for extending the repertoire by using a range of different virtual-reality generators. Suppose that we had a
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_001.jpg	hundred of them, each (for the sake of argument) with a different repertoire.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_002.jpg	Then the whole collection, combined with the programmable control system that determines which of them shall be used to run a given
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_003.jpg	program, is just a larger virtual-reality generator. That generator is subject to the argument I have given, so for every environment it can
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_004.jpg	render there will be infinitely many that it cannot.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_005.jpg	Furthermore, the assumption that different virtual-reality generators might have different repertoires turns out to be over-optimistic. As we
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_006.jpg	shall see in a moment, all sufficiently sophisticated virtual-reality generators have essentially the same repertoire. Thus our hypothetical
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_007.jpg	project of building the ultimate virtual-reality generator, which had been going so well, has suddenly run into a brick wall.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_008.jpg	Whatever improvements may be made in the distant future, the repertoire of the entire technology of virtual reality will never grow
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_009.jpg	beyond a certain fixed set of environments. Admittedly this set is infinitely large, and very diverse bycomparison with human experience
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_010.jpg	prior to virtual-reality technology. Nevertheless, it is only an infinitesimal fraction of the set of all logically possible environments.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_011.jpg	What would it feel like to be in a Cantgotu environment? Although the laws of physics do not permit us to be in one, it is still logically
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_012.jpg	possible and so it is legitimate to ask what it would feel like. Certainly, it could give us no new sensations, because a universal image
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_013.jpg	generator is possible and is assumed to be part of our high-technology virtual-reality generator. So a Cantgotu environment would seem
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_014.jpg	mysterious to us only after we had experienced it and reflected on the results. It would go something like this.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_015.jpg	Suppose you are a virtual-reality buff in the distant, ultra-high-techhnology future. You have become jaded, for it seems to you that you
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_016.jpg	have already tried everything interesting.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_017.jpg	But then one day a genie appears and claims to be able to transport you to a Cantgotu environment. You are sceptical, but agree to put its
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_018.jpg	claim to the test.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_019.jpg	You are whisked away to the environment. After a few expedients you seem to recognize it — it responds just like one of your favourite
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_020.jpg	environments, which on your home virtual-reality system has program number X. However, you keep experimenting, and eventually,
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_021.jpg	during the Xth subjective minute of the experience, the environment responds in a way that is markedly different from anything that
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_022.jpg	Environment X would do.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_023.jpg	So you give up the idea that this is Environment X. You may then notice that everything that has happened so far is also consistent with
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_024.jpg	another renderable environment, Environment Y. But then, during the Yth subjective minute you are proved wrong again. The
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_025.jpg	characteristic of a Cantgotu environment is simply this: no matter how often you guess, no matter how complex a program you
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_026.jpg	contemplate as being the one that might be rendering the environment, you will always be proved wrong because no program will render
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_027.jpg	it, on your virtual reality generator or on any other. Sooner or later you will have to bring the test to a close.
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_028.jpg	At that point you may well decide to concede the genie’s claim. That is nor to say that you could ever prove that you had been in a
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_029.jpg	Cantgotu environment, for there is always an even more complex program that the genie might have been running, which would match
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_030.jpg	your experiences so far. That is just the general feature of virtual reality that I have already discussed, namely that experience cannot prove
test/images/synth_doc_00151_line_031.jpg	that one is in a given environment, be it the Centre Court at Wimbledon or an environment of the Cantgotu type.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_000.jpg	Can this be explained biologically? Probably not yet, but recent neuroscience offers a few intriguing clues.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_001.jpg	Recall that the Imperial College team found that when the default mode network disintegrates (taking with it the sense of self), the brain’s
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_002.jpg	overall connectivity increases, allowing brain regions that don’t ordinarily communicate to form new lines of connection. Is it possible that
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_003.jpg	some of these new connections in the brain manifest in the mind as new meanings or perspectives? The connecting of formerly far- flung
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_004.jpg	dots? It may also be that psychedelics can directly imbue otherwise irrelevant sensory information with meaning. A recent paper in Current
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_005.jpg	Biology* described an experiment in which pieces of music that held no personal relevance for volunteers were played for them while on
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_006.jpg	LSD.Under the influence of the psychedelic, however, volunteers attributed marked and lasting personal meaning to the same songs.These
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_007.jpg	medicines may help us construct meaning, if not discover it.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_008.jpg	No doubt the suggestibility of the mind on psychedelics and the guiding presence of psychotherapists also play a role in attributing
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_009.jpg	meaning to the experience. In preparing volunteers for their journeys, Jeffrey Guss speaks explicitly about the acquisition of meaning,
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_010.jpg	telling his patients “that the medicine will show you hidden or unknown shadow parts of yourself; that you will gain insight into yourself,
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_011.jpg	and come to learn about the meaning of life and existence.” (He also tells them they may have a mystical or transcendent experience but
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_012.jpg	carefully refrains from defining it.) “As a result of this molecule being in your body, you’ll understand more about yourself and life and the
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_013.jpg	universe.” And more often than not this happens. Replace the science-y word “molecule” with “sacred mushroom” or “plant teacher,” and
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_014.jpg	you have the incantations of a shaman at the start of a ceremonial healing.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_015.jpg	But however it works, and whatever vocabulary we use to explain it, this seems to me the great gift of the psychedelic journey, especially to
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_016.jpg	the dying: its power to imbue everything in our field of experience with a heightened sense of purpose and consequence. Depending on
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_017.jpg	one’s orientation, this can be understood either in humanistic or in spiritual terms—for what is the Sacred but a capitalized version of
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_018.jpg	significance?
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_019.jpg	Even for atheists like Dinah Bazer—like me!—psychedelics can charge a world from which the gods long ago departed with the pulse of
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_020.jpg	meaning, the immanence with which they once infused it. The sense of a cold and arbitrary universe governed purely by chance is
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_021.jpg	banished. Especially in the absence of faith, these medicines, in the right hands, may offer powerful antidotes for the existential terrors that
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_022.jpg	afflict not only the dying.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_023.jpg	To believe that life has any meaning at all is of course a large presumption, requiring in some a leap of faith, but surely it is a helpful one,
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_024.jpg	and never more so than at the approach of death. To situate the self in a larger context of meaning, whatever it is—a sense of oneness with
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_025.jpg	nature or universal love—can make extinction of the self somewhat easier to contemplate. Religion has always understood this wager, but
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_026.jpg	why should religion enjoy a monopoly? Bertrand Russell wrote that the best way to overcome one’s fear of death “is to make your interests
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_027.jpg	graduallywider and more impersonal, until bit by bit the walls of the ego recede, and your life becomes increasingly merged in the
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_028.jpg	universal life.” He goes on: An individual human existence should be like a river: small at first, narrowly contained within its banks, and
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_029.jpg	rushing passionately past rocks and over waterfalls. Gradually, the river grows wider, the banks recede, the waters flow more quietly, and
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_030.jpg	in the end, without any visible break, they become merged in the sea, and painlessly lose their individual being. • • • PATRICK METTES
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_031.jpg	lived seventeen months after his psilocybin session, and according to Lisa those months were filled with a great many unexpected
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_032.jpg	satisfactions, alongside Patrick’s dawning acceptance that he was going to die.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_033.jpg	Lisa had initially been wary of the NYU trial, interpreting Patrick’s desire to participate as a sign he’d given up the fight. In the event, he
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_034.jpg	came away convinced he still had much to do in this life—much love to give and receive—and wasn’t yet ready to leave it and, especially,
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_035.jpg	his wife. Patrick’s psychedelic journey had shifted his perspective, from a narrow lens trained on the prospect of dying to a renewed focus
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_036.jpg	on how best to live the time left to him. “He had a new resolve. That there was a point to his life, that he got it, and was moving with it.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_037.jpg	“We still had our arguments,” Lisa recalled, “and we had a very trying summer” as they endured a calamitous apartment renovation in
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_038.jpg	Brooklyn. “That was hell on earth,” Lisa recalled, but Patrick “had changed.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_039.jpg	He had a sense of patience he had never had before, and with me he had real joy about things. It was as if he had been relieved of the duty
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_040.jpg	of caring about the details of life, and he could let all that go.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_041.jpg	Now it was about being with people, enjoying his sandwich and the walk on the promenade. It was as if we lived a lifetime in a year.”After
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_042.jpg	the psilocybin session, Lisa somehow convinced herself that Patrick was not going to die after all. He continued with his chemo and his
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_043.jpg	spirits improved, but she now thinks all this time “he knew very well he wasn’t going to make it.” Lisa continued to work, and Patrick
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_044.jpg	spent his good days walking the city. “He would walk everywhere, try every restaurant for lunch, and tell me about all the great places he
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_045.jpg	discovered. But his good days got fewer and fewer.” Then, in March 2012, he told her he wanted to stop chemo. “He didn’t want to die,”
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_046.jpg	Lisa says, “but I think he just decided that this is not how he wanted to live.” That fall his lungs began to fail, and Patrick wound up in the
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_047.jpg	hospital. “He gathered everyone together and said good-bye and explained that this is how he wanted to die. He had a very conscious
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_048.jpg	death.” Patrick’s seeming equanimity in the face of death exerted a powerful influence on everyone around him, Lisa said, and his room in
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_049.jpg	the palliative care unit at Mount Sinai became a center of gravity in the hospital. “Everyone, the nurses and the doctors, wanted to hang out
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_050.jpg	in our room; they just didn’t want to leave.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_051.jpg	Patrick would talk and talk. It was like he was a yogi. He put out so much love.” When Tony Bossis visited Patrick a week before he died,
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_052.jpg	he was struck by the mood in the room and by Patrick’s serenity. “He was consoling me. He said his biggest sadness was leaving his wife.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_053.jpg	But he was not afraid.” Lisa e-mailed me a photograph of Patrick she had taken a few days before he died, and when the image popped
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_054.jpg	open on my screen, it momentarily took my breath away. Here was an emaciated man in a hospital gown, an oxygen clip in his nose, but
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_055.jpg	with bright, shining blue eyes and a broad smile. On the eve of death, the man was beaming.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_056.jpg	Lisa stayed with Patrick in his hospital room night after night, the two of them often talking into the wee hours. “I feel like I have one foot
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_057.jpg	in this world and one in the next,” he told her at one point. “One of the last nights we were together, he said, ‘Honey, don’t push me. I’m
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_058.jpg	finding my way.’” At the same time, he sought to comfort her. “This is simply the wheel of life,” she recalls him saying. “‘You feel like
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_059.jpg	you’re being ground down by it now, but the wheel is going to turn and you’ll be on top again.’” Lisa hadn’t had a shower in days, and her
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_060.jpg	brother finally persuaded her to go home for a few hours. Minutes before she returned to hisbedside, Patrick slipped away. “I went home to
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_061.jpg	shower and he died.” We were speaking on the phone, and I could hear her crying softly. “He wasn’t going to die as long as I was there. My
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_062.jpg	brother had told me, ‘You need to let him go.’” Patrick was gone by the time she got back to the hospital. “He had died seconds before.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_063.jpg	It was like something had evaporated from him. I sat with him for three hours. It’s a long time before the soul is out of the room.” “It was a
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_064.jpg	good death,” Lisa told me, a fact she credits to the people at NYU and to Patrick’s psilocybin journey. “I feel indebted to them for what they
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_065.jpg	allowed him to experience—the deep resources they allowed him to tap into. These were his own deep resources.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_066.jpg	That, I think, is what these mind-altering drugs do.” “Patrick was far more spiritual than I was to begin with,” Lisa told me the last time we
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_067.jpg	spoke. It was clear his journey had changed her too. “It was an affirmation of a world I knew nothing about.
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_068.jpg	But there are more dimensions to this world than I ever knew existed.” Two: Addiction The dozen or so Apollo astronauts who have
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_069.jpg	escaped Earth’s orbit and traveled to the moon had the privilege of seeing the planet from a perspective never before available to our
test/images/synth_doc_00146_line_070.jpg	species, and several of them reported that the experience changed them in profound and enduring ways.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_000.jpg	For the first time since embarking on this project, I began to understand what the volunteers in the cancer-anxiety trials had been trying to
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_001.jpg	tell me: how it was that a psychedelic journey had granted them a perspective from which the very worst life can throw at us, up to and
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_002.jpg	including death, could be regarded objectively and accepted with equanimity. • • • ACTUALLY, this understanding arrived a little later,
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_003.jpg	during the last part of my psilocybin trip, when the journey took a darker turn. After spending an unknown number of hours in computer
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_004.jpg	world—for time was completely lost on me—I registered the desire to check back in on reality, and to pee again. Same deal: Mary guided
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_005.jpg	me to the bathroom by the elbow, geriatrically, and left me there to produce another spectacular crop of diamonds. But this time I dared to
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_006.jpg	look in the mirror. What looked back at me was a human skull, but for the thinnest, palest layer of skin stretched over it, tight as a drum.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_007.jpg	The bathroom was decorated in a Mexican folk art theme, and the head/skull immediately put me in mind of the Day of the Dead. With its
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_008.jpg	deep sockets and lightning bolt of vein zigzagging down its temple on one side, I recognized this ashen head/skull as my own but at the
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_009.jpg	same time as my dead grandfather’s. This was surprising, if only because Bob, my father’s father, is not someone with whom I ever felt
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_010.jpg	much in common. In fact I loved him for all the ways he seemed unlike me—or anyone else I knew.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_011.jpg	Bob was a preternaturally sunny and seemingly uncomplicated man incapable of thinking ill of anyone or seeing evil in the world. (His
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_012.jpg	wife, Harriet, amply compensated for his generosity of spirit. Bob had a long career as a liquor salesman, making the weekly rounds of the
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_013.jpg	nightclubs in Times Square for a company that everyone but he knew was owned by the mob. Upon reaching the age I am now, he retired
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_014.jpg	to become a painter of lovelynaive landscapes and abstractions in spectacular colors; I’d brought one of them with me to Mary’s room,
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_015.jpg	along with a watercolor of Judith’s. Bob was a genuinely happy, angst-free man who lived to be ninety-six, his paintings becoming ever
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_016.jpg	more colorful, abstract, and free toward the end.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_017.jpg	To see him so vividly in my reflection was chilling. A few years before, visiting Bob in the nursing home in the Colorado desert where he
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_018.jpg	would soon die, I’d watched what had been a fit and vigorous man (it had been his habit to stand on his head every day well into his
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_019.jpg	eighties) contract into a parenthesis of skin and bones marooned in a tiny bed. The esophageal muscles required to swallow had given out,
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_020.jpg	and he was tethered to a feeding tube. By then, his situation was pitiful in so many respects, but for some reason I fixed on the fact that
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_021.jpg	never again would a taste of food ever cross his lips.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_022.jpg	I splashed cold water on our joint face and made my unsteady way back to Mary. Risking another glance at her, this time I was rewarded
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_023.jpg	by the sight of a ravishing young woman, blond once again but now in the full radiance of youth. Mary was so beautiful I had to look away.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_024.jpg	She gave me another small mushroom—gram number four—and a piece of chocolate.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_025.jpg	Before I put on my eyeshade, I attempted to conduct the rotating mask test a second time . . . and it was a complete bust, neither confirming
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_026.jpg	nor disproving the hypothesis. As the mask began to rotate, gradually bringing its back side into view, the whole thing dissolved into a
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_027.jpg	gray jelly that slid down the screen of my laptop before I could determine whether the melting mask I was watching was convex or
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_028.jpg	concave. So much for conducting psychological experiments while tripping.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_029.jpg	I put on my eyeshades and sank back down into what now became a cracked and parched desert landscape dense with artifacts and images
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_030.jpg	of death. Bleached skulls and bones and the faces of the familiar dead passed before me, aunts and uncles and grandparents, friends and
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_031.jpg	teachers and my father-in-law—with a voice telling me I had failed to properly mourn all of them. It was true.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_032.jpg	I had never really reckoned the death of anyone in my life; something had always gotten in the way. I could do it here and now and did. I
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_033.jpg	looked hard at each of their faces, one after another, with a pity that seemed bottomless but with no fear whatsoever.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_034.jpg	Except once, when Icame to my aunt Ruthellen and watched, horrified, as her face slowly transformed into Judith’s. Ruthellen and Judith
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_035.jpg	were both artists, and both had been diagnosed with breast cancer around the same time. The cancer had killed Ruthellen and spared
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_036.jpg	Judith. So what was Judith doing down here among the unmourned dead? Had I been defending myself against that possibility all this
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_037.jpg	time?
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_038.jpg	Heart wide open, defenses melting, the tears began to flow. • • • I’VE LEFT OUT one important part of my journey to the underworld: the
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_039.jpg	soundtrack. Before going back under for this last passage, I had asked Mary to please stop playing spa music and put on something
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_040.jpg	classical. We settled on the second of Bach’s unaccompanied cello suites, performed by Yo-Yo Ma.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_041.jpg	The suite in D minor is a spare and mournful piece that I’d heard many times before, often at funerals, but until this moment I had never
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_042.jpg	truly listened to it. Though “listen” doesn’t begin to describe what transpired between me and the vibrations of air set in motion by the four
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_043.jpg	strings of that cello. Never before has a piece of music pierced me as deeply as this one did now.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_044.jpg	Though even to call it “music” is to diminish what now began to flow, which was nothing less than the stream of human consciousness,
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_045.jpg	something in which one might glean the very meaning of life and, if you could bear it, read life’s last chapter. (A question formed: Why
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_046.jpg	don’t we play music like this at births as well as funerals?
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_047.jpg	And the answer came immediately: there is too much life-already-lived in this piece, and poignancy for the passing of time that no birth, no
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_048.jpg	beginning, could possibly withstand it. Four hours and four grams of magic mushroom into the journey, this is where I lost whatever
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_049.jpg	ability I still had to distinguish subject from object, tell apart what remained of me and what was Bach’s music. Instead of Emerson’s
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_050.jpg	transparent eyeball, egoless and one with all it beheld, I became a transparent ear, indistinguishable from the stream of sound that flooded
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_051.jpg	my consciousness until there was nothing else in it, not even a dry tiny corner in which to plant an I and observe. Opened to the music, I
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_052.jpg	became first the strings, could feel on my skin the exquisitefriction of the horsehair rubbing over me, and then the breeze of sound flowing
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_053.jpg	past as it crossed the lips of the instrument and went out to meet the world, beginning its lonely transit of the universe.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_054.jpg	Then I passed down into the resonant black well of space inside the cello, the vibrating envelope of air formed by the curves of its spruce
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_055.jpg	roof and maple walls. The instrument’s wooden interior formed a mouth capable of unparalleled eloquence—indeed, of articulating
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_056.jpg	everything a human could conceive. But the cello’s interior also formed a room to write in and a skull in which to think and I was now it,
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_057.jpg	with no remainder.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_058.jpg	So I became the cello and mourned with it for the twenty or so minutes it took for that piece to, well, change everything. Or so it seemed;
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_059.jpg	now, its vibrations subsiding, I’m less certain.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_060.jpg	But for the duration of those exquisite moments, Bach’s cello suite had had the unmistakable effect of reconciling me to death—to the
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_061.jpg	deaths of the people now present to me, Bob’s and Ruthellen’s and Roy’s, Judith’s father’s, and so many others, but also to the deaths to
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_062.jpg	come and to my own, no longer so far off. Losing myself in this music was a kind of practice for that—for losing myself, period. Having let
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_063.jpg	go of the rope of self and slipped into the warm waters of this worldly beauty—Bach’s sublime music, I mean, and Yo-Yo Ma’s bow
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_064.jpg	caressing those four strings suspended over that envelope of air—I felt as though I’d passed beyond the reach of suffering and regret. • • •
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_065.jpg	THAT WAS MY PSILOCYBIN JOURNEY, as faithfully as I can recount it. As I read those words now, doubt returns in full force: “Fool, you
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_066.jpg	were on drugs!” And it’s true: you can put the experience in that handy box and throw it away, never to dwell on it again. No doubt this
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_067.jpg	has been the fate of countless psychedelic journeys that their travelers didn’t quite know what to do with, or failed to make sense of.
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_068.jpg	Yet though it is true that a chemical launched me on this journey, it is also true that everything I experienced I experienced: these are events
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_069.jpg	that took place in my mind, psychological facts that were neither weightless nor evanescent. Unlike most dreams, the traces these
test/images/synth_doc_00147_line_070.jpg	experiences inscribed remain indelible and accessible.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_000.jpg	That basic question was, in those days, quite respectable. But Richie’s PhD research was in the spirit of that concealed excursion into the
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_001.jpg	mind in his undergraduate work. The agenda embedded, sub rosa, in the study: exploring if signs of skill in attention differed in meditators
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_002.jpg	and nonmeditators. Did meditators get better at focusing? In those days, that was not a respectable question.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_003.jpg	Richie measured the brain electrical signals from the scalp of meditators as they heard tones or saw flashing LED lights, while he instructed
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_004.jpg	them to focus on the sounds and ignore the lights, or vice versa. Richie analyzed the electrical signals for “event-related potential” (ERP),
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_005.jpg	indicated by specific blips in response to a light and/or tone. The ERP, embedded in a chorus of noise, is a signal so minuscule it is
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_006.jpg	measured in microvolts—millionths of a volt.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_007.jpg	These tiny signals offer a window on how we allocate our attention. Richie found that the size of these tiny signals was diminished in
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_008.jpg	response to the tone when meditators focused on the light, while the signals triggered by the light were reduced in size when the
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_009.jpg	meditators focused their attention on the tone. That finding alone would be ho-hum; we would expect that. But this pattern of blocking out
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_010.jpg	the unwanted modality was much stronger in the meditators than in the controls—some of the first evidence that meditators were better at
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_011.jpg	focusing their attention than nonmeditators. Since selecting a target for focus and ignoring distractions marks a key attention skill, Richie
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_012.jpg	concluded that brain electrical recordings—the EEG— could be used for this assessment (routine today, but a step in scientific progress
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_013.jpg	back then).
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_014.jpg	Still, the evidence that meditators were any better at this than theback then). Still, the evidence that meditators were any better at this than
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_015.jpg	the control group, who had never meditated, was rather weak. In retrospect, we can see one reason why this evidence was in itself
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_016.jpg	questionable: Richie had recruited a mix of meditators, who deployed various methods.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_017.jpg	Back in 1975 we were quite naive about how important these variations in technique were. Today we know there are many aspects of
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_018.jpg	attention, and that different kinds of meditation train a variety of mental habits, and so, impact mental skills in varying ways. For example,
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_019.jpg	researchers at the Max Planck Institute for Human Cognitive and Brain Sciences in Leipzig, Germany, had novices practice daily for a few
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_020.jpg	months three different types of meditation: focusing on breathing; generating loving-kindness; and monitoring thoughts without getting
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_021.jpg	swept away by them.11 Breath focus, they found, was calming—seeming to confirm a widespread assumption about meditation’s
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_022.jpg	usefulness as a means to relax. But in contradiction to that stereotype, neither the loving-kindness practice nor monitoring thoughts made
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_023.jpg	the body more relaxed, apparently because each demands mental effort: for example, while watching thoughts you continually get swept
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_024.jpg	up in them—and then, when you notice this has happened, need to make a conscious effort to simply watch again.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_025.jpg	In addition, the loving-kindness practice, where you wish yourself and others well, understandably created a positive mood, while the
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_026.jpg	other two methods did not. So, differing types of meditation produce unique results—a fact that should make it a routine move to identify
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_027.jpg	the specific type being studied. Yet confusion about the specifics remains all too common.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_028.jpg	One research group, for instance, has collected state-of-the-art data on brain anatomy in fifty meditators, an invaluable data set.12 Except
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_029.jpg	that the names of the meditation practices being studied reveals a mixture of types—a hodgepodge. Had the specific mental training
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_030.jpg	entailed by each meditation type been methodically recorded, that data set might well yield even more valuable findings. (Even so, kudos
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_031.jpg	for disclosing this information, which too often goes unnoted. As we read through the now vast trove of research on meditation, we
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_032.jpg	sometimes wince when we come across the confusion and naiveté of some scientists about the specifics. Too often they are simply
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_033.jpg	mistaken, like the scientific article that said that in both Zen and Goenka-style vipassana, meditators have their eyes open (what’s wrong
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_034.jpg	here: Goenka has people close their eyes).A handful of studies have used an “antimeditation” method as an active control.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_035.jpg	In one version of this so-called antimeditation, volunteers were told to concentrate on as many positive thoughts as possible—which
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_036.jpg	actually resembles some contemplative methods, such as the loving-kindness meditation we will review in chapter six. The fact that those
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_037.jpg	experimenters thought this was unlike meditation speaks to their confusion about what exactly they were researching. The rule of
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_038.jpg	thumb—that what gets practiced gets improved—underscores the importance of matching a given mental strategy in meditation to its
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_039.jpg	result. This is true equally for those who study meditation and those who meditate: one must be aware of the likely outcomes from a given
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_040.jpg	meditation approach. They are not all the same, contrary to the misunderstanding among some researchers, and even practitioners.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_041.jpg	In the realm of mind (as everywhere else), what you do determines what you get. In sum, “meditation” is not a single activity but a wide
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_042.jpg	range of practices, all acting in their own particular ways in the mind and brain. Lost in Wonderland, Alice asked the Cheshire Cat, “Which
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_043.jpg	way should I go?” He replied, “That depends on where you want to get to.” The Cheshire Cat’s advice to Alice holds, too, for meditation.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_044.jpg	COUNTING THE HOURS Each of Dan’s “expert” meditators, all Transcendental Meditation teachers, had practiced TM for at least two
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_045.jpg	years. But Dan had no way of knowing how many total hours they had put in over those years.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_046.jpg	Nor did he know what the actual quality of those hours might have been. Few researchers, even today, have this crucial piece of data. But,
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_047.jpg	as we will see in more detail in chapter thirteen, “Altering Traits,” our model of change tracks how many lifetime hours of practice a
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_048.jpg	meditator has done and whether it was daily or on retreat. These total hours are then connected with shifts in qualities of being and the
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_049.jpg	underlying differences in the brain that give rise to them. Very often meditators are lumped into gross categories of experience, like
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_050.jpg	“beginner” and “expert,” without any further specifics.
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_051.jpg	One research group reported the daily time the people they studied put into meditation—rangingreported the daily time the people they
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_052.jpg	studied put into meditation—ranging from ten minutes a few times a week to 240 minutes daily—but not how many months or years they
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_053.jpg	had done so, which is essential in calculating lifetime hours of practice. Yet this calculation goes missing in the vast majority of meditation
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_054.jpg	studies. So that classic Zen study from the 1960s showing a failure to habituate to repeated sounds—one of the few existing then and one
test/images/synth_doc_00150_line_055.jpg	that had gotten us interested in the first place—actually gave sparse data on the Zen monks’ meditation experience.
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_000.jpg	Normally Big Pharma foots the bill for such trials, but thus far the pharmaceutical companies have shown scant interest in psychedelics.
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_001.jpg	For one thing, this class of drugs offers them little if any intellectual property: psilocybin is a product of nature, and the patent on LSD
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_002.jpg	expired decades ago. For another, Big Pharma mostly invests in drugs for chronic conditions, the pills you have to take every day. Why
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_003.jpg	would it invest in a pill patients might only need to take once in a lifetime? Psychiatry faces a similar dilemma: it too is wedded to
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_004.jpg	interminable therapies, whether that means the daily antidepressant or the weekly psychotherapy session.
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_005.jpg	It is true that a psychedelic session lasts several hours and usually requires two therapists be present for the duration, but if the therapy
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_006.jpg	works as it’s supposed to, there won’t be a lot of repeat business. It’s not at all clear what the business model might be. Yet. Several of the
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_007.jpg	researchers and therapists I’ve interviewed nevertheless look forward to a time, not far off, when psychedelic therapy is routineand widely
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_008.jpg	available, in the form of a novel hybrid of pharmacology and psychotherapy. George Goldsmith envisions a network of psychedelic
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_009.jpg	treatment centers, facilities in attractive natural settings where patients will go for their guided sessions.
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_010.jpg	He has formed a company called Compass Pathways to build these centers in the belief they can offer a treatment for a range of mental
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_011.jpg	illnesses sufficiently effective and economical that Europe’s national health services will reimburse for them. Goldsmith has so far raised
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_012.jpg	three million pounds to fund and organize psilocybin trials (starting with treatment-resistant depression) at multiple sites in Europe.
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_013.jpg	Already he is working with designers at IDEO, the international design firm, to redesign the entire experience of psychedelic therapy.
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_014.jpg	Paul Summergrad and Tom Insel have both joined his advisory board. Katherine MacLean, the former Hopkins researcher who wrote the
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_015.jpg	landmark paper on openness, hopes someday to establish a “psychedelic hospice,” a retreat center somewhere out in nature where not only
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_016.jpg	the dying but their loved ones can use psychedelics to help them let go—the patient and the loved ones both. “If we limit psychedelics just
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_017.jpg	to the patient,” she explains, “we’re sticking to the old medical model. But psychedelics are more radical than that.
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_018.jpg	I get nervous when people say they should only be prescribed by a doctor. I imagine a broader application.” In MacLean’s words it’s easy
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_019.jpg	to hear echoes of the 1960s experience with psychedelics—the excitement about their potential to help not only the sick but everyone else
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_020.jpg	too. This kind of thinking—or talking—makes some of her mainstream colleagues nervous. It’s exactly the kind of talk that Insel and
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_021.jpg	Summergrad were warning the community against.
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_022.jpg	Good luck with that. “The betterment of well people” is very much on the minds of most of the researchers I interviewed, even if some of
test/images/synth_doc_00153_line_023.jpg	them were more reluctant to discuss it on the record than institutional outsiders like Bob Jesse and Rick Doblin and Katherine MacLean.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_000.jpg	I owe everything to you,” Leary offers at one point, beaming his most excellent smile at Hubbard. “The galactic center sent you down just
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_001.jpg	at the right moment.” Hubbard doesn’t crack a smile. And then, a few minutes later: “You sure as heck contributed your part.”CHAPTER
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_002.jpg	FOUR TRAVELOGUE Journeying Underground MY PLAN HAD BEEN TO volunteer for one of the Hopkins or NYU experimental trials. If
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_003.jpg	I was going to have my own guided psychedelic journey, a harrowing prospect under any circumstances, I very much liked the idea of
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_004.jpg	traveling in the company of trained professionals close by a hospital emergency room. But the aboveground researchers were no longer
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_005.jpg	working with “healthy normals.” This meant that if I hoped to have the journey I had heard so much about, it would have to take place
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_006.jpg	underground.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_007.jpg	Could I find a guide willing to work with a writer who planned to publish an account of his journey, and would that person be someone I
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_008.jpg	felt sufficiently comfortable with and confident in to entrust with my mind? The whole endeavor was fraught with uncertainty and entailed
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_009.jpg	risks of several kinds—legal, ethical, psychological, and even literary. For how do you put into words an experience said to be ineffable?
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_010.jpg	“Curiosity” is an accurate but tepid word for what drove me. By now, I had interviewed at length more than a dozen people who had gone
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_011.jpg	on guided psychedelic journeys, and it was impossible to listen to their stories without wondering what the journey would be like for one’s
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_012.jpg	self.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_013.jpg	For many of them, these were among the two or three most profound experiences of their lives, in several cases changing them in positive
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_014.jpg	and lasting ways. To become more “open”—especially at this age, when the grooves of mental habit have been etched so deep as to seem
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_015.jpg	inescapable —was an appealing prospect. And then there was the possibility, however remote, of having some kind of spiritual epiphany.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_016.jpg	Many of the people I’d interviewed had started out stone-cold materialists and atheists, no more spiritually developed than I, and yet
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_017.jpg	several had had “mysticalexperiences” that left them with the unshakable conviction that there was something more to this world than we
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_018.jpg	know—a “beyond” of some kind that transcended the material universe I presume to constitute the whole shebang.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_019.jpg	I thought often about one of the cancer patients I interviewed, an avowed atheist who had nevertheless found herself “bathed in God’s
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_020.jpg	love.” Yet not everything I’d heard from these people made me eager to follow them onto the couch. Many had been borne by psilocybin
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_021.jpg	deep into their pasts, a few of them traveling all the way back to scenes of unremembered childhood trauma.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_022.jpg	These journeys had been wrenching, shaking the travelers to their core, but they had been cathartic too. Clearly these medicines—as guides
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_023.jpg	both above- and belowground invariably call the drugs they administer—powerfully stir the psychic pot, surfacing all sorts of repressed
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_024.jpg	material, some of it terrifying and ugly. Did I really want to go there? No!—to be perfectly honest. You should know I have never been one
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_025.jpg	for deep or sustained introspection.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_026.jpg	My usual orientation is more forward than back, or down, and I generally prefer to leave my psychic depths undisturbed, assuming they
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_027.jpg	exist. (There’s quite enough to deal with up here on the surface; maybe that’s why I became a journalist rather than a novelist or poet. All
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_028.jpg	that stuff down there in the psychic basement has been stowed there for a reason, and unless you’re looking for something specific to help
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_029.jpg	solve a problem, why would anyone willingly go down those steps and switch on that light? People generally think of me as a fairly
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_030.jpg	even-keeled and psychologically sturdy person, and I’ve played that role for so long now— in my family as a child, in my family as an
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_031.jpg	adult, with my friends, and with my colleagues—that it’s probably an accurate enough characterization.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_032.jpg	But every so often, perhaps in the wee-hour throes of insomnia or under the influence of cannabis, I have found myself tossed in a psychic
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_033.jpg	storm of existential dread so dark and violent that the keel comes off the boat, capsizing this trusty identity. At such times, I begin seriously
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_034.jpg	to entertain the possibility that somewhere deep beneath the equable presence I present, there exists a shadow me made up of forces
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_035.jpg	roiling, anarchic, and potentially mad.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_036.jpg	Just how thin is the skin of my sanity? There are times when I wonder. Perhaps we all do.
test/images/synth_doc_00152_line_037.jpg	But did I really want to find out?
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_000.jpg	Habits are undeniably useful tools, relieving us of the need to run a complex mental operation every time we’re confronted
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_001.jpg	with a new task or situation. Yet they also relieve us of the need to stay awake to the world: to attend, feel, think, and then
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_002.jpg	act in a deliberate manner. (That is, from freedom rather than compulsion. If you need to be reminded how completely
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_003.jpg	mental habit blinds us to experience, just take a trip to an unfamiliar country. Suddenly you wake up!
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_004.jpg	And the algorithms of everyday life all but start over, as if from scratch. This is why the various travel metaphors for the
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_005.jpg	psychedelic experience are so apt. The efficiencies of the adult mind, useful as they are, blind us to the present moment.
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_006.jpg	We’re constantly jumping ahead to the next thing.
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_007.jpg	We approach experience much as an artificial intelligence (AI) program does, with our brains continually translating the data
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_008.jpg	of the present into the terms of the past, reaching back in time for the relevant experience, and then using that to make its
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_009.jpg	best guess as to how to predict and navigate the future. One of the things that commends travel, art, nature, work, and
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_010.jpg	certain drugs to us is the way these experiences, at their best, block every mentalpath forward and back, immersing us in
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_011.jpg	the flow of a present that is literally wonderful—wonder being the by-product of precisely the kind of unencumbered first
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_012.jpg	sight, or virginal noticing, to which the adult brain has closed itself. (It’s so inefficient! Alas, most of the time I inhabit a
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_013.jpg	near-future tense, my psychic thermostat set to a low simmer of anticipation and, too often, worry. The good thing is I’m
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_014.jpg	seldom surprised.
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_015.jpg	The bad thing is I’m seldom surprised. What I am struggling to describe here is what I think of as my default mode of
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_016.jpg	consciousness. It works well enough, certainly gets the job done, but what if it isn’t the only, or necessarily the best, way to
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_017.jpg	go through life?
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_018.jpg	The premise of psychedelic research is that this special group of molecules can give us access to other modes of
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_019.jpg	consciousness that might offer us specific benefits, whether therapeutic, spiritual, or creative. Psychedelics are certainly not
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_020.jpg	the only door to these other forms of consciousness—and I explore some non-pharmacological alternatives in these
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_021.jpg	pages—but they do seem to be one of the easier knobs to take hold of and turn.
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_022.jpg	The whole idea of expanding our repertoire of conscious states is not an entirely new idea: Hinduism and Buddhism are
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_023.jpg	steeped in it, and there are intriguing precedents even in Western science. William James, the pioneering American
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_024.jpg	psychologist and author of The Varieties of Religious Experience, ventured into these realms more than a century ago. He
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_025.jpg	returned with the conviction that our everyday waking consciousness “is but one special type of consciousness, whilst all
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_026.jpg	about it, parted from it by the filmiest of screens, there lie potential forms of consciousness entirely different.” James is
test/images/synth_doc_00161_line_027.jpg	speaking, I realized, of the unopened door in our minds. For him, the “touch” that could throw open the door and disclose
test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_000.jpg	R. C. Lapate et al., “Awareness of Emotional Stimuli Determines the Behavioral Consequences of Amygdala Activation and
test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_001.jpg	Amygdala-Prefrontal Connectivity,” Scientific Reports 20:6 (2016): 25826; doi:10.1038/srep25826. 21. Benjamin Baird et al., “Domain-Specific
test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_002.jpg	Enhancement of Metacognitive Ability Following Meditation Training,” Journal of Experimental Psychology: General 143:5 (2014): 1972–79;
test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_003.jpg	http://dx.doi.org/10.1037/a0036882.
test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_004.jpg	Both the mindfulness and active control groups took forty-five- minute classes four times a week for two weeks, along with home practice
test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_005.jpg	for fifteen minutes daily. 22.

test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_007.jpg	109–19; doi: 10.3758/cabn.7.2.109.CHAPTER EIGHT. LIGHTNESS OF BEING 1. Marcus Raichle et al., “A Default Mode of Brain Function,”
