test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_036.jpg	CHAPTER NINE. MIND, BODY, AND GENOME 1.
test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_037.jpg	Natalie A. Morone et al., “A Mind-Body Program for Older Adults with Chronic Low Back Pain: A Randomized Trial,” JAMA Internal
test/images/synth_doc_00156_line_038.jpg	Medicine 176:3 (2016): 329–37.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_000.jpg	Therefore, immediately after a particular nerve has fired, the computer has at least one millisecond to decide whether, and when, that nerve
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_001.jpg	should fire again. If it has computed that decision within, say, half a millisecond, no tampering with the brain’s speed is necessary, and the
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_002.jpg	computer merely fires the nerve at the appropriate times. Otherwise, the computer causes the brain slow down (or, if necessary, to stop)
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_003.jpg	until the calculation of what should happen next is complete; it then restores the brain’s normal speed.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_004.jpg	What would this feel like to the user? By definition, like nothing. The user would experience only the environment specified in the
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_005.jpg	program, without any slowing down, stopping or restarting.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_006.jpg	Fortunately it is never necessary for a virtual-reality generator to make the brain operate faster than normal; that would eventually raise
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_007.jpg	problems of principle because, among other things, no signal can travel faster than the speed of light. This method allows us to specify in
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_008.jpg	advance an arbitrarily complicated environment whose simulation requires any finite amount of computation, and to experience that
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_009.jpg	environment at any subjective speed and level of detail that our minds are capable of assimilating.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_010.jpg	If the requisite calculations are too numerous for the computer to perform within the subjectively perceived time, the experience will be
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_011.jpg	unaffected, but the user will pay for its complexity in terms of externally elapsed time. The user might emerge from the virtual-reality
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_012.jpg	generator after what seemed subjectively like a five-minute experience, to find that years had passed in physical reality. A user whose brain
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_013.jpg	is switched off, for however long, and then switched on again will have an uninterrupted experience of some environment. But a user
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_014.jpg	whose brain is switched off for ever has no experiences at all from that moment on. This means that a program which might at some point
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_015.jpg	switch the user’s brain off, and never switch it on again, does not generate an environment for the user to experience and therefore does not
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_016.jpg	qualify as a valid program for a virtual-reality generator.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_017.jpg	But a program which, eventually, always switches the user’s brain back on causes the virtual-reality generator to render some environment.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_018.jpg	Even a program which emits no nerve signals at all renders the dark, silent environment of perfect sensory isolation. In our search for the
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_019.jpg	ultimate in virtual-reality we have strayed a very long way from what is feasible today, or even from what is on any foreseeable
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_020.jpg	technological horizon. So let me stress again that for our present purposes technological obstacles are irrelevant.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_021.jpg	We are not investigating what sorts of virtual-reality generator can be built, or even, necessarily, what sorts of virtual-reality generator will
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_022.jpg	ever be built, by human engineers. We are investigating what the laws of physics do and do not allow in the way of virtual reality.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_023.jpg	The reason why this is important has nothing to do with the prospects for making better virtual-reality generators. It is that the relation
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_024.jpg	ship between virtual reality and ‘ordinary’ reality is part of the deep, unexpected structure of the world, which this book is about.By
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_025.jpg	considering various tricks — nerve stimulation, stopping and starting the brain, and so on — we have managed to envisage a physically
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_026.jpg	possible virtual-reality generator whose repertoire covers the entire sensory range, is fully interactive, and is not constrained by the speed
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_027.jpg	or memory capacity of its computer. Is there anything outside the repertoire of such a virtual-reality generator? Would its repertoire be the
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_028.jpg	set of all logically possible environments?
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_029.jpg	It would not. Even this futuristic machine’s repertoire is drastically circumscribed by the mere fact of its being a physical object.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_030.jpg	It does not even scratch the surface of what is logically possible, as I shall now show. The basic idea of the proof — known as a diagonal
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_031.jpg	argument — predates the idea of virtual reality.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_032.jpg	It was first used by the nineteenth-century mathematician Georg Cantor to prove that there are infinite quantities greater than the infinity
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_033.jpg	of natural number (1, 2, 3…). The same form of proof is at the heart of the modern theory of computation developed by Alan Turing and
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_034.jpg	others in the 1930s. It was also used by Kurt Gödel to prove his celebrated ‘incompleteness theorem’, of which more in Chapter 10. Each
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_035.jpg	environment in our machine’s repertoire is generated by some program for its computer. Imagine the set of all valid programs for this
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_036.jpg	computer.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_037.jpg	From a physical point of view, each such program specifies a particular set of values for physical variables, on the disks or other media,
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_038.jpg	that represent the computer’s program. We know from quantum theory that all such variables are quantized, and therefore that, no matter
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_039.jpg	how the computer works, the set of possible programs is discrete.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_040.jpg	Each program can therefore be expressed as a finite sequence of symbols in a discrete code or computer language. There are infinitely many
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_041.jpg	such programs, but each one can contain only a finite number of symbols.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_042.jpg	That is because symbols are physical objects, made of matter in recognizable configurations, and one could not manufacture an infinite
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_043.jpg	number of them. As I shall explain in Chapter 10, these intuitively obvious physical requirements — that the programs must be quantized,
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_044.jpg	and that each of them must consist of a finite number of symbols and can be executed in a sequence of steps — are more substantive than
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_045.jpg	they seem. They are the only consequences of the laws of physics that are needed as input for the proof, but they are enough to impose
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_046.jpg	drastic restrictions on the repertoire of any physically possible machine. Other physical laws may impose even more restrictions, but they
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_047.jpg	would not affect the conclusions of this chapter.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_048.jpg	Now let us imagine this infinite set of possible programs arranged in an infinitely long list, and numbered Program 1, Program 2, and on.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_049.jpg	They could, for instance, be arranged in ‘alphabetical’ order with respect to the symbols in which they are expressed. Because each program
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_050.jpg	generates an environment, this list can also be regarded as a list of all the environments in the machine’s repertoire; we may call them
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_051.jpg	Environment 1, Environment 2, and so on. It could be that some of the environments are repeated in the list, because two different
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_052.jpg	programs might in effect perform the same calculations, but that will not affect the argument. What is important is that each environment
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_053.jpg	in our machine’s repertoire should appear at least once in the list.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_054.jpg	A simulated environment may be limited or unlimited in apparent physical size and apparent duration. An architect’s simulation of a
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_055.jpg	house, forexample, can be run for an unlimited time, but will probably cover only a limited volume.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_056.jpg	A video game might allow the user only a finite time for play before the game ends, or it might render a game-universe of unlimited size,
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_057.jpg	allow an unlimited amount of exploration and end only when the user deliberately ends it. To make the proof simpler, let us consider only
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_058.jpg	programs that continue to run for ever.
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_059.jpg	That is not much of a restriction, because if a program halts we can always choose to regard its lack of response as being the response of a
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_060.jpg	sensory-isolation environment. Let me define a class of logically possible environments which I shall call Cantgotu environments, partly in
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_061.jpg	honour of Cantor, Gödel and Turing, and partly for a reason I shall explain shortly. They are defined as follows. For the first subjective
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_062.jpg	minute, a Cantgotu environment behaves differently from Environment 1 (generated by Program 1 of our generator).
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_063.jpg	It does not matter how it does behave, so long as it is, to the user, recognizably different from Environment 1. During the second minute it
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_064.jpg	behaves differently from Environment 2 (though it is now allowed to resemble Environment 1 again). During the third minute, it behaves
test/images/synth_doc_00154_line_065.jpg	differently from Environment 3, and so on. Any environment that satisfied these rules I shall call a Cantgotu environment.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_000.jpg	Not for the yogis. Their remarkable meditation skills bespeak what’s technically known as a “state by trait interaction,” suggesting the
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_001.jpg	brain changes that underlie the trait also give rise to special abilities that activate during meditative states—here, a heightened speed of
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_002.jpg	onset, greater intensity, and extended duration. In contemplative science, an “altered state” refers to changes that occur only during
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_003.jpg	meditation. An altered trait indicates that the practice of meditation transformed the brain and biology so that meditation-induced changes
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_004.jpg	are seen before beginning to meditate. So a “state-by-trait” effect refers to temporary state changes that are seen only in those who display
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_005.jpg	enduring altered traits—the long-term meditators and the yogis.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_006.jpg	Several have shown up during the research in Richie’s lab. One example. Recall that the yogis show a pronounced elevation in gamma
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_007.jpg	activity during the open presence and compassion meditations, far greater than inactivity during the open presence and compassion
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_008.jpg	meditations, far greater than in the controls. This elevation in gamma activity was a change from baseline, their everyday levels—marking
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_009.jpg	another state-by-trait effect.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_010.jpg	What’s more, while they rest in “open presence,” the very distinction between a state and a trait blurs: in their tradition, the yogis are
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_011.jpg	explicitly instructed to mingle the state of open presence with their everyday life—to morph the state into a trait. READY FOR ACTION
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_012.jpg	One by one they lay in the scanners, their heads held firmly in place by cumbersome earphones. There was one group of meditation
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_013.jpg	novices, and another of Tibetan and Western yogis (lifetime average 34,000 hours); each one had his or her (yes, there were female yogis)
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_014.jpg	brain scanned while doing a compassion practice.4 The specific method they deployed was described by Matthieu Ricard, who
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_015.jpg	collaborated on the study, as follows. First bring to mind someone you care about deeply and relish the feeling of compassion toward that
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_016.jpg	person—and then hold that same loving-kindness toward all beings, without thinking of anyone in particular.5 During the session of
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_017.jpg	loving-kindness each person heard at random a series of sounds, some happy, like a baby laughing; others neutral, like background sounds
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_018.jpg	in a café, or still others, sounds of human suffering (like screams, as in the studies in chapter six).
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_019.jpg	Just as in previous studies of empathy and the brain, for everyone the neural circuitry for tuning in to distress activated more strongly
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_020.jpg	during compassion meditation than when those vocal signals of suffering came while the person was at rest. Significantly, this brain
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_021.jpg	response for sharing another person’s feelings was greater in the yogis compared to beginners. In addition, their expertise in compassion
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_022.jpg	practice also upped action in circuitry typically involved while we sense another person’s mental state or take their perspective. Finally,
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_023.jpg	there was a boost in brain areas, especially the amygdala, key for what’s salient; we feel another person’s distress is of compelling
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_024.jpg	importance and pay more attention. Tellingly, the yogis but not the beginners showed the final part of the brain’s arc to action, a jump in
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_025.jpg	activity in the motor centers that guide the body when we are ready to move—to take some decisive action to help, even though theare
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_026.jpg	ready to move—to take some decisive action to help, even though the subjects were lying still in a scanner.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_027.jpg	The yogis showed a huge boost in these circuits. The involvement of neural regions for action, particularly the premotor cortex, seems
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_028.jpg	striking: to emotional resonance with a person’s suffering it adds the readiness to help. The yogi’s neural profile during compassion seems
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_029.jpg	to reflect an endpoint of the path of change.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_030.jpg	For people who have never meditated before, absolute beginners, the pattern does not show up during their meditation on compassion —it
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_031.jpg	takes a bit of practice. There’s a dose response here: this pattern shows up a bit in beginners, more in people who have put in more lifetime
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_032.jpg	hours of meditation, and to the greatest extent in the yogis. Intriguingly, yogis hearing sounds of people in distress while they were doing
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_033.jpg	loving-kindness meditation showed less activity than others do in their postcingulate cortex (PCC), a key area for self-focused thought.6 In
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_034.jpg	the yogis, hearing sounds of suffering seems to prime a focus on others.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_035.jpg	They also show a stronger connection between the PCC and the prefrontal cortex, an overall pattern suggesting a “down-regulation” of the
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_036.jpg	“what will happen to me?” self-concern that can dampen compassionate action.7 Some of the yogis later explained that their training
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_037.jpg	imbued them with preparedness for action, so the moment they encounter suffering they are predisposed to act without hesitation to help
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_038.jpg	the person. This preparedness, along with their willingness to engage with someone’s suffering, counters the normal tendency to withdraw,
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_039.jpg	to back away from a person in distress.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_040.jpg	That seems to embody the advice of Tibetan meditation master (and Matthieu’s main teacher) Dilgo Khyentse Rinpoche to yogis such as
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_041.jpg	these: “Develop a complete acceptance and openness to all situations and emotions, and to all people, experiencing everything totally
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_042.jpg	without mental reservations and blockages. . . .”8 PRESENCE TO PAIN An eighteenth-century Tibetan text urges meditators to practice
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_043.jpg	“on whatever harms come your way,” adding, “When sick, practice on that sickness. . . . Whencold, practice on that coldness. By practicing
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_044.jpg	in this way all situations will arise as meditation.”9 Mingyur Rinpoche, likewise, encourages making all sensation, even pain, our “friend,”
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_045.jpg	using it as a basis for meditation.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_046.jpg	Since the essence of meditation is awareness, any sensation that anchors attention can be used as support—and pain particularly can be
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_047.jpg	very effective in focusing. Treating it as a friend “softens and warms” our relationship, as he puts it, as we gradually learn to accept the
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_048.jpg	pain rather than try to get rid of it. With that advice in mind, consider what happened when Richie’s group used the thermal stimulator to
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_049.jpg	create intense pain in the yogis.
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_050.jpg	Each yogi (including Mingyur) was compared to a meditation-naive volunteer matched for age and gender. For a week before they came to
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_051.jpg	be studied, these volunteers learned to generate an “open presence,” an attentional stance of letting whatever life presents us come and go,
test/images/synth_doc_00155_line_052.jpg	without adding thoughts or emotional reactions.
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_000.jpg	No more mushrooms for you!’ “‘Were you drinking or on drugs?’ is what our culture says when you have a powerful
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_001.jpg	experience.” Yet even a moment’s reflection tells you that attributing the content of the psychedelic experience to “drugs”
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_002.jpg	explains virtually nothing about it. The images and the narratives and the insights don’t come from nowhere, and they
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_003.jpg	certainly don’t come from a chemical. They come from inside our minds,* and at the very least have something to tell us
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_004.jpg	about that.
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_005.jpg	If dreams and fantasies and free associations are worth interpreting, then surely so is the more vivid and detailed material
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_006.jpg	with which the psychedelic journey presents us. It opens a new door on one’s mind.
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_007.jpg	And about that my psychedelic journeys have taught me a great many interesting things. Many of these were the kinds of
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_008.jpg	things one might learn in the course of psychotherapy: insights into important relationships; the outlines of fears and desires
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_009.jpg	ordinarily kept out of view; repressed memories and emotions; and, perhaps most interesting and useful, a new perspective
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_010.jpg	on how one’s mind works. This, I think, is the great value of exploring non-ordinary states of consciousness: the light they
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_011.jpg	reflect back on the ordinary ones, which no longer seem quite so transparent or so ordinary.
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_012.jpg	To realize, as William James concluded, that normal waking consciousness is but one of many potential forms of
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_013.jpg	consciousness—ways of perceiving or constructing the world—separated from it by merely “the filmiest of screens,” is to
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_014.jpg	recognize that our account of reality, whether inward or outward, is incomplete at best. Normal waking consciousness might
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_015.jpg	seem to offer a faithful map to the territory of reality, and it is good for many things, but it is only a map—and not the only
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_016.jpg	map. As to why these other modes of consciousness exist, we can only speculate.
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_017.jpg	Most of the time, it is normal waking consciousness that best serves the interests of survival—and is most adaptive. But
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_018.jpg	there are moments in the life of an individual or acommunity when the imaginative novelties proposed by altered states of
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_019.jpg	consciousness introduce exactly the sort of variation that can send a life, or a culture, down a new path. For me, the
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_020.jpg	moment I recognized the tenuousness and relativity of my own default consciousness came that afternoon on Fritz’s
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_021.jpg	mountaintop, when he taught me how to enter a trance state by means of nothing more than a pattern of rapid breathing
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_022.jpg	and the sounds of rhythmic drumming. Where in the world has that been all my life? This is nothing Freud or any number of
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_023.jpg	psychologists and behavioral economists haven’t told us, but the idea that “normal” consciousness is but the tip of a large
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_024.jpg	and largely uncharted psychic iceberg is now for me something more than a theory; the hidden vastness of the mind is a
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_025.jpg	felt reality.
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_026.jpg	I don’t mean to suggest I have achieved this state of ego-transcending awareness, only tasted it. These experiences don’t
test/images/synth_doc_00162_line_027.jpg	last, or at least they didn’t for me. After each of my psychedelic sessions came a period of several weeks in which I felt
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_000.jpg	I felt as though I were communing directly with a plant for the first time and that certain ideas I had long thought about and written
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_001.jpg	about— having to do with the subjectivity of other species and the way they act upon us in ways we’re too self-regarding to
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_002.jpg	appreciate—had taken on the flesh of feeling and reality. I looked through the negative spaces formed by the hydrangea leaves to fix my
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_003.jpg	gaze on the swamp maple in the middle of the meadow beyond, and it too was now more alive than I’d everknown a tree to be, infused
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_004.jpg	with some kind of spirit—this one, too, benevolent. The idea that there had ever been a disagreement between matter and spirit seemed
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_005.jpg	risible, and I felt as though whatever it is that usually divides me from the world out there had begun to fall away. Not completely: the
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_006.jpg	battlements of ego had not fallen; this was not what the researchers would deem a “complete” mystical experience, because I retained the
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_007.jpg	sense of an observing I.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_008.jpg	But the doors and windows of perception had opened wide, and they were admitting more of the world and its myriad nonhuman
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_009.jpg	personalities than ever before. Buoyed by this development, I sat up now and looked out over my desk, through the big window that faced
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_010.jpg	back to the house. When I sited the building, I carefully framed the main view between two very old and venerable trees, a stolidly vertical
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_011.jpg	ash on the right and an elegantly angled and intricately branched white oak on the left. The ash has seen better days; storms have shorn
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_012.jpg	several important limbs from it, wrecking its symmetry and leaving some ragged stumps. The oak was somewhat healthier, in full leaf now
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_013.jpg	with its upturned limbs reaching into the sky like the limbs of a dancer.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_014.jpg	But the main trunk, which had always leaned precariously to one side, now concerned me: a section of it had rotted out at ground level,
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_015.jpg	and for the first time it was possible to look clear through it and see daylight. How was it possibly still standing? As I gazed at the two trees
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_016.jpg	I had gazed at so many times before from my desk, it suddenly dawned on me that these trees were—obviously!— my parents: the stolid
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_017.jpg	ash my father, the elegant oak my mother. I don’t know exactly what I mean by that, except that thinking about those trees became identical
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_018.jpg	to thinking about my parents. They were completely, indelibly, present in those trees.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_019.jpg	And so I thought about all they had given me, and about all that time had done to them, and what was going to become of this prospect,
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_020.jpg	this place (this me!), when they finally fell, as eventually they would. That parents die is not exactly the stuff of epiphany, but the prospect,
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_021.jpg	no longer distant or abstract, pierced me more deeply than it ever had, and I was disarmed yet again by the pervasive sense of poignancy
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_022.jpg	that trailed me all that afternoon.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_023.jpg	Yet I must have still had some wits about me, because I made a note to call the arborist tomorrow; maybe something could be done to
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_024.jpg	reduce the weight on the leaning side of the oak, in order to prevent it from falling, if only for a while longer.My walk back to the house
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_025.jpg	was, I think, the peak of the experience and comes back to me now in the colors and tones of a dream. There was, again, the sense of
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_026.jpg	pushing my body through a mass of air that had been sweetened by phlox and was teeming, almost frenetic, with activity. The dragonflies,
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_027.jpg	big as birds, were now out in force, touching down just long enough to kiss the phlox blossoms and then lift off, before madly crisscrossing
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_028.jpg	the garden path. These were more dragonflies than I had ever seen in one place, so many in fact that I wasn’t completely sure if they were
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_029.jpg	real.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_030.jpg	(Judith later confirmed the sighting when I got her to come outside. And as they executed their flight patterns, they left behind them
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_031.jpg	contrails that persisted in the air, or so at least it appeared. Dusk now approaching, the air traffic in the garden had built to a riotous
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_032.jpg	crescendo: the pollinators making their last rounds of the day, the plants still signifying to them with their flowers: me, me, me!
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_033.jpg	In one way I knew this scene well—the garden coming briefly back to life after the heat of a summer day has relented—but never had I felt
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_034.jpg	so integral to it. I was no longer the alienated human observer, gazing at the garden from a distance, whether literal or figural, but rather
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_035.jpg	felt part and parcel of all that was transpiring here. So the flowers were addressing me as much as the pollinators, and perhaps because the
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_036.jpg	very air that afternoon was such a felt presence, one’s usual sense of oneself as a subject observing objects in space—objects that have been
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_037.jpg	thrown into relief and rendered discrete by the apparent void that surrounds them—gave way to a sense of being deep inside and fully
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_038.jpg	implicated in this scene, one more being in relation to the myriad other beings and to the whole. “Everything is interaction and reciprocal,”
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_039.jpg	wrote Humboldt, and that felt very much the case, and so, for the first time I can remember, did this: “I myself am identical with nature.” •
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_040.jpg	• • I HONESTLY DON’T KNOW what to make of this experience. In a certain light at certain moments, I feel as though I had had some
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_041.jpg	kind of spiritual experience. I had felt the personhood of other beings in a way I hadn’t before; whatever it is that keeps us from feeling our
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_042.jpg	full implication in nature had been temporarily in abeyance.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_043.jpg	There had also been, I felt, anopening of the heart, toward my parents, yes, and toward Judith, but also, weirdly, toward some of the plants
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_044.jpg	and trees and birds and even the damn bugs on our property. Some of this openness has persisted.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_045.jpg	I think back on it now as an experience of wonder and immanence. The fact that this transformation of my familiar world into something I
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_046.jpg	can only describe as numinous was occasioned by the eating of a little brown mushroom that Stamets and I had found growing on the edge
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_047.jpg	of a parking lot in a state park on the Pacific coast—well, that fact can be viewed in one of two ways: either as an additional wonder or as
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_048.jpg	support for a more prosaic and materialist interpretation of what happened to me that August afternoon. According to one interpretation, I
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_049.jpg	had had “a drug experience,” plain and simple.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_050.jpg	It was a kind of waking dream, interesting and pleasurable but signifying nothing. The psilocin in that mushroom unlocked the
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_051.jpg	5-hydroxytryptamine 2-A receptors in my brain, causing them to fire wildly and set off a cascade of disordered mental events that, among
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_052.jpg	other things, permitted some thoughts and feelings, presumably from my subconscious (and, perhaps, my reading too), to get cross-wired
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_053.jpg	with my visual cortex as it was processing images of the trees and plants and insects in my field of vision.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_054.jpg	Not quite a hallucination, “projection” is probably the psychological term for this phenomenon: when we mix our emotions with certain
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_055.jpg	objects that then reflect those feelings back to us so that they appear to glisten with meaning. T.
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_056.jpg	S. Eliot called these things and situations the “objective correlatives” of human emotion. Emerson had a similar phenomenon in mind when
test/images/synth_doc_00157_line_057.jpg	he said that “Nature always wears the colors of the spirit,” suggesting it is our minds that dress her in such significance.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_000.jpg	Serious research into psychedelics had been more or less purged from the university fifty years ago, soon after Timothy Leary’s Harvard
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_001.jpg	Psilocybin Project crashed andburned in 1963. Not even Berkeley, it seemed, was ready to go there again, at least not yet. Third data point:
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_002.jpg	The dinner table conversation jogged a vague memory that a few years before somebody had e-mailed me a scientific paper about
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_003.jpg	psilocybin research. Busy with other things at the time, I hadn’t even opened it, but a quick search of the term “psilocybin” instantly fished
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_004.jpg	the paper out of the virtual pile of discarded e-mail on my computer.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_005.jpg	The paper had been sent to me by one of its co-authors, a man I didn’t know by the name of Bob Jesse; perhaps he had read something I’d
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_006.jpg	written about psychoactive plants and thought I might be interested. The article, which was written by the same team at Hopkins that was
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_007.jpg	giving psilocybin to cancer patients, had just been published in the journal Psychopharmacology. For a peer-reviewed scientific paper, it
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_008.jpg	had a most unusual title: “Psilocybin Can Occasion Mystical-Type Experiences Having Substantial and Sustained Personal Meaning and
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_009.jpg	Spiritual Significance.” Never mind the word “psilocybin”; it was the words “mystical” and “spiritual” and “meaning” that leaped out
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_010.jpg	from the pages of a pharmacology journal.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_011.jpg	The title hinted at an intriguing frontier of research, one that seemed to straddle two worlds we’ve grown accustomed to think are
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_012.jpg	irreconcilable: science and spirituality. Now I fell on the Hopkins paper, fascinated. Thirty volunteers who had never before used
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_013.jpg	psychedelics had been given a pill containing either a synthetic version of psilocybin or an “active placebo”— methylphenidate, or
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_014.jpg	Ritalin—to fool them into thinking they had received the psychedelic. They then lay down on a couch wearing eyeshades and listening to
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_015.jpg	music through headphones, attended the whole time by two therapists.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_016.jpg	(The eyeshades and headphones encourage a more inward- focused journey. After about thirty minutes, extraordinary things began to
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_017.jpg	happen in the minds of the people who had gotten the psilocybin pill. The study demonstrated that a high dose of psilocybin could be used
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_018.jpg	to safely and reliably “occasion” a mystical experience—typically described as the dissolution of one’s ego followed by a sense of merging
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_019.jpg	with nature or the universe. This might not come as news to people who take psychedelic drugs or to the researchers who first studied
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_020.jpg	them back in the 1950s and 1960s. But it wasn’t at all obvious to modern science, or to me, in 2006, when the paper was published.What was
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_021.jpg	most remarkable about the results reported in the article is that participants ranked their psilocybin experience as one of the most
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_022.jpg	meaningful in their lives, comparable “to the birth of a first child or death of a parent.” Two-thirds of the participants rated the session
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_023.jpg	among the top five “most spiritually significant experiences” of their lives; one-third ranked it the most significant such experience in their
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_024.jpg	lives.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_025.jpg	Fourteen months later, these ratings had slipped only slightly. The volunteers reported significant improvements in their “personal
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_026.jpg	well-being, life satisfaction and positive behavior change,” changes that were confirmed by their family members and friends. Though no
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_027.jpg	one knew it at the time, the renaissance of psychedelic research now under way began in earnest with the publication of that paper.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_028.jpg	It led directly to a series of trials—at Hopkins and several other universities—using psilocybin to treat a variety of indications, including
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_029.jpg	anxiety and depression in cancer patients, addiction to nicotine and alcohol, obsessive-compulsive disorder, depression, and eating
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_030.jpg	disorders. What is striking about this whole line of clinical research is the premise that it is not the pharmacological effect of the drug itself
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_031.jpg	but the kind of mental experience it occasions—involving the temporary dissolution of one’s ego—that may be the key to changing one’s
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_032.jpg	mind. • • • AS SOMEONE not at all sure he has ever had a single “spiritually significant” experience, much less enough of them to make a
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_033.jpg	ranking, I found that the 2006 paper piqued my curiosity but also my skepticism.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_034.jpg	Many of the volunteers described being given access to an alternative reality, a “beyond” where the usual physical laws don’t apply and
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_035.jpg	various manifestations of cosmic consciousness or divinity present themselves as unmistakably real. All this I found both a little hard to
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_036.jpg	take (couldn’t this be just a drug- induced hallucination?) and yet at the same time intriguing; part of me wanted it to be true, whatever
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_037.jpg	exactly “it” was. This surprised me, because I have never thought of myself as a particularly spiritual, much less mystical, person. This is
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_038.jpg	partly a function of worldview, I suppose, and partly of neglect: I’ve never devoted much time to exploring spiritualpaths and did not have
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_039.jpg	a religious upbringing. My default perspective is that of the philosophical materialist, who believes that matter is the fundamental
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_040.jpg	substance of the world and the physical laws it obeys should be able to explain everything that happens.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_041.jpg	I start from the assumption that nature is all that there is and gravitate toward scientific explanations of phenomena. That said, I’m also
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_042.jpg	sensitive to the limitations of the scientific-materialist perspective and believe that nature (including the human mind) still holds deep
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_043.jpg	mysteries toward which science can sometimes seem arrogant and unjustifiably dismissive.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_044.jpg	Was it possible that a single psychedelic experience—something that turned on nothing more than the ingestion of a pill or square of blotter
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_045.jpg	paper—could put a big dent in such a worldview? Shift how one thought about mortality?
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_046.jpg	Actually change one’s mind in enduring ways? The idea took hold of me. It was a little like being shown a door in a familiar room—the
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_047.jpg	room of your own mind—that you had somehow never noticed before and being told by people you trusted (scientists!) that a whole other
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_048.jpg	way of thinking—of being!—lay waiting on the other side. All you had to do was turn the knob and enter. Who wouldn’t be curious?
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_049.jpg	I might not have been looking to change my life, but the idea of learning something new about it, and of shining a fresh light on this old
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_050.jpg	world, began to occupy my thoughts. Maybe there was something missing from my life, something I just hadn’t named. Now, I already
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_051.jpg	knew something about such doors, having written about psychoactive plants earlier in my career. In The Botany of Desire, I explored at
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_052.jpg	some length what I had been surprised to discover is a universal human desire to change consciousness.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_053.jpg	There is not a culture on earth (well, one*) that doesn’t make use of certain plants to change the contents of the mind, whether as a matter of
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_054.jpg	healing, habit, or spiritual practice. That such a curious and seemingly maladaptive desire should exist alongside our desires for
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_055.jpg	nourishment and beauty and sex—all of which make much more obvious evolutionary sense—cried out for an explanation. The simplest
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_056.jpg	was that these substances help relieve pain and boredom. Yet the powerful feelings and elaborate taboos and rituals that surround many of
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_057.jpg	these psychoactive species suggest there must be something more to it.
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_058.jpg	For our species, I learned, plants and fungi with the power to radically alter consciousness have long and widely been used as tools for
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_059.jpg	healingthe mind, for facilitating rites of passage, and for serving as a medium for communicating with supernatural realms, or spirit
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_060.jpg	worlds. These uses were ancient and venerable in a great many cultures, but I ventured one other application: to enrich the collective
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_061.jpg	imagination—the culture—with the novel ideas and visions that a select few people bring back from wherever it is they go. • • • NOW
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_062.jpg	THAT I HAD DEVELOPED an intellectual appreciation for the potential value of these psychoactive substances, you might think I would
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_063.jpg	have been more eager to try them. I’m not sure what I was waiting for: courage, maybe, or the right opportunity, which a busy life lived
test/images/synth_doc_00158_line_064.jpg	mainly on the right side of the law never quite seemed to afford.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_000.jpg	Many people go to psychotherapists for just such problems. Dan did not.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_001.jpg	But years later he was diagnosed with that adrenal disorder, the cause of his long- standing high blood pressure. One of those adrenal
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_002.jpg	symptoms: elevated levels of cortisol, the stress hormone that triggers feelings of anxiety.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_003.jpg	Along with his years of meditation, a drug that adjusts that adrenal problem seemed also to handle the cortisol—and the anxiety. IN A
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_004.jpg	NUTSHELL Although meditation was not originally intended to treat psychological problems, in modern times it has shown promise in
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_005.jpg	the treatment of some, particularly depression and anxiety disorders. In a meta-analysis of forty-seven studies on the application of
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_006.jpg	meditation methods to treat patients with mental health problems, the findings show that meditation can lead to decreases in depression
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_007.jpg	(particularly severe depression), anxiety, and pain—about as much as medications but with no side effects. Meditation also can, to a lesser
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_008.jpg	degree, reduce the toll of psychological stress. Loving-kindness meditation may be particularly helpful to patients suffering from trauma,
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_009.jpg	especially those with PTSD.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_010.jpg	The melding of mindfulness with cognitive therapy, or MBCT, has become the most empirically well-validated psychological treatment
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_011.jpg	with a meditation basis. This integration continues to have a wide impact in the clinical world, with empirical tests of applications to an
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_012.jpg	ever larger range of psychological disorders under way. While there are occasional reports of negative effects of meditation, the findings to
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_013.jpg	date underscore the potential promise of meditation- based strategies, and the enormous increase in scientific research in these areas bodes
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_014.jpg	well for the future.11 A Yogi’s Brain I n the steep hills above the ridge-hugging Himalayan village of McLeod Ganj, you might stumble on a
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_015.jpg	small hut or remote cave housing a Tibetan yogi on a long-term, solo retreat.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_016.jpg	In the spring of 1992, an intrepid team of scientists, Richie and Cliff Saron among them, traveled to those huts and caves to assess the brain
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_017.jpg	activity of the yogi within each. A three-day journey had brought them to McLeod Ganj, the hill station in the foothills of the Himalayas
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_018.jpg	that is home to the Dalai Lama and the Tibetan Government-in-Exile. There the scientists set up shop in a guesthouse owned by a brother of
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_019.jpg	the Dalai Lama, who resides nearby. Several rooms were given over to unpacking and assembling the equipment for deployment in
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_020.jpg	backpacks for transport up to the mountain hermitages.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_021.jpg	In those days such brain measurements required a mélange of EEG electrodes and amplifiers, computer monitors, video recording
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_022.jpg	equipment, batteries, and generators. That equipment, much larger than today’s, weighed several hundred pounds. Traveling with those
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_023.jpg	instruments in their hard protective cases, the researchers resembled a nerdy rock band. There were no roads to follow; yogis on retreat
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_024.jpg	choose the most remote place they can find. And so, with great effort, and the help of several porters, the scientists lugged their measuring
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_025.jpg	instruments to the yogis.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_026.jpg	The Dalai Lama himself had identified these yogis as masters in lojong, a systematic mind training method; in his view these were ideal
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_027.jpg	subjects for study. The Dalai Lama had written a letter urging the yogis to cooperate, and even sent along a personal emissary, a monk from
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_028.jpg	his private office, to vouch for the top- level request that they participate.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_029.jpg	Arriving at a yogi’s hermitage, the scientists presented the letter and through a translator asked to monitor the yogi’s brain while he
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_030.jpg	meditated. The same answer came from each yogi in turn: No.The same answer came from each yogi in turn: No. To be sure, they all were
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_031.jpg	exceptionally friendly and warm.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_032.jpg	Some offered to teach the scientists the very practices they wanted to measure. A few said they would think about it. But none would go
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_033.jpg	ahead then and there. Some may have heard about another yogi who once had been persuaded by a similar letter from the Dalai Lama to
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_034.jpg	leave his retreat and travel to a university in faraway America to demonstrate his ability to raise his core body temperature at will.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_035.jpg	That yogi had died soon after his return, and rumors on the mountainside held that the experiment had played a role. For most of these
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_036.jpg	yogis, science was quite foreign; none had much inkling of the role of science in modern Western culture. Moreover, of the eight yogis the
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_037.jpg	team met on this expedition, only one had ever seen an actual computer before Richie and the team arrived. A few of the yogis made the
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_038.jpg	canny argument that they had no idea what, exactly, the strange machines measured. If the measurements were irrelevant to what they
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_039.jpg	were doing, or if their brain failed to meet some scientific expectation, it might look to some as though their methods were of no use.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_040.jpg	That, they said, might discourage those on the same path. Whatever the reasons, the net result of this scientific expedition was a resounding
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_041.jpg	nothing. Despite the failure to get cooperation, let alone data, and though futile in the short term, the exercise proved instructive, beginning
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_042.jpg	a steep learning curve.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_043.jpg	For starters, better to bring the meditators to the equipment, especially in a well- fortified brain lab—if they would come. For another,
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_044.jpg	research on such adepts confronts unique challenges beyond their rarity, their intentional remoteness, and their unfamiliarity with or
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_045.jpg	disinterest in scientific endeavors. While their mastery at this inner expertise seems akin to world-class rankings in sports, in this “sport,”
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_046.jpg	the better you get the less you care about your ranking—let alone social status, riches, or fame. That list of indifferences includes any
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_047.jpg	personal pride you might take in what scientific measures show about your inner accomplishments.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_048.jpg	What mattered to them was how the results might influence others for better or worse. Prospects for scientific studies were dim. A
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_049.jpg	SCIENTIST AND A MONKEnter Matthieu Ricard, whose degree in molecular genetics from France’s Pasteur Institute had been under the
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_050.jpg	tutelage of François Jacob, who later won the Nobel Prize in Medicine.1 As a postdoc Matthieu abandoned his promising career in biology
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_051.jpg	to become a monk; over the decades since, he has lived in retreat centers, monasteries, and hermitages. Matthieu was an old friend of ours;
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_052.jpg	he had often participated (as had we) in dialogues (organized by the Mind and Life Institute) between the Dalai Lama and various groups
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_053.jpg	of scientists, where Matthieu voiced the Buddhist viewpoint on whatever topic was at hand.2 You might recall that during the “destructive
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_054.jpg	emotions” dialogue, the Dalai Lama exhorted Richie to test meditation rigorously and extract what was of value for the benefit of the larger
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_055.jpg	world. The Dalai Lama’s call to action touched Matthieu as strongly as it did Richie, stirring in this monk’s mind (to his surprise) a
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_056.jpg	long-unused expertise in the scientific method.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_057.jpg	Matthieu himself was the first monk to come for study at Richie’s lab, spending several days as experimental subject and as collaborator on
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_058.jpg	methods to refine the protocol used with a succession of other yogis. Matthieu Ricard was a coauthor on the main journal article reporting
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_059.jpg	initial findings with yogis.3 For much of the time Matthieu had spent as a monk in Nepal and Bhutan, he was the personal attendant to
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_060.jpg	Dilgo Khyentse Rinpoche, one of the last century’s most universally revered Tibetan meditation masters.4 Many, many lamas of note
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_061.jpg	among those living in exile from Tibet—including the Dalai Lama—had sought out Dilgo Khyentse for private instruction. This put
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_062.jpg	Matthieu at the heart of a large network within the Tibetan meditative world.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_063.jpg	He knew whom to suggest as potential subjects of study—and, perhaps most important, was trusted by those very meditation experts.
test/images/synth_doc_00159_line_064.jpg	Matthieu’s participation made all the difference in recruiting those elusive adepts.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_000.jpg	A handful of headlines from the period suggests the mood: “LSD-Use Charged with Killing Teacher”; “Sampled LSD, Youth Plunges from
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_001.jpg	Viaduct”; “LSD Use Near Epidemic in California”; “Six Students Blinded on LSD Trip in Sun”; “Girl, 5, Eats LSD and Goes Wild”; “Thrill
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_002.jpg	Drug Warps Mind, Kills”; and “A Monster in Our Midst—a Drug Called LSD.” Even Life magazine, which had helped ignite public
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_003.jpg	interest in psychedelics just nine years before with R. Gordon Wasson’s enthusiastic article on psilocybin, joined the chorus of
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_004.jpg	condemnation, publishing a feverish cover story titled “LSD: The Exploding Threat of the Mind Drug That Got out of Control.” Never
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_005.jpg	mind that the magazine’s publisher and his wife had recently had several positive LSD experiences themselves (under the guidance of
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_006.jpg	Sidney Cohen); now the kids were doing it, and it had gotten “out of control.” With pictures of crazed people cowering in corners, the story
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_007.jpg	warned that “an LSD trip is not always a round trip”but rather could be “a one-way trip to an asylum, a prison or a grave.”* As Clare
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_008.jpg	Boothe Luce wrote to Sidney Cohen in 1965, “LSD has been your Frankenstein monster.” • • • OTHER POWERFUL DRUGS subject to
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_009.jpg	abuse, such as the opiates, have managed to maintain a separate identity as a legitimate tool of medicine. Why not psychedelics? The story
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_010.jpg	of Timothy Leary, the most famous psychedelic researcher, made it difficult to argue that a bright line between the scientific and the
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_011.jpg	recreational use of psychedelics could be drawn and patrolled.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_012.jpg	The man had deliberately—indeed gleefully—erased all such lines. But the “personality” of the drug may have as much to do with the
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_013.jpg	collapse of such distinctions as the personalities of people like Timothy Leary or the flaws in their research. What doomed the first wave of
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_014.jpg	psychedelic research was an irrational exuberance about its potential that was nourished by the drugs themselves—that, and the fact that
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_015.jpg	these chemicals are what today we would call disruptive technologies.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_016.jpg	For people working with these powerful molecules, it was impossible not to conclude that—like that divinity student running down
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_017.jpg	Commonwealth Avenue—you were suddenly in possession of news with the power to change not just individuals but the world. To
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_018.jpg	confine these drugs to the laboratory, or to use them only for the benefit of the sick, became hard to justify, when they could do so much for
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_019.jpg	everyone, including the researchers themselves!
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_020.jpg	Leary might have made his more straitlaced colleagues cringe at his lack of caution, yet most of them shared his exuberance and had come
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_021.jpg	to more or less the same conclusions about the potential of psychedelics; they were just more judicious when speaking about them in
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_022.jpg	public. Who among the first generation of psychedelic researchers would dispute a word of this classic gust of Leary exuberance, circa
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_023.jpg	1963: “Make no mistake: the effect of consciousness-expanding drugs will be to transform our concepts of human nature, of human
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_024.jpg	potentialities, of existence. The game is about to be changed, ladies and gentlemen. Man is about to make use of that fabulous electrical
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_025.jpg	network he carries around inhis skull.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_026.jpg	Present social establishments had better be prepared for the change. Our favorite concepts are standing in the way of a floodtide, two
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_027.jpg	billion years building up.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_028.jpg	The verbal dam is collapsing. Head for the hills, or prepare your intellectual craft to flow with the current.”* So perhaps Leary’s real sin
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_029.jpg	was to have the courage of his convictions —his and everyone else’s in the psychedelic research community. It’s often said that a political
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_030.jpg	scandal is what happens when someone in power inadvertently speaks the truth.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_031.jpg	Leary was all too often willing to say out loud to anyone in earshot what everyone else believed but knew better than to speak or write
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_032.jpg	about candidly. It was one thing to use these drugs to treat the ill and maladjusted—society will indulge any effort to help the wayward
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_033.jpg	individual conform to its norms—but it is quite another to use them to treat society itself as if it were sick and to turn the ostensibly healthy
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_034.jpg	into wayward individuals. The fact is that whether by their very nature or the way that first generation of researchers happened to
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_035.jpg	construct the experience, psychedelics introduced something deeply subversive to the West that the various establishments had little choice
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_036.jpg	but to repulse.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_037.jpg	LSD truly was an acid, dissolving almost everything with which it came into contact, beginning with the hierarchies of the mind (the
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_038.jpg	superego, ego, and unconscious) and going on from there to society’s various structures of authority and then to lines of every imaginable
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_039.jpg	kind: between patient and therapist, research and recreation, sickness and health, self and other, subject and object, the spiritual and the
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_040.jpg	material. If all such lines are manifestations of the Apollonian strain in Western civilization, the impulse that erects distinctions, dualities,
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_041.jpg	and hierarchies and defends them, then psychedelics represented the ungovernable Dionysian force that blithely washes all those lines
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_042.jpg	away. But it surely is not the case that the forces unleashed by these chemicals are necessarily ungovernable. Even the most powerful acids
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_043.jpg	can be carefully handled and put to use as tools for accomplishing important things.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_044.jpg	What is the story of the first-wave researchers if not a story about searching for an appropriate container for these powerful chemicals?
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_045.jpg	They tested several different possibilities: the psychotomimetic, the psycholytic, the psychedelic, and, still later, the entheogenic. None
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_046.jpg	were perfect, but each represented a different way to regulate the power of these compounds, by proposing a set of protocolsfor their use as
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_047.jpg	well as a theoretical framework. Where Leary and the counterculture ultimately parted ways with the first generation of researchers was in
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_048.jpg	deciding that no such container—whether medical, religious, or scientific—was needed and that an unguided, do-it-yourself approach to
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_049.jpg	psychedelics was just fine.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_050.jpg	This is risky, as it turns out, and probably a mistake. But how would we ever have discovered this, without experimenting? Before 1943,
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_051.jpg	our society had never had such powerful mind-changing drugs available to it. Other societies have had long and productive experience
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_052.jpg	with psychedelics, and their examples might have saved us a lot of trouble had we only known and paid attention. The fact that we regard
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_053.jpg	many of these societies as “backward” probably kept us from learning from them.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_054.jpg	But the biggest thing we might have learned is that these powerful medicines can be dangerous—both to the individual and to the
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_055.jpg	society—when they don’t have a sturdy social container: a steadying set of rituals and rules— protocols—governing their use, and the
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_056.jpg	crucial involvement of a guide, the figure that is usually called a shaman. Psychedelic therapy—the Hubbard method—was groping
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_057.jpg	toward a Westernized version of this ideal, and it remains the closest thing we have to such a protocol. For young Americans in the 1960s,
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_058.jpg	for whom the psychedelic experience was new in every way, the whole idea of involving elders was probably never going to fly.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_059.jpg	But this is, I think, the great lesson of the 1960s experiment with psychedelics: the importance of finding the proper context, or container,
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_060.jpg	for these powerful chemicals and experiences. Speaking of lines, psychedelics in the 1960s did draw at least one of them, and it has
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_061.jpg	probably never before been quite so sharp or bright: the line, I mean, between generations. Saying exactly how or what psychedelics
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_062.jpg	contributed to the counterculture of the 1960s is not an easy task, there were so many other forces at work. With or without psychedelics,
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_063.jpg	there probably would have been a counterculture; the Vietnam War and the draft made it more than likely.
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_064.jpg	But the forms the counterculture took and its distinctive styles—of music, art, writing, design, and social relations—would surely have
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_065.jpg	been completely different were it not for these chemicals. Psychedelics also contributed to what Todd Gitlin has called the “as if” mood of
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_066.jpg	1960s politics—the sense that everything now was up for grabs, that nothing given was inviolate, andthat it might actually be possible to
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_067.jpg	erase history (there was that acid again) and start the world over again from scratch. But to the extent that the upheaval of the 1960s was the
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_068.jpg	result of an unusually sharp break between generations, psychedelics deserve much of the blame—or credit—for creating this
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_069.jpg	unprecedented “generation gap.” For at what other time in history did a society’s young undergo a searing rite of passage with which the
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_070.jpg	previous generation was utterly unfamiliar? Normally, rites of passage help knit societies together as the young cross over hurdles and
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_071.jpg	through gates erected and maintained by their elders, coming out on the other side to take their place in the community of adults. Not so
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_072.jpg	with the psychedelic journey in the 1960s, which at its conclusion dropped its young travelers onto a psychic landscape unrecognizable to
test/images/synth_doc_00160_line_073.jpg	their parents.
