test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_000.jpg	The answer is that we must use rules of inference which, to the best of our understanding, will cause our symbols to
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_001.jpg	behave, in the relevant ways, like the abstract entities they denote. How can we be sure that they will? We cannot. Suppose
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_002.jpg	that some critics object to our rules of inference because they think that our symbols will behave differently from the
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_003.jpg	abstract entities.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_004.jpg	We cannot appeal to the authority of Aristotle or Plato, nor can we prove that our rules of inference are infallible (quite
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_005.jpg	apart from Gödel’s theorem, this would lead to an infinite regress, for we should first have to prove that the method of proof
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_006.jpg	that we used was itself valid). Nor can we haughtily tell the critics that there must be something wrong with their intuition,
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_007.jpg	because our intuition says that the symbols will mimic the abstract entities perfectly.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_008.jpg	All we can do is explain. We must explain why we think that, under the circumstances, the symbols will behave in the
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_009.jpg	desired way under our proposed rules.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_010.jpg	And the critics can explain why they favour a rival theory. A disagreement over two such theories is, in part, a disagreement
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_011.jpg	about the observable behaviour of physical objects. Such disagreements can be addressed by the normal methods of
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_012.jpg	science. Sometimes they can be readily resolved; sometimes not.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_013.jpg	Another cause of such a disagreement could be a conceptual clash about the nature of the abstract entities themselves.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_014.jpg	Then again, it is a matter of rival explanations, this time about abstractions rather than physical objects.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_015.jpg	Either we could come to a common understanding with our critics, or we could agree that we were discussing two different
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_016.jpg	abstract objects, or we could fail to agree. There are no guarantees.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_017.jpg	Thus, contrary to the traditional belief, it is not the case that disputes within mathematics can always be resolved by purely
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_018.jpg	procedural means. A conventional symbolic proof seems at first sight to have quite a different character from the ‘hands-on’
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_019.jpg	virtual-reality sort of proof.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_020.jpg	But we see now that they are related in the way that computations are to physical experiments. Any physical experiment
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_021.jpg	can be regarded as a computation, and any computation is a physical experiment. In both sorts of proof, physical entities
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_022.jpg	(whether in virtual reality or not) are manipulated according to rules. In both cases the physical entities represent the
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_023.jpg	abstract entities of interest. And in both cases the reliability of the proof depends on the truth of the theory that physical
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_024.jpg	and abstract entities do indeed share the appropriate properties.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_025.jpg	We can also see from the above discussion that proof is a physical process. In fact, a proof is a type of computation.
test/images/synth_doc_00163_line_026.jpg	‘Proving’ a proposition means performing a computation which, if one has done it correctly, establishes that the proposition
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_000.jpg	What of the world it does admit it tends to objectify, for the ego wants to reserve the gifts of subjectivity to itself. That’s
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_001.jpg	why it fails to see that there is a whole world of souls and spirits out there, by which I simply mean subjectivities other than
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_002.jpg	our own. It was only when the voice of my ego was quieted by psilocybin that I was able to sense that the plants in my
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_003.jpg	garden had a spirit too. (In the words of R.
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_004.jpg	M. Bucke, a nineteenth- century Canadian psychiatrist and mystic, “I saw that the universe is not composed of dead matter,
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_005.jpg	but is, on the contrary, a living Presence.”) “Ecology” and “coevolution” are scientific names for the same phenomena: every
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_006.jpg	species a subject acting on other subjects. But when this concept acquires the flesh of feeling, becomes “more deeply
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_007.jpg	interfused,” as it did during my first psilocybin journey, I’m happy to call it a spiritual experience. So too my various
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_008.jpg	psychedelic mergings: with Bach’s cello suite, with my son, Isaac, with my grandfather Bob, all spirits directly apprehended
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_009.jpg	and embraced, each time with a flood of feeling. So perhaps spiritual experience is simply what happens in the space that
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_010.jpg	opens up in the mind when “all mean egotism vanishes.” Wonders (and terrors) we’re ordinarily defended against flow into
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_011.jpg	our awareness; the far ends of the sensory spectrum, which are normally invisible to us, our senses can suddenly admit.
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_012.jpg	While the ego sleeps, the mind plays, proposing unexpected patterns of thought and new rays of relation. The gulf between
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_013.jpg	self and world, that no-man’s-land which in ordinary hours the ego so vigilantly patrols, closes down, allowing us to feel less
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_014.jpg	separate and more connected, “part and particle” of some larger entity. Whether we call that entity Nature, the Mind at
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_015.jpg	Large, or God hardly matters.
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_016.jpg	But it seems to be in the crucible of that merging that death loses some of its sting.CHAPTER FIVE THE NEUROSCIENCE
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_017.jpg	Your Brain on Psychedelics WHAT JUST HAPPENED in my brain? A molecule had launched me on each of these trips, and I
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_018.jpg	returned from my travels intensely curious to learn what the chemistry could tell me about consciousness and what that
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_019.jpg	might reveal about the brain’s relationship to the mind.
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_020.jpg	How do you get from the ingestion of a compound created by a fungus or a toad (or a human chemist) to a novel state of
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_021.jpg	consciousness with the power to change one’s perspective on things, not just during the journey, but long after the
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_022.jpg	molecule has left the body? Actually, there were three different molecules in question—psilocin, LSD, and 5-MeO-DMT—but
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_023.jpg	even a casual glance at their structures (and I say this as someone who earned a D in high school chemistry) indicates a
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_024.jpg	resemblance. All three molecules are tryptamines.
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_025.jpg	A tryptamine is a type of organic compound (an indole, to be exact) distinguished by the presence of two linked rings, one
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_026.jpg	of them with six atoms and the other with five. Living nature is awash in tryptamines, which show up in plants, fungi, and
test/images/synth_doc_00164_line_027.jpg	animals, where they typically act as signaling molecules between cells. The most famous tryptamine in the human body is
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_000.jpg	But did I really want to find out? R.
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_001.jpg	D. Laing once said there are three things humanbeings are afraid of: death, other people, and their own minds. Put me
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_002.jpg	down as two for three. But there are moments when curiosity gets the better of fear.
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_003.jpg	I guess for me such a moment had arrived. • • • BY “PSYCHEDELIC UNDERGROUND,” I don’t mean the shadowy world of
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_004.jpg	people making, selling, and using psychedelic drugs illegally. I have in mind a specific subset of that world, populated by
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_005.jpg	perhaps a couple hundred “guides,” or therapists, working with a variety of psychedelic substances in a carefully prescribed
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_006.jpg	manner, with the intention of healing the ill or bettering the well by helping them fulfill their spiritual, creative, or emotional
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_007.jpg	potential. Many of these guides are credentialed therapists, so by doing this work they are risking not only their freedom but
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_008.jpg	also their professional licenses.
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_009.jpg	I met one who was a physician and heard about another. Some are religious professionals—rabbis and ministers of various
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_010.jpg	denominations; a few call themselves shamans; one described himself as a druid.
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_011.jpg	The rest are therapists trained in dizzying combinations of alternative schools: I met Jungians and Reichians, Gestalt
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_012.jpg	therapists and “transpersonal” psychologists; energy healers; practitioners of aura work, breathwork, and bodywork; EST,
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_013.jpg	past-life, and family constellation therapists, vision questers, astrologers, and meditation teachers of every stripe—a shaggy
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_014.jpg	reunion of that whole 1970s class of alternative “modalities” that usually get lumped together under the rubric of the
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_015.jpg	“human potential movement” and that has as its world headquarters Esalen. The New Age terminology can be a little
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_016.jpg	off-putting; there were times when I felt I was listening to people whose language and vocabulary had stopped evolving
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_017.jpg	sometime in the early 1970s, at the very moment when psychedelic therapy was forced underground, freezing a subculture
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_018.jpg	in time.
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_019.jpg	I tracked down several of these people in the Bay Area, which probably has the largest concentration of underground guides
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_020.jpg	in the country, without much difficulty. Asking around, I soon discovered that a friend had a friend who worked with a guide
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_021.jpg	down in Santa Cruz, doing an annual psilocybin journey on the occasion of his birthday. I also soon discovered that the
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_022.jpg	membrane between the aboveground and thebelowground psychedelic worlds is permeable in certain places; a couple of
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_023.jpg	the people I befriended while reporting on the university psilocybin trials were willing to introduce me to “colleagues” who
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_024.jpg	worked underground. One introduction led to another as people came to trust my intentions.
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_025.jpg	By now, I’ve interviewed fifteen underground guides and have worked with five. Considering the risks involved, I found most
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_026.jpg	of these people unexpectedly open, generous, and trusting. Although the authorities have so far shown no interest in going
test/images/synth_doc_00165_line_027.jpg	after people practicing psychedelic- assisted therapy, the work remains illegal and so is dangerous to share with a journalist
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_000.jpg	For a theory to predict something ‘in principle’ means that the predictions follow logically from the theory, even if in practice
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_001.jpg	the amount of computation that would be needed to generate some of the predictions is too large to be technologically
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_002.jpg	feasible, or even too large for it to be physically possible for us to carry it out in the universe as we find it. Being able to
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_003.jpg	predict things or to describe them, however accurately, is not at all the same thing as understanding them.
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_004.jpg	Predictions and descriptions in physics are often expressed as mathematical formulae. Suppose that I memorize the formula
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_005.jpg	from which I could, if I had the time and the inclination, calculate any planetary position that has been recorded in the
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_006.jpg	astronomical archives. What exactly have I gained, compared with memorizing those archives directly?
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_007.jpg	The formula is easier to remember — but then, looking a number up in the archives may be even easier than calculating it
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_008.jpg	from the formula. The real advantage of the formula is that it can be used in an infinity of cases beyond the archived data,
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_009.jpg	for instance to predict the results of future observations.
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_010.jpg	It may also yield the historical positions of the planets more accurately, because the archived data contain observational
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_011.jpg	errors.Yet even though the formula summarizes infinitely more facts than the archives do, knowing it does not amount to
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_012.jpg	understanding planetary motions. Facts cannot be understood just by being summarized in a formula, any more than by
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_013.jpg	being listed on paper or committed to memory. They can be understood only by being explained. Fortunately, our best
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_014.jpg	theories embody deep explanations as well as accurate predictions.
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_015.jpg	For example, the general theory of relativity explains gravity in terms of a new, four- dimensional geometry of curved space
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_016.jpg	and time. It explains precisely how this geometry affects and is affected by matter. That explanation is the entire content of
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_017.jpg	the theory; predictions about planetary motions are merely some of the consequences that we can deduce from the
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_018.jpg	explanation.
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_019.jpg	What makes the general theory of relativity so important is not that it can predict planetary motions a shade more
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_020.jpg	accurately than Newton’s theory can, but that it reveals and explains previously unsuspected aspects of reality, such as the
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_021.jpg	curvature of space and time. This is typical of scientific explanation.
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_022.jpg	Scientific theories explain the objects and phenomena of our experience in terms of an underlying reality which we do not
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_023.jpg	experience directly. But the ability of a theory to explain what we experience is not its most valuable attribute. Its most
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_024.jpg	valuable attribute is that it explains the fabric of reality itself. As we shall see, one of the most valuable, significant and also
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_025.jpg	useful attributes of human thought generally is its ability to reveal and explain the fabric of reality. Yet some philosophers —
test/images/synth_doc_00167_line_026.jpg	and even some scientists — disparage the role of explanation in science.
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_000.jpg	The sheer complexity of the human brain and the greater number of different mental states in its repertoire (as compared
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_001.jpg	with other animals) make the maintenance of order a top priority, lest the system descend into chaos. Once upon a time,
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_002.jpg	Carhart-Harris writes, the human or protohuman brain exhibited a much more anarchic form of “primary consciousness,”
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_003.jpg	characterized by “magical thinking”—beliefs about the world that have been shaped by wishes and fears and supernatural
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_004.jpg	interpretation. (In primary consciousness, Carhart-Harris writes, “cognition is less meticulous in its sampling of the external
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_005.jpg	world and is instead easily biased by emotion, e.g., wishes and anxieties.”) Magical thinking is one way for human minds to
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_006.jpg	reduce their uncertainty about the world, but it is less than optimal for the success of the species.
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_007.jpg	A better way to suppress uncertainty and entropy in the human brain emerged with the evolution of the default mode
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_008.jpg	network, Carhart-Harris contends, a brain-regulating system that is absent or undeveloped in lower animals and young
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_009.jpg	children. Along with the default mode network, “a coherent sense of self or ‘ego’ emerges” and, with that, the human
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_010.jpg	capacity for self-reflection and reason. Magical thinking gives way to “a more reality-bound style of thinking, governed by
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_011.jpg	the ego.” Borrowing from Freud, he calls this more highly evolved mode of cognition “secondary consciousness.” Secondary
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_012.jpg	consciousness “pays deference to reality and diligently seeks to represent the world as precisely as possible” in order to
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_013.jpg	minimize “surprise and uncertainty (i.e. entropy).” The article offers an intriguing graphic depicting a “spectrum of cognitive
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_014.jpg	states,” ranging from high-entropy mental states to low ones. At the high-entropy end of the spectrum, he lists psychedelic
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_015.jpg	states; infant consciousness; early psychosis; magical thinking; and divergent or creative thinking.
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_016.jpg	At the low-entropy end of the spectrum, he lists narrow or rigid thinking; addiction; obsessive-compulsive disorder;
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_017.jpg	depression; anesthesia; and, finally, coma. Carhart-Harris suggests that the psychological “disorders” at the low- entropy
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_018.jpg	end of the spectrum are not the result of a lack of order in thebrain but rather stem from an excess of order.
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_019.jpg	When the grooves of self- reflective thinking deepen and harden, the ego becomes overbearing. This is perhaps most clearly
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_020.jpg	evident in depression, when the ego turns on itself and uncontrollable introspection gradually shades out reality.
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_021.jpg	Carhart- Harris cites research indicating that this debilitating state of mind (sometimes called heavy self-consciousness or
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_022.jpg	depressive realism) may be the result of a hyperactive default mode network, which can trap us in repetitive and destructive
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_023.jpg	loops of rumination that eventually close us off from the world outside. Huxley’s reducing valve contracts to zero.
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_024.jpg	Carhart-Harris believes that people suffering from a whole range of disorders characterized by excessively rigid patterns of
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_025.jpg	thought— including addiction, obsessions, and eating disorders as well as depression—stand to benefit from “the ability of
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_026.jpg	psychedelics to disrupt stereotyped patterns of thought and behavior by disintegrating the patterns of [neural] activity upon
test/images/synth_doc_00168_line_027.jpg	which they rest.” So it may be that some brains could stand to have a little more entropy, not less.
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_000.jpg	On your last morning, we’ll compare notes and try to integrate and make sense of your experience. Then I’ll cook you a big
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_001.jpg	breakfast to get you ready to face the interstate!” We scheduled a time for me to come back. • • •THE FIRST THING I
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_002.jpg	learned about myself that first afternoon, working with Fritz in the yurt, is that I am “easy to put under”—susceptible to
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_003.jpg	trance, a mental space completely new to me and accessible by nothing more than a shift in the pattern of one’s breathing.
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_004.jpg	It was the damnedest thing. Fritz’s instructions were straightforward: Breathe deeply and rapidly while exhaling as strongly
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_005.jpg	as you can. “At first it will feel unnatural and you’ll have to concentrate to maintain the rhythm, but after a few minutes your
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_006.jpg	body will take over and do it automatically.” I stretched out on the mattress and donned a pair of eyeshades while he put
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_007.jpg	on some music, something generically tribal and rhythmic, dominated by the pounding of a drum. He placed a plastic bucket
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_008.jpg	at my side, explaining that occasionally people throw up.
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_009.jpg	It was hard work at first, to breathe in such an exaggerated and unnatural way, even with Fritz’s enthusiastic coaching, but
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_010.jpg	then all at once my body took over, and I found that no thought was required to maintain the driving pace and rhythm. It
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_011.jpg	was as if I had broken free from gravity and settled into an orbit: the big deep breaths just came, automatically. Now I felt
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_012.jpg	an uncontrollable urge to move my legs and arms in sync with the pounding of the drums, which resonated in my rib cage
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_013.jpg	like a powerful new heartbeat. I felt possessed, both my body and my mind. I can’t remember many thoughts except “Hey,
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_014.jpg	this is working, whatever it is!” I was flat on my back yet dancing wildly, my arms and legs moving with a will of their own.
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_015.jpg	All control of my body I had surrendered to the music. It felt a little like speaking in tongues, or what I imagine that to be,
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_016.jpg	with some external force taking over the mind and body for its own obscure purpose. There wasn’t much visual imagery,
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_017.jpg	just the naked sensation of exhilaration, until I began to picture myself on the back of a big black horse, galloping headlong
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_018.jpg	down a path through a forest.
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_019.jpg	I was perched up high on its shoulders, like a jockey, holding on tight as the beast scissored its great muscles forward and
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_020.jpg	back with each long stride. As my rhythm synced with that of the horse, I could feel myself absorbing the animal’s power.
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_021.jpg	It felt fantastic to so fully inhabit my body, as if for the first time. And yet because I am not a very confident rider (or
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_022.jpg	dancer!), it also felt precarious, as if were I to miss a breath or beat I might tumble off.I had no idea how long the trance
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_023.jpg	lasted, time was utterly lost on me, but when Fritz gently brought me back to the present moment and the reality of the
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_024.jpg	room, simply by encouraging me to slow and relax my breathing, he reported I had been “in it” for an hour and fifteen
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_025.jpg	minutes. I felt flushed and sweaty and triumphant, as if I had run a marathon; Fritz said I looked “radiant”—“young like a
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_026.jpg	baby.” “You had no resistance,” he said approvingly; “that’s a good sign for tomorrow.” I had no idea what had just
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_027.jpg	happened, could recall little more of the hour than riding the horse, but the episode seemed to have involved a terrific
test/images/synth_doc_00169_line_028.jpg	physical release of some kind. Something had let go of me or been expunged, and I felt buoyant. And humbled by the
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_000.jpg	Gordon Wasson published his notorious Life magazine article about the “mushrooms that cause strange visions.” For Leary,
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_001.jpg	the mushrooms were transformative. In an afternoon, his passion to understand the human mind had been reignited
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_002.jpg	—indeed, had exploded. “In four hours by the swimming pool in Cuernavaca I learned more about the mind, the brain, and
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_003.jpg	its structures than I did in the precedingfifteen as a diligent psychologist,” he wrote later in Flashbacks, his 1983 memoir. “I
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_004.jpg	learned that the brain is an underutilized biocomputer . . . I learned that normal consciousness is one drop in an ocean of
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_005.jpg	intelligence. That consciousness and intelligence can be systematically expanded.
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_006.jpg	That the brain can be reprogrammed.” Leary returned from his journey with an irresistible urge to “rush back and tell
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_007.jpg	everyone,” as he recalled in High Priest, his 1968 memoir. And then in a handful of sentences he slid into a prophetic voice,
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_008.jpg	one in which the whole future trajectory of Timothy Leary could be foretold: Listen!
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_009.jpg	Wake up! You are God! You have the Divine plan engraved in cellular script within you. Listen! Take this sacrament!
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_010.jpg	You’ll see! You’ll get the revelation! It will change your life!
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_011.jpg	But at least for the first year or two at Harvard, Leary went through the motions of doing science. Back in Cambridge that
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_012.jpg	fall, he recruited Richard Alpert, a promising assistant professor who was heir to a railroad fortune, and, having secured the
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_013.jpg	tacit approval of their department chair, David McClelland, the two launched the Harvard Psilocybin Project, operating out of
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_014.jpg	a tiny broom closet of an office in the Department of Social Relations in a house at 5 Divinity Avenue. (I went looking for the
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_015.jpg	house, but it has long since been razed and replaced by a sprawling, block-long brick science building. Leary, ever the
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_016.jpg	salesman, had convinced Harvard that the research he proposed to undertake was squarely in the tradition of William
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_017.jpg	James, who in the early years of the century had also studied altered states of consciousness and mystical experience at
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_018.jpg	Harvard.
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_019.jpg	The university placed one condition on the research: Leary and Alpert could give the new drugs to graduate students, but
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_020.jpg	not to undergraduates. Before long, an intriguingly titled new seminar showed up in the Harvard course listings:
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_021.jpg	Experimental Expansion of Consciousness The literature describing internally and externally induced changes in awareness
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_022.jpg	will be reviewed.
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_023.jpg	The basic elements of mystical experiences will be studied cross-culturally. Themembers of the seminar will participate in
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_024.jpg	experiences with consciousness expanding methods and a systematic analysis of attention will be paid to the problems of
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_025.jpg	methodology in this area.
test/images/synth_doc_00171_line_026.jpg	This seminar will be limited to advanced graduate students. Admission by consent of the instructor. “Experimental Expansion
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_000.jpg	There are chemical messages passing in both directions as well. I am assuming that in principle those messages could also
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_001.jpg	be intercepted and replaced at some point between the brain and the rest of the body. Thus the user would lie motionless,
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_002.jpg	connected to the computer, but having the experience of interacting fully with a simulated world — in effect, living there.
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_003.jpg	Figure 5.2 illustrates what I am envisaging. Incidentally, though such technology lies well in the future, the idea for it is
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_004.jpg	much older than the theory of computation itself.
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_005.jpg	In the early seventeenth century Descartes was already considering the philosophical implications of a sense- manipulating
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_006.jpg	‘demon’ that was essentially a virtual-reality generator of the type shown in Figure 5.2, with a supernatural mind replacing
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_007.jpg	the computer. From the foregoing discussion it seems that any virtual-reality generator must have at least three principal
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_008.jpg	components: a set of sensors (which may be nerve-impulse detectors) to detect what the user is doing, a set of image
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_009.jpg	generators (which may be nerve-stimulation devices), and a computer in control. My account so far has concentrated on the
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_010.jpg	first two of these, the sensors and the image generators. That is because, at the present primitive state of the technology,
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_011.jpg	virtual-reality research is still preoccupied with image generation.
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_012.jpg	But when we look beyond transient technological limitations, we see that image generators merely provide the interface —
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_013.jpg	the ‘connecting cable’ — between the user and the true virtual-reality generator, which is the computer. For it is entirely
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_014.jpg	within the computer that the specified environment is simulated. It is the computer that provides the complex and
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_015.jpg	autonomous ‘kicking back’ that justifies the word ‘reality’ in ‘virtual reality’.
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_016.jpg	The connecting cable contributes nothing to the user’s perceived environment, being from the user’s point of view
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_017.jpg	‘transparent’, just as we naturally do not perceive our own nerves as being part of our environment. Thus virtual-reality
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_018.jpg	generators of the future would be better described as having only one principal component, a computer, together with some
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_019.jpg	trivial peripheral devices.FIGURE 5.2.
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_020.jpg	Virtual reality as it might be implemented in the future. I do not want to understate the practical problems involved in
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_021.jpg	intercepting all the nerve signals passing into and out of the human brain, and in cracking the various codes involved. But
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_022.jpg	this is a finite set of problems that we shall have to solve once only. After that, the focus of virtual-reality technology will
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_023.jpg	shift once and for all to the computer, to the problem of programming it to render various environments. What
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_024.jpg	environments we shall be able to render will no longer depend on what sensors and image generators we can build, but on
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_025.jpg	what environments we can specify. ‘Specifying’ an environment will mean supplying a program for the computer, which is
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_026.jpg	the heart of the virtual- reality generator.
test/images/synth_doc_00170_line_027.jpg	Because of the interactive nature of virtual reality, the concept of an accurate rendering is not as straightforward for virtual
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_000.jpg	We deem an explanation to be better if it leaves fewer loose ends (such as entities whose properties are themselves
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_001.jpg	unexplained), requires fewer and simpler postulates, is more general, meshes more easily with good explanations in other
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_002.jpg	fields and so on. But why should a better explanation be what we always assume it to be in practice, namely the token of a
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_003.jpg	truer theory? Why, for that matter, should a downright bad explanation (one that has none of the above attributes, say)
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_004.jpg	necessarily be false? There is indeed no logically necessary connection between truth and explanatory power.
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_005.jpg	A bad explanation (such as solipsism) may be true. Even the best and truest available theory may make a false prediction in
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_006.jpg	particular cases, and those might be the very cases in which we rely on the theory. No valid form of reasoning can logically
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_007.jpg	rule out such possibilities, or even prove them unlikely. But in that case, what justifies our relying on our best explanations
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_008.jpg	as guides to practical decision-making? More generally, whatever criteria we used to judge scientific theories, how could the
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_009.jpg	fact that a theory satisfied those criteria today possibly imply anything about what will happen if we rely on the theory
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_010.jpg	tomorrow?
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_011.jpg	This is the modern form of the ‘problem of induction’. Most philosophers are now content with Popper’s contention that new
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_012.jpg	theories are not inferred fromanything, but are merely hypotheses.
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_013.jpg	They also accept that scientific progress is made through conjectures and refutations (as described in Chapter 3), and that
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_014.jpg	theories are accepted when all their rivals are refuted, and not by virtue of numerous confirming instances. They accept that
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_015.jpg	the knowledge obtained in this way tends, in the event, to be reliable. The problem is that they do not see why it should be.
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_016.jpg	Traditional inductivists tried to formulate a ‘principle of induction’, which said that confirming instances made a theory more
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_017.jpg	likely, or that ‘the future will resemble the past’, or some such statement. They also tried to formulate an inductive scientific
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_018.jpg	methodology, laying down rules for what sort of inferences one could validly draw from ‘data’.
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_019.jpg	They all failed, for the reasons I have explained. But even if they had succeeded, in the sense of constructing a scheme that
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_020.jpg	could be followed successfully to create scientific knowledge, this would not have solved the problem of induction as it is
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_021.jpg	nowadays understood. For in that case ‘induction’ would simply be another possible way of choosing theories, and the
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_022.jpg	problem would remain of why those theories should be a reliable basis for action. In other words, philosophers who worry
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_023.jpg	about this ‘problem of induction’ are not inductivists in the old-fashioned sense.
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_024.jpg	They do not try to obtain or justify any theories inductively. They do not expect the sky to fall in, but they do not know how
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_025.jpg	to justify that expectation. Philosophers today yearn for this missing justification.
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_026.jpg	They no longer believe that induction would provide it, yet they have an induction-shaped gap in their scheme of things, just
test/images/synth_doc_00173_line_027.jpg	as religious people who have lost their faith suffer from a ‘God-shaped gap’ in their scheme of things. But in my opinion
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_000.jpg	A typical theory in a modern student’s syllabus may be harder to understand than any of the master builder’s rules of
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_001.jpg	thumb; but the modern theories are far fewer, and their explanatory power gives them other properties such as beauty,
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_002.jpg	inner logic and connections with other subjects which make them easier to learn. Some of the ancient rules of thumb are
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_003.jpg	now known to be erroneous, while others are known to be true, or to be good approximations to the truth, and we know
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_004.jpg	why that is so. A few are still in use.
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_005.jpg	But none of them is any longer the source of anyone’s understanding of what makes structures stand up. I am not, of
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_006.jpg	course, denying that specialization is occurring in many subjects in which knowledge is growing, including architecture. This
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_007.jpg	is not a one-way process, for specializations often disappear too: wheels are no longer designed or made by wheelwrights,
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_008.jpg	nor ploughs by ploughwrights, nor are letters written by scribes.
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_009.jpg	It is nevertheless quite evident that the deepening, unifying tendency I have been describing is not the only one at work: a
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_010.jpg	continual broadening is going on at the same time. That is, new ideas often do more than just supersede, simplify or unify
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_011.jpg	existing ones. They also extend human understanding into areas that were previously not understood at all — or whose very
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_012.jpg	existence was not guessed at. They may open up new opportunities, new problems, new specializations and even new
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_013.jpg	subjects.
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_014.jpg	And when that happens it may give us, at least temporarily, more to learn in order to understand it all. The science of
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_015.jpg	medicine is perhaps the most frequently cited case of increasing specialization seeming to follow inevitably from increasing
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_016.jpg	knowledge, as new cures and better treatments for more diseases are discovered. But even in medicine the opposite,
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_017.jpg	unifying tendency is also present, and is becoming stronger. Admittedly, many functions of the body are still poorly
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_018.jpg	understood, and so are the mechanisms of many diseases.
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_019.jpg	Consequently some areas of medical knowledge still consist mainly of collections of recorded facts, together with the skills
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_020.jpg	and intuitions of doctors who have experience of particular diseases and particular treatments, and who pass on these skills
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_021.jpg	and intuitions from one generation to the next. Much of medicine, in other words, is still in the rule-of-thumb era, and when
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_022.jpg	new rules of thumb are discovered there is indeed more incentive for specialization. But as medical and biochemical
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_023.jpg	research comes up with deeper explanations of disease processes (and healthy processes) in the body, understanding is
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_024.jpg	also on the increase.
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_025.jpg	More general concepts are replacing more specific ones as common, underlying molecular mechanisms are found for
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_026.jpg	dissimilar diseases in different parts of the body. Once a disease can be understood as fitting into a general framework, the
test/images/synth_doc_00174_line_027.jpg	role of the specialist diminishes. Instead, physicians coming across an unfamiliar disease or a rare complication can rely
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_000.jpg	Solipsism must also postulate the existence of an additional class of processes — invisible, inexplicable processes which give
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_001.jpg	the mind the illusion of living in an external reality. The solipsist, who believes that nothing exists other than the contents of
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_002.jpg	one mind, must also believe that that mind is a phenomenon of greater multiplicity than is normally supposed. It contains
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_003.jpg	other-people-like thoughts, planet-like thoughts and laws-of-physics-like thoughts.
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_004.jpg	These thoughts are real. They develop in a complex way (or pretend to), and they have enough autonomy to surprise,
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_005.jpg	disappoint, enlighten or thwart that other class of thoughts which call themselves ‘I.’ Thus the solipsist’s explanation of the
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_006.jpg	world is in terms of interacting thoughts rather than interacting objects. But those thoughts are real, and interact according
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_007.jpg	to the same rules that the realist says govern the interaction of objects. Thus solipsism, far from being a world-view stripped
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_008.jpg	to its essentials, is actually just realism disguised and weighed down by additional unnecessary assumptions — worthless
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_009.jpg	baggage, introduced only to be explained away. By this argument we can dispense with solipsism and all the related
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_010.jpg	theories.
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_011.jpg	They are all indefensible. Incidentally, we have already rejected one world- view on these grounds, namely positivism (the
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_012.jpg	theory that all statements other than those describing or predicting observations are meaningless).
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_013.jpg	As Iremarked in Chapter 1, positivism asserts its own meaninglessness, and therefore cannot be consistently defended. So
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_014.jpg	we can continue, reassured, with common-sense realism and the pursuit of explanations by scientific methods. But in the
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_015.jpg	light of this conclusion, what can we say about the arguments that made solipsism and its relatives superficially plausible,
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_016.jpg	namely that they could neither be proved false nor ruled out by experiment? What is the status of those arguments now? If
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_017.jpg	we have neither proved solipsism false nor ruled it out by experiment, what have we done?
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_018.jpg	There is an assumption built into this question. It is that theories can be classified in a hierarchy, ‘mathematical’ —>
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_019.jpg	‘scientific’ —> ‘philosophical’, of decreasing intrinsic reliability. Many people take the existence of this hierarchy for granted,
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_020.jpg	despite the fact that these judgements of comparative reliability depend entirely on philosophical arguments, arguments
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_021.jpg	that classify themselves as quite unreliable!
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_022.jpg	In fact, the idea of this hierarchy is a cousin of the reductionist mistake I discussed in Chapter 1 (the theory that
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_023.jpg	microscopic laws and phenomena are more fundamental than emergent ones). The same assumption occurs in inductivism,
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_024.jpg	which supposes that we can be absolutely certain of the conclusions of mathematical arguments because they are
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_025.jpg	deductive, reasonably sure of scientific arguments because they are ‘inductive’, and forever undecided about philosophical
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_026.jpg	arguments, which it sees as little more than matters of taste. But none of that is true.
test/images/synth_doc_00172_line_027.jpg	Explanations are not justified by the means by which they were derived; they are justified by their superior ability, relative
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_000.jpg	The tenth problem was to find a set of rules of inference with the above properties, and, by their own standards, to prove
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_001.jpg	them consistent. Hilbert was to be definitively disappointed. Thirty-one years later, Kurt Gödel revolutionized proof theory
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_002.jpg	with a root-and-branch refutation from which the mathematical and philosophical worlds are still reeling: he proved that
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_003.jpg	Hilbert’s tenth problem is insoluble. Gödel proved first that any set of rules of inference that is capable of correctly validating
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_004.jpg	even the proofs of ordinary arithmetic could never validate a proof of its own consistency.
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_005.jpg	Therefore there is no hope of finding the provably consistent set of rules that Hilbert envisaged. Second, Gödel proved that
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_006.jpg	if a set of rules of inference in some (sufficiently rich) branch of mathematics is consistent (whether provably so or not),
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_007.jpg	then within that branch of mathematics there must exist valid methods of proof that those rules fail to designate as valid.
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_008.jpg	This is called Gödel’s incompleteness theorem. To prove his theorems, Gödel used a remarkable extension of the Cantor
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_009.jpg	‘diagonal argument’ that I mentioned inChapter 6. He began by considering any consistent set of rules of inference.
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_010.jpg	Then he showed how to construct a proposition which could neither be proved nor disproved under those rules. Then he
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_011.jpg	proved that that proposition would be true. If Hilbert’s programme had worked, it would have been bad news for the
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_012.jpg	conception of reality that I am promoting in this book, for it would have removed the necessity for understanding in judging
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_013.jpg	mathematical ideas.
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_014.jpg	Anyone — or any mindless machine — that could learn Hilbert’s hoped-for rules of inference by heart would be as good a
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_015.jpg	judge of mathematical propositions as the ablest mathematician, yet without needing the mathematician’s insight or
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_016.jpg	understanding, or even having the remotest clue as to what the propositions were about. In principle, it would be possible
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_017.jpg	to make new mathematical discoveries without knowing any mathematics at all, beyond Hilbert’s rules. One would simply
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_018.jpg	check through all possible strings of letters and mathematical symbols in alphabetical order, until one of them passed the
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_019.jpg	test for being a proof or disproof of some famous unsolved conjecture. In principle, one could settle any mathematical
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_020.jpg	controversy without ever understanding it — without even knowing the meanings of the symbols, let alone understanding
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_021.jpg	how the proof worked, or what it proved, or what the method of proof was, or why it was reliable.
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_022.jpg	It may seem that the achievement of a unified standard of proof in mathematics could at least have helped us in the overall
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_023.jpg	drive towards unification — that is, the ‘deepening’ of our knowledge that I referred to in Chapter 1. But the opposite is the
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_024.jpg	case. Like the predictive ‘theory of everything’ in physics, Hilbert’s rules would have told us almost nothing about the fabric
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_025.jpg	of reality. They would, as far as mathematics goes, have realized the ultimate reductionist vision, predicting everything (in
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_026.jpg	principle) but explaining nothing.
test/images/synth_doc_00175_line_027.jpg	Moreover, if mathematics had been reductionist then all the undesirable features which I argued in Chapter 1 are absent
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_000.jpg	The only reason why we fall downwards when unsupported is that the fabric of space and time in which we exist is curved.
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_001.jpg	Not only do explanations change, but our criteria and ideas about what should count as an explanation are gradually
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_002.jpg	changing (improving) too. So the list of acceptable modes of explanation will always be open-ended, and consequently the
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_003.jpg	list of acceptable criteria for reality must be open-ended too. But what is it about an explanation — given that, for whatever
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_004.jpg	reasons, we find it satisfactory — that should make us classify some things as real and others as illusory or imaginary?
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_005.jpg	James Boswell relates in his Life of Johnson how he and Dr Johnson were discussing Bishop Berkeley’s solipsistic theory of
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_006.jpg	the non-existence of the material world.
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_007.jpg	Boswell remarked that although no one believed the theory, no one could refute it either. Dr Johnson kicked a large rock
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_008.jpg	and said, as his foot rebounded, ‘I refute it thus.’ Dr Johnson’s point was that Berkeley’s denial of the rock’s existence is
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_009.jpg	incompatible with finding an explanation of the rebound that he himself felt.
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_010.jpg	Solipsism cannot accommodate any explanation of why that experiment — or any experiment — should have one outcome
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_011.jpg	rather than another. To explain the effect that the rock had on him, Dr Johnson was forced to take a position on the nature
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_012.jpg	of rocks. Were they part of an autonomous external reality, or were they figments of his imagination?
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_013.jpg	In the latter case he would have to conclude that ‘his imagination’ was itself a vast, complex, autonomous universe. The
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_014.jpg	same dilemma confronted the solipsistic professor who, if pressed for explanations, would be forced to take a position on
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_015.jpg	the nature of the audience. And the Inquisition would have had to take a position on the source of the underlying regularity
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_016.jpg	in the motion of planets, a regularity that is explicable only by reference to the heliocentric theory.
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_017.jpg	For all these people, taking their own position seriously as an explanation of the world would lead them directly to realism
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_018.jpg	and Galilean rationality. But Dr Johnson’s idea is more than a refutation of solipsism. It also illustrates the criterion for reality
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_019.jpg	that is used in science, namely, if something can kick back, it exists. ‘Kicking back’ here does not necessarily mean that the
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_020.jpg	alleged object is responding to being kicked — to being physically affected as Dr Johnson’s rock was.
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_021.jpg	It is enough that when we ‘kick’ something, the object affects us in ways that require independent explanation. For
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_022.jpg	example, Galileo had no means of affecting planets, but he could affect the light that came from them.
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_023.jpg	His equivalent of kicking the rock was refracting that light through the lenses of his telescopes and eyes. That light
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_024.jpg	responded by ‘kicking’ his retina back.
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_025.jpg	The way it kicked back allowed him to conclude not only that the light was real, but that the heliocentric planetary motions
test/images/synth_doc_00177_line_026.jpg	required to explain the patterns in which the light arrivedwere also real. By the way, Dr Johnson did not directly kick the
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_000.jpg	Doblin, born in 1953, is a great shaggy dog with a bone; he has been lobbying to change the government’s mind about
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_001.jpg	psychedelics since shortly after graduating from New College, in Florida, in 1987. After experimenting with LSD as an
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_002.jpg	undergraduate, and later with MDMA, Doblin decided his calling in life was to become a psychedelic therapist.
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_003.jpg	But after the banning of MDMA in 1985, that dream became unachievable without a change in federal laws and regulations,
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_004.jpg	so he decided he’d better first get a doctorate in public policy at Harvard’s Kennedy School. There, he mastered the
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_005.jpg	intricacies of the FDA’s drug approval process, and in his dissertation plotted the laborious path to official acceptance that
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_006.jpg	psilocybin and MDMA are now following.Doblin is disarmingly, perhaps helplessly, candid, happy to talk openly to a reporter
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_007.jpg	about his formative psychedelic experiences as well as political strategy and tactics. Like Timothy Leary, Doblin is the
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_008.jpg	happiest of warriors, never not smiling and exhibiting a degree of enthusiasm for the work you wouldn’t expect from a man
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_009.jpg	who has been knocking his head against the same wall for his entire adult life. Doblin works out of a somewhat Dickensian
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_010.jpg	office tucked into the attic of his rambling colonial in Belmont, Massachusetts, at a desk stacked to the ceiling with
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_011.jpg	precarious piles of manuscripts, journal articles, photographs, and memorabilia reaching back more than forty years.
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_012.jpg	Some of the memorabilia commemorates the time early in his career when Doblin decided the best way to end sectarian
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_013.jpg	strife would be to mail a group of the world’s spiritual leaders tablets of MDMA, a drug famous for its ability to break down
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_014.jpg	barriers between people and kindle empathy. Around the same time, he arranged to have a thousand doses of MDMA sent
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_015.jpg	to people in the Soviet military who were working on arms control negotiations with President Reagan. For Doblin, winning
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_016.jpg	FDA approval for the medical use of psychedelics —which he believes is now in view, for both MDMA and psilocybin—is a
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_017.jpg	means to a more ambitious and still more controversial end: the incorporation of psychedelics into American society and
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_018.jpg	culture, not just medicine.
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_019.jpg	This of course is the same winning strategy followed by the campaign to decriminalize marijuana, in which promoting the
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_020.jpg	medical uses of cannabis changed the drug’s image, leading to a more general public acceptance. Not surprisingly, this sort
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_021.jpg	of talk rankles more cautious heads in the community (Bob Jesse among them), but Rick Doblin is not one to soft- pedal his
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_022.jpg	agenda or to even think about taking an interview off the record. This gets him a lot of press; how much it helps the cause
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_023.jpg	is debatable. But there is no question that especially in the last several years Doblin has succeeded in getting important
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_024.jpg	research approved and funded, especially in the case of MDMA, which has long been MAPS’s main focus. MAPS has
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_025.jpg	sponsored several small clinical trials that have demonstrated MDMA’s value in treating post-traumatic stress disorder, or
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_026.jpg	PTSD.
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_027.jpg	(Doblin defines psychedelics generously, so as to include MDMA and even cannabis, even though their mechanisms of action
test/images/synth_doc_00176_line_028.jpg	in the brain are very different from that of the classical psychedelics. But beyond helping those sufferingwith PTSD and
