test/images/synth_doc_00195_line_024.jpg	The Dalai Lama extends this sentiment infinitely. Although we have no proof, there may well be other worlds in galaxies
test/images/synth_doc_00195_line_025.jpg	near or far with their own life-forms. If so, he assumes they, too, would wish to avoid suffering and want happiness. 31. A.
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_000.jpg	Why did they evolve the ability to produce a chemical compound so closely related to serotonin, the neurotransmitter, that it
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_001.jpg	can slip across the blood-brain barrier andtemporarily take charge of the mammalian brain? Was it a defense chemical,
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_002.jpg	intended to poison mushroom eaters? That would seem to be the most straightforward explanation, yet it is undermined by
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_003.jpg	the fact the fungus produces the hallucinogen almost exclusively in its “fruiting body”—that part of the organism it is
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_004.jpg	happiest to have eaten.
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_005.jpg	Was there perhaps some benefit to the mushroom in being able to change the minds of the animals that eat it?* There
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_006.jpg	were also the more philosophical questions posed by the existence of a fungus that could not only change consciousness but
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_007.jpg	occasion a profound mystical experience in humans. This fact can be interpreted in two completely different ways. On the
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_008.jpg	first interpretation, the mind-altering power of psilocybin argues for a firmly materialist understanding of consciousness and
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_009.jpg	spirituality, because the changes observed in the mind can be traced directly to the presence of a chemical —psilocybin.
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_010.jpg	What is more material than a chemical?
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_011.jpg	One could reasonably conclude from the action of psychedelics that the gods are nothing more than chemically induced
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_012.jpg	figments of the hominid imagination. Yet, surprisingly, most of the people who have had these experiences don’t see the
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_013.jpg	matter that way at all. Even the most secular among them come away from their journeys convinced there exists something
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_014.jpg	that transcends a material understanding of reality: some sort of a “Beyond.” It’s not that they deny a naturalistic basis for
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_015.jpg	this revelation; they just interpret it differently. If the experience of transcendence is mediated by molecules that flow
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_016.jpg	through both our brains and the natural world of plants and fungi, then perhaps nature is not as mute as Science has told
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_017.jpg	us, and “Spirit,” however defined, exists out there—is immanent in nature, in other words, just as countless premodern
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_018.jpg	cultures have believed.
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_019.jpg	What to my (spiritually impoverished) mind seemed to constitute a good case for the disenchantment of the world becomes
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_020.jpg	in the minds of the more psychedelically experienced irrefutable proof of its fundamental enchantment. Flesh of the gods,
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_021.jpg	indeed. So here was a curious paradox.
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_022.jpg	The same phenomenon that pointed to a materialist explanation for spiritual and religious belief gave people an experience
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_023.jpg	so powerful it convinced them of the existence of a nonmaterial reality—the very basis of religious belief.I hoped that
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_024.jpg	getting to know the psychoactive LBMs (mycologist shorthand for “little brown mushrooms”) at the bottom of this paradox
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_025.jpg	might clarify the matter or, perhaps, somehow dissolve it. I was already something of a mushroom hunter, secure in my
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_026.jpg	ability to identify a handful of edible woodland species (chanterelles, morels, black trumpets, and porcini) with a high
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_027.jpg	enough degree of confidence to eat what I found. However, I had been told by all my teachers that the world of LBMs was
test/images/synth_doc_00197_line_028.jpg	far more daunting in its complexity and peril; many if not most of the species that can kill you are LBMs. But perhaps with
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_000.jpg	We just need to be better listeners.” These were riffs I’d heard Stamets deliver in countless talks and interviews.
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_001.jpg	“Mushrooms have taught me the interconnectedness of all life-forms and the molecular matrix that we share,” he explains in
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_002.jpg	another one. “I no longer feel that I am in this envelope of a human life called Paul Stamets. I am part of the stream of
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_003.jpg	molecules that are flowing through nature. I am given a voice, given consciousness for a time, but I feel that I am part of
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_004.jpg	this continuum of stardust into which I am born and to which I will return at the end of this life.” Stamets sounded very
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_005.jpg	much like the volunteers I met at Hopkins who had had full-blown mystical experiences, people whose sense of themselves
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_006.jpg	as individuals had been subsumed into a larger whole—a form of “unitive consciousness,” which, in Stamets’s case, had
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_007.jpg	folded him into the web of nature, as its not so humble servant. “I think Psilocybes have given me new insights that may
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_008.jpg	allow me to help steer and speed fungal evolution so that we can find solutions to our problems.” Especially in a time of
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_009.jpg	ecological crisis, he suggests, we can’t afford to wait for evolution, unfolding at its normal pace, to put forth these solutions
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_010.jpg	in time. Let the depatterning begin.
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_011.jpg	As Stamets held forth, and forth, I couldn’t help but picture in my mind Alex Grey’s wacked painting of the stoned ape, with
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_012.jpg	the tornadoes of thought flying out of his hairy head. So much of what Stamets has to say treads a perilously narrow ledge,
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_013.jpg	perched between the autodidact’s soaring speculative flights and the stoned crank’s late night riffings that eventually send
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_014.jpg	everyone in earshot off to bed. But just when I was beginning to grow impatient with his meanders, and could hear the call
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_015.jpg	of my sleeping bag from inside the yurt, he, or I, turned a corner, and his mycological prophecies suddenly appeared to me
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_016.jpg	in a more generous light. The day before, Stamets had given me a tour of the labs and grow rooms at Fungi Perfecti, the
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_017.jpg	company he founded right out of college.Tucked into the evergreen forest a short walk from his house, the Fungi Perfecti
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_018.jpg	complex consists of a series of long white metal buildings that look like Quonset huts or small hangars.
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_019.jpg	Outside are piles of wood chips, discarded fungi, and growing media. Some of the buildings house the grow rooms where he
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_020.jpg	raises medicinal and edible species; others contain his research facility, with clean rooms and laminar flow chambers in
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_021.jpg	which Stamets reproduces fungi from tissue culture and conducts his experiments.
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_022.jpg	On the office walls hang several of his patents, framed. Amid the torrent of words, what I observed in these buildings was a
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_023.jpg	salutary reminder that while Stamets is surely a big talker, he is not just a talker.
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_024.jpg	He is a big doer too, a successful researcher and entrepreneur who is using fungi to make original contributions across a
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_025.jpg	remarkably wide range of fields, from medicine and environmental restoration to agriculture and forestry and even national
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_026.jpg	defense. Stamets is in fact a scientist, albeit of a special kind. Exactly what kind of scientist I didn’t completely understand
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_027.jpg	until a few weeks later, when I happened to read a wonderful biography of Alexander von Humboldt, the great early
test/images/synth_doc_00198_line_028.jpg	nineteenth-century German scientist (and colleague of Goethe’s) who revolutionized our understanding of the natural world.
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_000.jpg	Now, I already knew something about such doors, having written about psychoactive plants earlier in my career. In The
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_001.jpg	Botany of Desire, I explored at some length what I had been surprised to discover is a universal human desire to change
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_002.jpg	consciousness. There is not a culture on earth (well, one*) that doesn’t make use of certain plants to change the contents
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_003.jpg	of the mind, whether as a matter of healing, habit, or spiritual practice. That such a curious and seemingly maladaptive
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_004.jpg	desire should exist alongside our desires for nourishment and beauty and sex—all of which make much more obvious
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_005.jpg	evolutionary sense—cried out for an explanation. The simplest was that these substances help relieve pain and boredom.
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_006.jpg	Yet the powerful feelings and elaborate taboos and rituals that surround many of these psychoactive species suggest there
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_007.jpg	must be something more to it. For our species, I learned, plants and fungi with the power to radically alter consciousness
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_008.jpg	have long and widely been used as tools for healingthe mind, for facilitating rites of passage, and for serving as a medium
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_009.jpg	for communicating with supernatural realms, or spirit worlds. These uses were ancient and venerable in a great many
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_010.jpg	cultures, but I ventured one other application: to enrich the collective imagination—the culture—with the novel ideas and
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_011.jpg	visions that a select few people bring back from wherever it is they go. • • • NOW THAT I HAD DEVELOPED an intellectual
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_012.jpg	appreciation for the potential value of these psychoactive substances, you might think I would have been more eager to try
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_013.jpg	them.
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_014.jpg	I’m not sure what I was waiting for: courage, maybe, or the right opportunity, which a busy life lived mainly on the right
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_015.jpg	side of the law never quite seemed to afford. But when I began to weigh the potential benefits I was hearing about against
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_016.jpg	the risks, I was surprised to learn that psychedelics are far more frightening to people than they are dangerous.
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_017.jpg	Many of the most notorious perils are either exaggerated or mythical. It is virtually impossible to die from an overdose of
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_018.jpg	LSD or psilocybin, for example, and neither drug is addictive. After trying them once, animals will not seek a second dose,
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_019.jpg	and repeated use by people robs the drugs of their effect.* It is true that the terrifying experiences some people have on
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_020.jpg	psychedelics can risk flipping those at risk into psychosis, so no one with a family history or predisposition to mental illness
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_021.jpg	should ever take them. But emergency room admissions involving psychedelics are exceedingly rare, and many of the cases
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_022.jpg	doctors diagnose as psychotic breaks turn out to be merely short-lived panic attacks.
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_023.jpg	It is also the case that people on psychedelics are liable to do stupid and dangerous things: walk out into traffic, fall from
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_024.jpg	high places, and, on rare occasions, kill themselves. “Bad trips” are very real and can be one of “the most challenging
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_025.jpg	experiences of [a] lifetime,” according to a large survey of psychedelic users asked about their experiences.* But it’s
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_026.jpg	important to distinguish what can happen when these drugs are used in uncontrolled situations, without attention to set and
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_027.jpg	setting, from what happens under clinical conditions, after careful screening and under supervision. Since the revival of
test/images/synth_doc_00199_line_028.jpg	sanctioned psychedelic researchbeginning in the 1990s, nearly a thousand volunteers have been dosed, and not a single
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_000.jpg	In 1965, CBS News broadcast an admiring hour- long “special report” on the hospital’s work with alcoholics, called LSD: The
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_001.jpg	Spring Grove Experiment. The response to the program was so positive that the Maryland state legislature established a
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_002.jpg	multimillion- dollar research facility on the campus of the Spring Grove State Hospital, called the Maryland Psychiatric
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_003.jpg	Research Center.
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_004.jpg	Stan Grof, Walter Pahnke, and Bill Richards were hired to help run it, along with several dozen other therapists,
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_005.jpg	psychiatrists, pharmacologists, and support staff. Equally hard to believe today is the fact that, as Richards told me,
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_006.jpg	“whenever we hired someone, they would receive a couple of LSD sessions as part of their training to do the work.
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_007.jpg	We had authorization! How else could you be sensitive to what was going on in the mind of the patient? I wish we could do
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_008.jpg	that at Hopkins.” The fact that such an ambitious research program continued at Spring Grove well into the 1970s suggests
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_009.jpg	the story of the suppression of psychedelic research is a little more complicated than the conventional narrative would
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_010.jpg	indicate. While it is true that some research projects— such as Jim Fadiman’s creativity trials in Palo Alto—received orders
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_011.jpg	from Washington to stop, other projects on long-term grants were allowed to continue until the money ran out, as it
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_012.jpg	eventually did.
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_013.jpg	Rather than shut down all research, as many in the psychedelic community believe happened, the government simply made
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_014.jpg	it more difficult to get approvals, and funding gradually dried up. As time went on, researchers found that on top of all the
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_015.jpg	bureaucratic and financial hurdles they also had to dealwith “the snicker test”: How would your colleagues react when you
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_016.jpg	told them you were running experiments with LSD? By the mid-1970s, psychedelics had become something of a scientific
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_017.jpg	embarrassment—not because they were a failure, but because they had become identified with the counterculture and with
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_018.jpg	disgraced scientists such as Timothy Leary. But there was nothing embarrassing about psychedelic research at Spring Grove
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_019.jpg	in the late 1960s and early 1970s. Then, and there, it looked like the future. “We thought this was the most incredible
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_020.jpg	frontier in psychiatry,” Richards recalls. “We would all sit around the conference table talking about how we were going to
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_021.jpg	train the hundreds if not thousands of therapists that would be needed to do this work.
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_022.jpg	(And look, we’re having the same conversation again today! There were international conferences on psychedelic research,
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_023.jpg	and we had colleagues throughout Europe doing similar work. The field was taking off. But in the end the societal forces
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_024.jpg	were stronger than we were.” In 1971, Richard Nixon declared Timothy Leary, a washed-up psychology professor, “the
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_025.jpg	most dangerous man in America.” Psychedelics were nourishing the counterculture, and the counterculture was sapping the
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_026.jpg	willingness of America’s young to fight. The Nixon administration sought to blunt the counterculture by attacking its
test/images/synth_doc_00200_line_027.jpg	neurochemical infrastructure.
